THE RED PAGE
Pot au Feu —Marmaduke Pickthall (Syd-
ney Book Club). Some short stories of
Oriental life, which recall the delightful
Said, the Fisherman; others of Switzer-
land and England. All worth reading.
All the World Wondered— Leonard Merrick
(Sydney Book Club). The first and
longest is about the worst of these
happy yarns, mostly about playwrights
and actresses. A good selection of
earlier Merrick stories is printed as
The Gall from the Past in Nelson’s 9d.
library.
Jim Hands —R. W. Child (Macmillan).
A quietly humorous American love
story, told by the girl’s father.
Flamsted Quarries —Mary Waller (G.
Robertson and Co.).' The sort of story
Hall Caine might have written if he
had grown up an American woman in-
stead of a Manx.
* * * *
Amongst the best of the cheap reprints are
H. Belloc’s Life of Danton (Nelson, Is.),
and Ole Luk-Oie’s The Green Curve
(Blackwood). The latter is a collection of
short storied, praised here on their first ap-
pearance.
DE PROFUNDIS.
She who was false to me became a stair
Piercing azure to
The dumb and battleme’nted Dark, where
through
(So near is God to Love’s divine despair),
I snatched tlie boon the desert ravens bare,
Pellucid joy from tear-filled Marah drew,
Heard “Peace, be still!” compose my tem-
pest crew,
And saw, again, the soft-eyed stars at
prayer.
Into mine eyes, in Love’s aphelion chilled,
Darkness, the urn of Light, their old soul
pours:
Silence, whose foam is cosmic song, re-
stores
The jangled ears her perjuries had killed:
And, drenched in utter exile’s briny rain,
Heart, on a whim’s wheel bruised, throbs
love again.
vie.
BERNARD O'DOWD.
Shaw’s New Plays and Prefaces
When Shaw was merely a dramatic critic
he stated his idea of comedy in Spencerian
language. He said that “its function is to
dispel unconsciousness by means of analy-
sis.” His own education has been a process
of disillusionment, and nis principal business
in life has been to dispel popular ignorance
by knocking the stuffing out of some hoary
illusions. It is the jester in the court of
Demos, as in earlier courts, who can indulge
in plain home talk most effectively, and
Shaw took up the role of jester for the sake
of its privileges. The purists say that he is
no dramatist, and the pedants say that he
has nothing but the shreds and patches of
other men’s philosophies. Shaw does not
mind what they say he hasn’t, so long as he
has a:n audience, for he knows that if he talks
long enough he will get the people to mend
their drains so that their souls may be saved.
In his Statement which was rejected by
the Select Committee on the Censorship,
Shaw labelled himself as “a specialist in im-
moral and heretical plays,” and defined the
popular meaning of “immoral” as “whatever
is contrary to established manners and cus-
toms.”
“My reputation has been gained by my
persistent struggle to force the public to re-
consider its morals. In particular I regard
much current morality as to economic and
sexual relations as disastrously wrong, and
I regard certain doctrines of the Christian
religion as understood in England to-day
with abhorrence. I write plays with the de-
liberate object of converting the nation to
my opinions in these matters.”
The contents of his latest volume —The Doc-
tor’s Dilemma, Getting Married, and The
Showing-up of Blanco Posnet (Constable,
6s.) —are double-barrelled attacks upon es-
tablished manners and customs —by play and
by preface. Anyone is in danger of get-
ting married, most people have required a
doctor, and everyone has some views on re-
ligion ; so these plays appeal to all.
Shaw takes some fair specimens of the
medical profession and analyses them in The
Doctor’s Dilemmd. Dr. Ridgeon has dis-
covered that an injection of opsonin will
butter the disease microbes so that they may
be more readily devoured by the white cor-
puscles. He is knighted, and a number of
colleagues call to congratulate him, and the
talk which follows is full of amusing disclo-
sures which justify Ridgeon’s confession
that “we’re not a profession, we’re a conspi-
racy.”
If by any chance the reader misses the
point of the first act, the preface rams it
home: ’
What is called Science has always pur-
sued the Elixir of Life and the Philosopher’s
Stone, and is just hs busy after them to-day
as ever it was in the days of Paracelsus. We
call them by different names —Immunization
or Radiology, or what not, but the dreams
which lure us into the adventures from which
we learn are always at bottom the same.
Science becomes dangerous only when it im-
agines that it has reached its goal.
It is said that the Roman augurs winked at
one another, and there would be nothing
much in Shaw’s doctors if they merely gave
each other away. The fine irony of the play
is in bringing them into contrast with Louis
Dubedat, an unscrupulous artist of genius.
Dubedat’s “shameful misconduct,” as a man
belonged to an unreal life, and in his dying
speech to the doctors he made a profession
of faith as an artist:
“In my own real world I have never done
anything wrong, never denied my faith,
never been untrue to myself. I’ve been
threatened and blackmailed and insulted and
starved. But I've played the game. I've
fought the good fight. ... (He feebly folds
his hands and utters his credo) :
“I believe in Michael Angelo, Velasquez,
and Rembrandt; in the. might of design,
the mystery of color, the redemption of ali
things by beauty everlasting and the mes-
sage of art that has made these hands
blessed. Amen.”
The preface contains a strong case for
bringing doctors under municipal control as
paid servants of the State.
Getting Married is a long conversation
without the customary division into acts or
scenes. The conversation leads to nowhere
in particular; but it is fairly sparkling.
The greengrocer says of his wife:
“She never understood that married people
should take holidays from one another, if
they are to keep at all fresh.”
“She’s never known any man but me, and
she can’t properly know me, because she
don’t know other men to compare me with.
Of course, she knows her parents in—well,
in the way one does know one’s parents.”
Leo, whose decree absolute is nearly due,
would like to retain her husband and the
other man:
“Why can’t I marry them both? I love
them both, I should like to marry a lot of
men. I should like to have Rejjy for every
day and Sinjon for concerts, and some great
austere saint for about once a year at the
end of the season, and some perfectly blith-
ering idiot of a boy to be, quite wicked
with.”
The Bishop: “A man is like a phono-
graph with half a dozen records. You soon
get tired of them all, yet you have to sit at
table while he reels them off to every new
visitor. In the end you have to be content
with his common humanity, and when you
come down to that you find out about men
what a great English poet of my acquaint-
ance used to say about women: that they
all taste alike.” . . .
Leo: “Then it’s a mistake to get married.”
The Bishop : “It is, my dear; but it’s a
much bigger mistake not to get married.”
* * * *
The most dramatic of the three plays is
that in which Blanco Posnet, a disreput-
able gambler, is tried for horse-stealing by
the sheriff and a jury of cowboys. Blanco
talks with the blasphemous freedom of his
kind. “Take care, he said to his
pious brother, “He hasn’t finished with you
yet. He always has a trick up His sleeve”
The brother, an elder who sells grog, be-
lieved that “what kqeps America to-day
the purest of the nations is that when she’s
not working she’s too drunk to hear the
voice of the tempter.”
The ruffianly Blanco gave the horse he
stole tp a woman with a sick child, so that
she might get to the doctor. Then Blanco
saw a message written on the rainbow—
“l’ve got the cincli on you this time, Blanco
Posnet”; and while reading it he was
arrested. The horse thief comes to the con-
clusion that though lie doesn’t understand
the game He plays, there is no doubt that
He didn’t make us for nothing.
“He made me because He had a job for me.
He let me run loose till the job was ready,
and then I had to come along and do it,
hanging or no hanging. And I tell you it
felt bully ; just bully.”
There can ,be no objection to the doctrine,
but the irreverent forms of speech used by
Blanco caused the British Censor to pro-
hibit the acting of the play. The-question
of the censorship is dealt with trenchantly
in Shaw’s prefaces. These are as vivacious
as the dialogues,; for Shaw acts on the
principle of Milton’s chantecleer, who,
“with lively din, scatters the rear of dark-
ness thin.”
Granville Barker and Pinero
The influence of Bernard Shaw is ap-
parent in Granville Barker’s realistic
comedy —The Madras House (E. W. Cole,
Is. 9d.). It is a Discussion about Woman
and the Art of Dress. Most of the talk
has that faithfulness to his types which dis-
tinguished Barker’s Waste and The Voysey
Inheritance.
Technically, the first act is a mistake.
It is devoted to scrappy remarks at the
house of Huxtable, a prosperous draper
with six unmarried daughters, to whom
Philip Madras and a friend have paid a
Sunday morning call. Some of it is plain
“How do you do,” a good deal is mere beat-
ing the wind, and its main purpose, ap-
parently, is to balance the six unmarried
daughters against “that industrial seraglio,”
the Madras House, in which Huxtable,
Philip Madras and his father are partners.
The act closes with the hollow sound of
the gong—“the dinner hour striking its own
empty stomach,” and a chorus of “good-
byes.”
Madras House is to be sold to a senti-
mental American, who has already made a
fortune in drapery. He explains the simple
plan:
The Ladies’ Department served bV gentle-
men ; the gentlemen’s by ladies'. Always, of
course, within the bounds of delicacy. Sur-
rounded by Gracious Womanhood does the
sensual man forget how much money he is
spending or does he not? . . . Then, sir,
I should like you to see our Ladies’ Fancy
Department at its best. Athletes, every
one of ’em. Not a man under six feet,
bronzed, noble fellows. .. . The women
come in flocks.
There is some comedy in the scenes, and the
various threads of interest are interwoven
fairly well. The curtain falls on a lull in
the conversation between Madras
and his wife before a dying fire. They
touch the fringe of weighty problems, and
get to no conclusion. Such conversation in
a plotless drama needs to be as bright and
audacious as Shaw’s, and that is not the
case in The Madras Bouse.
* * * *
Pinero’s Mid-Channel is a variant of the
domestic tragedy in which he has specialised
since The Second Mrs. Tanqueray. The
story is a commonplace one. After 14 years
of married life, without children, the success-
ful stockbroker has become indifferent and
his wife raspy. A friendly adviser likens
their case to that of passengers between
Folkestone and Boulogne. There is a place
mid-channel where the choppy sea tries the
best of tempers; after that’s passed, all’s
well. However, the couple part at that
place, and each finds the usual solace. There
is an attempted reconciliation; each has dis-
missed the temporary comforter; but the
husband bullies a confession out of the wife
and refuses to forgive her lapse. Thrown
on to the other man, she finds that he has
already made other arrangements—so she
ends her life.
Exbept for the heavy jocularity of the
good-natured friend, there is no relief in
these dialogues of petty wrangle and
misunderstanding. The characters are
not lifted to a tragic level, nor is
the wife’s suicide the inevitable outcome of
the situation. Death rounds off a trouble
conveniently; but it is often as cheap a final
curtain as that of the reunited couple Smiling
through their tears, which would have been
the natural end of such a play in the days
before Ibsen.
Pinero’s realism is lop-sided, for it ex-
cludes joy. , A continuous atmosphere of
gloom can only be justified by something tre-
mendous in the way of thunder and lightning.
Brandos’ “Lassalle”
Ferdinand Lassalle is a: figure of unusual
interest in the history of modern Europe.
As a writer, ap orator and a Socialist
leader his position in Germany in the ’6o’s
was almost equal to that of Bismarck. He
had the Jewish type of mind in the highest
degree of efficiency, and a magnetic person-
ality which made; him a great politician and
a great lover. His duel over the remarkable
woman, Helene von Donniges, and his death
at the hands of the weakling, Racowitza,
gave him a romantic halo, which was en-
larged by Meredith’s use of the story in The
Tragic Comedians. A great deal has been
written about Lassalle since then, but there
has been no full-length literary portrait of
the man until George Braudes made one
some years ago. This has now been trans-
lated into English —Ferdinand Lassalle
(Heinemann, 75.), and it makes a fitting
companion picture to the- autobiography of
Helene von Ilacowitza, which was reviewed
here last year.
* * * i *
Amongst the things of interest in Viereck’s
Confessions of a Barbarian is an interview
with George Brandes, the sage of Copen-
hagen. In true American fashion, Viereck
forced himself upon the old man with a verbal
introduction from James Huneker, whom
Brandes had never met :
“Yes,” he replied, without vanity and, let it
be added, without smiling, “I am famous.
But that js a meaningless phrase in view of the
decreasing sale of my books.' In some cases the
sales have dwindled'down to thirty or forty
copies.”
“Some of my books have been more fortunate
than others. The complete edition of mv Danish
writings was subscribed for by 6000 people
between 1899 and 1902. That is a great number
for a uqnntry with a population of only two and a
half millions.”
“ All my books published in the English lan-
guage earn me less than £lO per annum.”
“There are only a few immortals. In all the
revolving years the world has produced scarcely
twelve, and I shall not be among them. And
yet, work alone is the cup that stays and comforts
us.”
M. Faguet’s Ideas of Baudelaire
Emile Faguet is a literary Mr. Venus; as
his testimonial, written by himself, tells us,
he is an expert in the analysis and arrange-
ment of an author’s general ideas and ten-
dencies, from which process there results, if
not a portrait, at least a well-balanced
skeleton that will stand alone. ‘‘ln the
vicious pride of his youth,” he made pro-
phecy of Baudelaire, “il ne survivra pas,
c’est I’affaire d’une generation,” and pending
his speedy extinction, omitted him from his
“Literary History of France,” as was
mentioned on this page when the book
appeared. Now (La Revue, Sept. 10) he
dissects this body that persists in living,
and finds that Baudelaire is short-winded, a
barren rascal who writes only short poems,
and not many of them, a discovery he had
previously made about De Musset, Gautier
and De Vigny; what it has to do with the
value of their poetry only M. Faguet knows.
Further, “ Notez que ce novateur n’a
aucune idee neuve. Ilfaut de Vigny atten-
dre jusqu’a Sully-Prudhomme pour trouver
des idees nouvelles. . . Jamais Baudelaire ne
traite que le lieu commun fripe jusqu’ ala
corde.” The reader, conscious of the sugges-
tion of crowded ideas behind every phrase of
the most original French poet of his century,
wonders: wonders too, however “le poete
aride de la became a symbol of un-
utterable wickedness—a volatile poison, to be
handled tally after the eyes were masked with
glass. M. ? Faguet amplifies. La Benediction:
le poete est ici-bas un martyr. Les Phares :
les artistes sont les lumieres de I'humanite. .
La Charogne : le mot d’Eecle.siastique v unus
est interitus hominum et jumentorum (follow-
ing Brunetiere). Now he sees what the critic
would be at; these tags are what the poems
mean to him. The only comment is to quote
the shortest example, La Beaule, which
according to M. Faguet=la beaute rend les
choses belles.
Je suis belle, O mortels ! cotnme un rSve de
pierre,
Et rfibn seiu, oit chacun s’est meurtri tour k tour,
E*t fait pour inspirer au poete un amour
Eternel et muet ainsi que la matibre,
•Te trdne dans I’azur comme un sphinx incompris,;
J’unis un cceur de neige h la blancheur des cygnes;
Je hais le mouvement qui deplace les lignes ;
Et jamais je ne pleure et jamais je ne rig.
Les poetes, devant mes grandes attitudes,
Que j’ai l’air d’emprunter aux plus fiera monu-
ments,
Consumerontleur jours en d’austbres Etudes:
Car j’ai pour fasciner ces doeiles amants,
De purs miroirs qui font, toutes ehoses plus belles :
Mes yeuX, mes larges yeux aux elartes 4ternelles.
M. Faguet’s “idee” is apparently a . misty
recollection of the penultimate verse. This
is the soi't of criticism that makes Lear a
warning to fathers, and The Ancient Mari-
ner a protest against cruelty to animals.
Applied tentatively to M. Faguet’s poete-
philosophe, it yields these original ideas:
Love is changeless amid change (Oe'qui dure
and "Au hard de I’eau); Nil desperandum,
keep the pot a-bilin’, girls (Les Danaides);
Happiness is found in sacrifice (Le Bonheur,
on the faith of Firman Roz) ; Man depends
oil his fellows (Le Songe).
The mistake lies in the method—the
attempt to isolate the “idea” of a poem.
Poetry does not deal with ideas, except as
fused and transfornied by the imagination of
the poet. And the poem compact of idea,
emotion and form, the concrete expression
of “ the spiritual unity or mood ” (as C. J.
Brennan, the clearest exponent of the
theory, puts it) cannot be resolved.
Add to his method the temperament of
the critic—“il ne lui manque qu’un peu de
sensibilite, un peu de tendresse, un peu de
paresse, un peu de sensualite,” says Jules
Lemaitre—and beneath the scalpel and
cleaver the poetry escapes, refusing, like
Mr, Venus's disdainful charmer, “to be
regarded in that bony light.”
V.V.V.
“Awe? I have never been aware of senti-
ments of awe towards any*“ artist, save
Charles Baudelaire. My secret attitude to
them has always been that I would like to
shake their bauds and tell them briefly in
their private slang, whatever their private
slang was, that they had given immense
pleasure to another artist. I have excepted
Charles Baudelaire ever since I read his
correspondence, in which he is eternally try-
ing to borrow ten francs from some one, and,
if they cannot make it ten —then five.
There is something so excessively poignant,
and to me so humiliating, in the- spectacle
of the grand author of La Charogne going
about among his acquaintance in search of
a dollar, that I would only think about it
when I wished to inflict on myself a pen-
ance. It is a spectacle unique. Like the
King of Thule song in Berlioz’s Damnation
of Faust, it resembles nothing else of its
kind. If the artist does not stand in awe
before that monumental enigma of human
pride which called itself Charles Baudelaire,
how shall the artist’s posture be described?”
—Arnold Bennett (English Review).

gUNYIP'
# Forest Devil.
HOW A LADY WORKED
THE “DEVIL.”
Messrs. J. COOPER & SONS,
Melbourne.
PANTON HILL,
17th October, 1010.
Dear Sirs, —Just a few lines to let you know how we are getting along with the Bunyip
Forest Devil father bought from you three weeks ago. Father says it is the BEST
MACHINE HE HAS EVER USED, and he has seen and used a good many, and is very
pleased with it. I must let you know a little what we have done with it. Our trees are
mostly white gum, box, and messmate. We have uprooted trees that were 4ft. 6in. in
diameter, or so thick that the anchor rope was just big enough to go round them, with only
father and myself on the lever. There was one giant that, when I said to pull it, father
seemed very doubtful, and told me to wait until my brothers, came home from school; but I
said, “ Put the ropes on ” ; and both of us got on the lever, and, in a short time, out came the
monster with roots in the air, and made a hole not less than sft. deep and 12ft. wide. We
had not seen a tree pulled like it. Father would not have the hole filled up, but lets it stop
there so people can see WHAT A POWERFUL MACHINE THE "BUNYIP” IS. I
have PULLED TREES MYSELF, about 2ft. in diameter, without any help. Some of our
trees are dead about five years, and the roots are mostly green, and bring up a great lot of
soil, but it is all the same. THE GREEN ONES COME OUT JUST AS EASY AS THE
DEAD ONES. There is NO STOPPING when once the “ Devil” catches hold of them —
THEY HAVE TO COME WITH THE BUNYIP. Wishing you every success in the
future, yours truly, (Signed) MARIA NOTSCHALL.
John Cooper 8 Sons,
287-9 Elizabeth Street (opp. St.
Francis’s Church), MELBOURNE.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Fair Fortunes.
Lo, the fivers reek their richness! From the Hunter
to the Tweed
Pour the farm-lands forth their harvests; pleasant
pastures greet the eye;
Here the dairy cattle fatten; here is water, grass
and feed,
And a certainty of season as the fertile years
roll by.
But I long for old Monaro, for the place ■ where I
was born
Where the clear creeks sing and sparkle from their
sources in the snow
While I watch my silky milkers, while I count my
bags of corn,
I am pining for the places where the hefty tim-
bers grow.
1 am weary of these summers and these tilth lands
deep and fair,
Of the lucerne’s green abundance that you cut
and cut again;
And these swollen cattle pumpkins fill my spirit
with despair;
I grow weary ’mid my melons and repine among
my cane.
Oh, ’tie south from Kosciusko; or ’tis westward of
Bonang,
By the bridle tracks a-winding that my heart
Would have me go,
Through the gullies of Monaro, that grey moun-
tains overhang
With their feet among the clover and their heads
amid the snow.
Here the flowers bloom in winter. We have fuchsias
in July.
Here the bank clerk grows guavas in his kitchen
garden, full
Of sub-tropical profusion. Here will effort slight
supply
All requirements of existence from a store-house
bountiful.
But my heart is in her ranges. In the land where
1 was born,
Where a clearer air, frost-laden, brings red energy
and health.
Though I’m growing rich and richer ’mid the
melons, cane and corn,
I have asked my soul the question: “Is Content the
price of Wealth ?”
I was bred to stock and station, as a soldier to his
trade —
To the life of spur and saddle, .where a man
would gallop free,
Round the steepest hills, unthinking, into gorges,
undismayed,
That would set these river cockies all a-shiver
just to see.
Here the air hangs thick and heavy. When the
monsoon brings its rain,
You can feel these fat lands growing. There’s
but little work to do,
For the Hindu takes your husking and the big
mills crush your cane,
And the new-chum does the milking for a very
slender “screw.”
I am fat and over fifty ; I’m the Mayor of the Shire,
Hold my river lands in freehold, and my bank
account by now —
Yet how bitter, when you search it, tastes the apple
of desire! —
May be Written down in figures at a modest forty
thou’.
I’m a settler to be envied by the settlers of the
South,
By the strugglers on the ranges; by the battlers
of the West;
By the little cocky farmers who are living hand to
mouth,
And the fellows in the drought-lands and the
toilers and the rest.
With my bungalow resounding to the pianola’s
strains;
With my buggy horses prancing on the gravel by
the gate,
With a fortune, swelling hourly in the profits and
the gains,
It is likely they’d regard me as a favored sen of
Fate.
Yet these mountains of Monaro and the hut where
I was born
Come before me like a picture, till the melons
and the cane
Have evanished into shadow, with the milkers and
the corn,
And with Love and Youth to spur me, I am
riding home again.
VICTORIA.
E. J. BRADY.
A Picture.
A ripple of light
Like the first wave-break of a tidal dawn
Across the heaven.
Far above, a form
Stealing, in robes of green, with eyes of jet
To gaze upon the dawn-grey silent road.
All litten by a little lantern-flame
Blown by the dawn wind, burning.
From the dust
Beneath, a song: “0 dawning sky,
Once more we greet at the witch’s web,
Netted with kiss and sigh!
I have watched the vampire-soul to her lips
Steal, and her Sin I know;
Yet down the road to the Witch I ride
With eyes that see, yet are blind (for pride),
As ever a man must go!
“0 dawn-wind’s breath!
Her eyes look down into mine like lights
In an evil dream of Death;
Her lips are red with the stain of life
From the pierced vein ebbing slow;
Yet down the road to the Witch is borne
My soul that sees, yet is mute (from scorn);
As ever a man must got ”
A stifled cry
Drowned in the orchard-song of waking birds:
A closing casement, and a lamp that dies:
Dawn on a long road empty to the world. . .
N.S.W.
HUGH McKAY.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-
Six Shillings sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communica-
tions to be addressed to William Macleod, Manag-
ing Director.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves ) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
therej the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where prac-

THURSDAY, APRIL 20, 1911.
AN APPEAL TO THE NATION’S
HORSE-SENSE.
Next Wednesday’s polling will do some-
thing else besides settle whether the National
Parliament is to straggle along bereft of
powers which the meanest provincial assem-
blies possess; it will act as a supreme test of
the political intelligence of Australia.
Never has the party which represents the
cause of Vested Interests lied harder or
more audaciously. Prom the very begin-
ning, when a Sydney M.P., speaking with
all the elaborate gloom of one in the presence
of a Great Horror, laboriously enumerated
122 different functions which, he alleged,
would be “stolen” from the States by an
affirmative vote, the campaign has been a
steady appeal to ignorance and prejudice.
No discreditable dodge for snaring the votes
of the uninstructed has been neglected. _ Ihe
“Liberal” dailies have, with matchless impu-
dence, counselled their worker-readers to
vote “No, twice” on the incredible grounds
that if the amendments asked for by the La-
bor Government are carried, there will be a
slump in wages —which is something equiva-
lent to the spider warning the fly of the
frightful cold lie will get if he doesn t throw
the web around his shoulders. _ W hile Vic-
torian “Liberalism” has been insisring that
the defeat of the “Noes” will mean the
domination of Victoria by lass-Canberra,
NSW “Liberalism” has been urging with
equal zest that “Yes, Yes” will mean the fur-
tlier spoliation of the Mother State by Mel-
bourne. The Melbourne Age, a manufac-
turers’ paper, affects to see in the amend-
ments the ruin of the manufacturers; the
Sydney Telegraph, whose best customers
are interested in sugar, gas, coal and ferries,
scoffs at the fear of the Victorian, buc fore-
casts that the proposals, if carried, must ef-
fect the downfall of the altruistic vends in
N. S. Wales. If railway servants are given
a right of appeal to the Federal Arbitration
Court —a policy which was favored^ by both
Deakin and Cook six years ago —“Liberal-
ism” contends that the Australian I arlia-
ment will interfere with freights, fares, time-
tables, and other details of the State llail-
wavs in so asinine a fashion as to drive a
horrified oversea Cohen right out of the
money-lending business, so far as this country
is concerned. The absurd Yellow Pup, ever
enthusiastic in the cause of Anti-Australian-
ism, has laid it down that the powers asked
for by one of the few wholly Protestant Min-
istries that Australia has known are sought
with the object of gratifying Rome. And
the Australian Women’s National League,
of Collins-street, Melbourne, remarks, in a
widely distributed circular, that an affirma-
tive vote “not only paves the way, but
makes inevitable complete Socialism (.col-
lective ownership), involving the absolute
destruction of private ownership, the home
and individual liberty.”
* * « *
The advocates of the Referenda proposals
have been guiltless of insulting the intelli-
gence of the nation with stuff like that. I hey
have based their case on three plain proposi-
tions :
(1) That certain Federal legislation is urgently
TCJ (2) <( 'That without the amendments asked for• the
urgently required legislation can never be achieved.
(3) That the granting of the amendments cannot
cause the various disasters that are prophesied unless
a majority in both the Representatives and the
Senate goes completely mad.
Among the things that are needed at once,
nut cannot be attained with the Constitution
in its present imperfect state, are:
(1) The New Protection.
(2) An extension of the powers of the federal
Arbitration Court. ,
(3) Power to control monopolies when they ex-
ploit the public.
(4) The power to declare a Common Rule, so that
the unfair employer shall not get a trade advantage
over the fair employer.
The electors of the Commonwealth have
three times signified their approval of the
New Protection, an arrangement whereby
not only the manufacturer, but the wage-
earner and consumer as well, shall be pro-
tected. Some of Australia’s bitterest Anti-
Laborites have been impelled to publicly de-
clare in favor of it. The same people agree,
as a matter of abstract principle, that rail-
way employees who consider themselves in-
dustrially wronged should have as much
right to appeal to the Federal Arbitration
Court as any other employees. They would,
they say, give the Government power to put
these needed reforms in the Statute Book,
did they not fear the Trade and Commerce
Clause —the calamitous circumstance which
(allegedly) is going to bring about “the
overturning of the businesses ot Sydney bottle
accumulators” and other petty traders of
the sort. The Government says that these
latter fears are utterly groundless, for these
reasons: —
(a) The Trade and Commerce Clause will not
give Parliament power to interfere with the details
of trades, vide. the Privy Council’s decision in the
famous Canadian case on this subject, and the High
Court’s more recent judgment here; '
(b) That, even if the National Parliament had
power to meddle with the business of hawkers, ped-
lars and so forth, it might be relied on not to do so;
(c) That, lacking the Trade and Commerce Clause
amendment, neither the New Protection nor the
Common Rule can become operative.
The type of Victorian whose views are
voiced by the Melbourne Age is hostile to the
New Protection, for obvious reasons. He
is largely a manufacturer, and wants all the
financial benefit of the existing tariff, with-
out sharing any of it with his employees.
On the other hand, he approves of the fea-
ture of the Referenda proposals which pro-
vide for Federal control of monopolies. And
few of him owns the requisite nerve to pub-
licly challenge the justice of _ the Common
Rule principle. So the position is that,
for purely selfish reasons, numbers of ‘Lib-
erals” are opposing legislation which various
sections of them admit to be beneficial. But,
in trying to get others to follow them, they
do not tell the truth about their selfishness.
Rather, they rely on one thing, and one
thing only—that various dreadful things
may happen if tne National Parliament is
given the desired power, and then goes mad
and authorises the dreadful things to be
done. What the “Liberals” don’t point out
is that the State Parliaments. now possess
all the powers which the National Parlia-
ment asks for; and, as it is just as likely
that the State Parliaments will do the mad
things as that the National Parliament will
do them, there is no more danger in trans-
ferring the powers than in leaving them
where they are.
* * * *
These things are plain enough to profes-
sional students of politics. But, unfortu-
nately, the average citizen studies politics
in only a cursory fashion. And therein lies
the brightest hope of Vested Interests. The
•Liberal” party press and party leaders who,
for the past two months, have. been campaign-
ing on the assumption that it is possible to
humbug hordes of Democrats into voting the
Tory ticket, have a bad, not to say rotten,
record in this line. They are the wlio
attempted to foist Reid’s ridiculous Tiger
on a derisive people ; who boomed the Dread-
nought foolishness as a pretext for side-
stepping local defence: who engineered the
jy use and who went down with a thud in
each instance. The trouble with these
swindles was that they were not subtle
enough. Something more complicated was
required to hoodwink a people which has en-
joyed free and compulsory education for a
quarter of a century. To a certain extent
the Referenda issue supplied the needed
goods. In the nature of things, it was
capable of being clouded in a mesh of legal
jargon ; and “Liberalism” has clouded it in
that manner for all the party writers and
orators were worth. The layman is prone
to imagine that the Law is loaded, and his
weakness has been played on assiduously.
Every Anti-Labor lawyer has insisted loudly
that the amendments asked for by the Gov-
ernment, even where they are admittedly
sound in principle, are charged to the muzzle
with appalling dangers, understandable only
by those who have formally qualified to pur-
sue Costs. The legal authorities, who are
also Nationalists, have repeatedly given this
contention the lie; but since they are few,
and Australia is vast, they have been as
voices arguing faintly in a wilderness of
Gentlemen One. The Government has no
daily paper to voice its sentiments in the
places where assistance of the sort is most
needed ; and many thousands of electors have
not heard the case for the Referenda put
from any platform. In the last resort they
will have to arrive at a decision by bringing
their liorse-sense to bear on the arguments.
And really the task is not difficult; for, after
all, the whole matter lies in a small com-
pass. Some months ago a Labor Govern-
ment was returned by a vast majority to
do certain things. It has tried to do those
things, and it finds the way blocked. There-
fore it lias a claim now on every man who
voted for it last year. It was told to do
certain things; it says it hasn’t the neces-
sary material, and it asks for a supply. If
the Labor party is to have a chance to do
what it was told to do, its supporters must
vote “Yes, Yes.” And while there is this
special claim on the Labor party, there is a
large, general claim upon every Australian.
Shall the Australian sentiment prevail, or
shall provincialism rule? The cloud of ar-
gument should not be allowed for one mo-
ment to hide that issue. By keeping that
steadily in view, and voting “Yes, Yes,” the
people of Australia will not only give their
National Parliament powers which it should
have had 10 years ago, but they will also
hand themselves a valuable testimony for
perception and independent judgment.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Deakin’s Advice to S’Africa.
Three years ago S’Africa, which was then
busy in the workshop, forging a Constitu-
tion for itself, asked Australia, as a me-
chanic of experience, for some advice touch-
ing the construction of its new machine.
Deakin —as the Commonwealth’s foreman
or boss engineer—affably agreed to supply
the goods, and accordingly had prepared an
official memorandum, dated August 11.
1908; and presently, as Prime Minister and
Minister for External Affairs, he sent it oil
to S’Africa, loaded with the following pas-
sage :
So far, the powers of the Commonwealth Parlia-
ment have, on the whole, proved to be adequate.
Attention mav, however, be directed to a few points
in which Australian (and so far as it is applicable,
American) experience has shown difficulties and weak-
ness.
(a) The specific powers should be defined in
words as general as possible, avoiding, as far as
possible, all conditions, exceptions and limitations,
e.g., “Trade and Commerce with other countries, and
among the States.” The limitation of Inter-State
trade and external commerce bisects the subject of
trade and commerce, and makes a hard _ and fast
division of jurisdiction, of which it is difficult to
determine the boundaries, and which does not cor-
respond with any natural distinction in the con-
duct of business. It would be more satisfactory,
IF POSSIBLE, TO TAKE TOWEtt OVER “TRADE AND
Commerce” generally.
Groom — Deaicin’s Attorney-General —en-
dorsed the memorandum enthusiastically : —-
I have carefully perused the memorandum pre-
pared by Mr. Garran during my absence in Queens-
land, AID, I FULLY AGREE WITH THE VIEWS EX-
PRESSED.
Now, the question which Deakin and
Groom ought to answer is whether they
were misleading S’Africa then in telling it
to take power, if it could, over “Trade and
Commerce generally,” or whether they are
misleading Australia now in advising it not
to take power, when it can, over the same
subjects.

The Rotten Buttresses of a Weak Case.
Melbourne Age recently cited some alleged
results of a “Yes” vote to the Referenda
questions, as follows :
Farmers would not only be bound down by strict
conditions with regard to wages paid to farm hands,
but the Parliament would decide the maximum
number of cows a man should be called upon to
milk, the quantity of potatoes he should dig, or the
fruit he should gather in a day, and whether it was
the man’s or the employer’s duty to wash the milk-
ing utensils or water the stock..
This is as grotesque as it would be if it
were contended that the N.S.W. Government
must not permit llackenschmidt to ride on
its tram-cars, on the grounds that be
“ would, ” break the conductor’s ne«k if a
fare were demanded from him. And it IS , as
insane as it would be if a British Prime
Minister were urged to keep George V. un-
der lock and key, because, under the Prerog-
ative, he holds certain powers which lie
would be accordingly certain to exercise.
“It would very much surprise people,” says
Walter Bageiiot, in a sentence that has
become classical, “if they were only told how
many things the Queen (Victoria) could
do without consulting Parliament.”
Not to mention other things, she could disband the
armv • . she could dismiss all the officers, from
the General Commanding-in-Chief downwards; she
could dismiss all the sailors too; she could sell oft
our ships of war and all our naval stores , she could
make a Deace by the sacrifice of Cornwall, and begin
a war for the conquest of Brittany ; she could make
every citizen in the United Kingdom, male or female,
a Peer* . . • she could pardon all offenders. In a
word, the Queen could upset all the action of civil
government. .
Queen Victoria did none of these things, tor
the simple reason that she was possessed ot
Common Sense. The position has not al-
tered in any material degree since Bagehot s
day ■ and we may therefore wait' till King
George V. exercises the undoubted power
of his Prerogative by handing over Tasmania
and Maoriland to Japan, and making a
present of Scotland to the Kaiser, before
(in the words of Melbourne Age) we begin
to “think of the Federal Parliament looking
into every hamlet, every factory, shop,
shearing-shed, stock-yard, mine, farm, sta-
tion, orchard, piggery—every conceivable
place in which a man or woman may be em-
ployed—and solemnly dictating the hours,
the pay, the duration of smoke on, and
whether a man’s time begins when he
wakes up or when he arrives on the 30b.
All Liberalism’s objections to the Referenda
uroposals stand broad-based on this same
imbecile assumption that, • if the National
Parliament is not securely chained down like
a wild animal, it not only may, but must, un-
necessarily harass the Rectors, ruin their
businesses, and generally behave like a
lunatic. The National party claims that the
Australian Parliament —elected as it is on
an adult suffrage basis —is to be trusted to
use such powers as it is accorded in a rea-
sonable fashion ;—as reasonably, for example,
as any sovereign of German extraction who
ever reigned in Britain. Allow that, and
the barrackers for Iteaction haven’t a leg to
stand on.

State Rights and a Few Combines.
When Bigjohn Forrest, in the debate on the
Referenda proposals in the Federal House of
Reps., said he thought the States could deal
with trusts, and Irvine, K.C., assured him
that they couldn’t, the “No-No” papers kept
as still as mice, and never elaborated the
point. Meanwhile Bruce Smith, Cook
and Co. dodged the difficulty by strenuously
denying that trusts or combines exist in
Australia. The facts, of course, are against
them. There are already three glaring in-
stances of the genuine and undoubted trust,
viz., (1) the tobacco combine, (2) the ship-
ping combine, and (3) the coal vend.
These have abolished political economy, and
have locked up the law of supply and de-
mand in their city offices. In addition to
these, there are many lines in which the
big handlers fix prices by an arrangement or
horrible understanding. Of these meat,
jam, bricks, timber, flour, bread and furni-
ture are good instances. Sugar is a mon-
opoly outright. The farmer who is being
scared into voting “No” by horrible visions
of Socialism, is affected by two combines—
(l) agricultural machinery, and (2) fertil-
iser, both of which have triumphed over the
law of supply and demand, and are able to
decide what he must pay. The poor are
affected by the world-wide Rockefeller
monopoly of kerosene oil, by the still more
recent 7A per cent, clapped on furniture, by
a marvellous unanimity among the manu-
facturers of jam (quite accidental, of
course), and by certain transactions in re-
gard to exportation of butter which have
sent up the price of that. species of fat.
The man who denies the existence of trusts
in Australia must be either a wilful liar or
an ostrich; and if what Irvine, K.C., told
Bigjohn Forrest in the Federal House of
Reps, is true, and the States cannot deal
effectively with these trusts, then the choice
at the Referenda poll is plainly whether the
people of Australia, through the Australian
Parliament, shall control trusts, or whether
the trusts shall control the people.
A Scheme to Avoid Unnecessary Costs.
The following is a Referendum section
which causes Melbourne Argus and b. M.
Herald to see the same bright shade of
red :
When each House of the Parliament in the same
session has by resolution declared that an industry
... is the subject of a monopoly, the Parliament
shall have power to make laws for the carrying on
the business . . . and acquiring for that purpose,
on just terms, any property used in connection- with
the industry or business.
The villainy of this suggestion, according to
the Bloated press, is that Parliament should
seek to arrogate to itself the right say
what is or what is not a monopoly. Are
our Judges not to be trusted'V” howls the
Argus ; and “A fresh insult to the High
Court!” cries the Herald. Of course the
proposal amounts to nothing of the sort. It
the High Court Judges could, in their ca-
pacity as common-sense men of the world,
identify or repudiate an alleged monopoly,
and do it unhampered by any legal side-
issues, the country would be perfectly willing
to accept their dicta. However, they can t;
and till the British law alters its entire char-
acter, they never will. An instance of the
hopelessness of leaving this sort of problem
to be resolved by the Bench was afforded in
the mighty struggle that raged round the
definition of “a place/’ Tears ago an Act
was passed which forbade the using of a
place” for betting. In due course the Law
began prosecuting bettors under this Act.
And then the Judges started to tell an an-
xious army of gamblers just what ‘‘a place
was. They decided that a movable box
within a ring on a racecourse was ‘‘a place ,
on the other hand, the habitual use for bet-
ting purposes of a spot in a public park was
not “a place.” A stool covered by an um-
brella was “a place”; take away the um-
brella, and it might not be a place. An en-
closed field, hired by a committee for the pur-
pose of holding dog-races, was not “a place ;
but a cricket-ground was. If a bookmaker
walked about a ring making bets the ring
was not “a place” i if he stood still it was.
And so on ad infinitum. The Costs in con-
nection with these various decisions
amounted to tens of thousands of pounds,
and, to-day, the bettor is no more sure when
he is or is not wagering in ‘‘a place,” than
he was 15 years ago, and, in despair, the
British Wowser has abandoned the elusive
job of pinning him down. It is with the ob-
ject of avoiding such trouble that the Aus-
tralian Government has asked the electors to
A Soliloquy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Australia Reasons with llersclf after Hear-
ing the Confusion Orators.
Fat sitteth on my brisket heavily;
Tlie anointed waistcoat of liis holy girth
Is stretched almost to bursting. Woe is me.
I dare not prick his vast rotundity
With the sharp dagger of a little “Yes,
For that would surely ease me of his weight,
And being eased, who knows what I might
dd? .
Might I not even sit upon myself
More heavily than him that sitteth on mo'
How shall I choose? Shall I deny myself
The right to move a finger or a toe
Lest that my toe do injury to me,
Or that my finger work me violence !
Lo! I am sore affrighted of my toe,
And of my moving finger terrified!
Were it not better rather to remain
A cushion for the hallowed rest of Fat .•
To be or not to be? That is the question.
lam not, if Fat is. But there’s the rub;
Shall I be I if with my little “Yes”
1 give myself what now belongs to me,
But which without that gift I cannot use?
Now am I I, but shall I still be I,
Altering the constitution of myself,
And taking certain of my rights from me?
I am as puzzled as a piece of string
Tied in twelve knots — but I am voting
“YES!”
N.S.W.

The Union of the Churches and Another
Matter.
Eves since Lutiieb erupted in Germany
there has been a steady multiplication o
more or less virulent sects. Lach in turn
claimed that it had the proper religious
goods to sell; and for several centuries a
most holy racket was made by the hucksters
who shouted their wares one against another.
Every now and again one of the beatified
would collar a stray heretic and dip him in
boiling oil for the good of his soul and the
glory of religion. Sometimes a sacred not
ensued, and throats were freely cut through
out the length and breadth of. Europe oyer
the meaning of a word or the interpretation
of a phrase. Hates, spites, malice and all
bitterness had the time of their lives ; and
the energy of bitterness only slackened when
modern Democracy took a hand in politics,
and the public demand for something to eat
drowned the roar of the warring saints who
fought like devils for the hope of Heaven and
the love of peace on earth. Gradually as
the main religious uproar subsided, some
sanely religious people began . to look into
the causes of the turmoil, and discovered that
the difference between the heretic who had
been boiled in oil, and the saint who had
done the boiling, was rather less than that
between Tweedledum and Tweedledee. A
faint mention was then made of the fact
that church union might be a pleasant and
comely thing, and that, as saints were be-
coming scarcer and scarcer, and showers of
blessing few and far between, it might be
good to join forces, and see if such
a junction would have any beneficial effect
on the flow of threepenny bits, and the “ad-
vancement of the cause.” Already some
progress has been made, and there are now
practically none who deny that, so far at
least as the Protestant churches are con-
cerned, more and more union would be a
good thing, and should be striven for. But
a number of religious bodies who hold one
great ethical idea in common still find a stu-
pendous difficulty in uniting to give practical
shape to that idea. Each section or coterie,
convinced of the absolute rightness of its
own position—or, at any rate, obstinately
prejudiced against admitting itself to be in
the wrong—has been unwilling to yield a
point to . its rival; and instead of a
vast organisation which might at least have
commanded the respect and reverence of the
world, there is a jumble of raging factions,
shouting the watchwords of their own paltry
separatism, amid the tears of the better
brand of saints, and the derision of the mul-
titude of sinners. Some years ago another
great ethical idea came to a people, addicted
to horses, cricket and home-made poetry,
whose scattered homes lay between the In-
dian and the Pacific oceans. The name of
the idea was Australia. Eagerly they sought
to give reality to their conception, to make
it a practical, living force that should lift
them to a higher plane. The movement
promised well; some progress has indeed
been made with it. But now when it is
proposed to carry it on, it is discovered
that the community, in this matter too, has
split into sects and factions. Wrangling
up and down the land like raging Wowsers,
they tear their opponents with fearful words,
boil their competitors in evil-smelling oil
of invective, and, for love of their country,
curse their country’s children. Here an
Alfred Deakin rants, or a Bruce Smith
preaches fire and brimstone; there a Wade
or a Denham hurls abuse at an opponent.
Sometimes a Murray turns in his slumber,
muttering the verses of a damnatory psalm
as a charm against the horrible energy of
a Hughes ; at times an ill-balanced Hol-
man is converted, or a Beeby, driven crazy
by the din, turns apostate. It is the riot
of the reformed churches over again, only a
trifle more fantastic and absurd. And once
more the better brand of saints—the men
who are staunch to the great sentiment of a
United Australia —look on tearfully, while
the outsider views the ghastly struggle of the
little sectaries with derision. But it is no
use merely viewing the sectaries’ struggle
with derision. Derision will not count next
Wednesday, anywhere except at the ballot-
box. Then, by their votes and by their
votes alone, Australians are to say whether
the absurd racket shall continue on to the
crack of doom, or be squelched once and for
all, even as Mary concludes the cockroach
with the dainty sole of her slipper.

Capitalist and Worker in Australia.
“X” digs :
A few hard figures are here subjoined to worry
over. They are an attempt to show the relative
shares received by Capital and Labor from industry
in Australia, and are based on the Commonwealth
Statistician’s figures. They are inspired by a message
in the Argus (10/4/T1) :
“A recital of some of the results stated by Mr.
W. H. Mallock, the noted publicist, in his latest
work, will be interesting and instructive. They
have been arrived at by the most minute, painstak-
ing, and impartial investigation of all the facts as to
wages and incomes in the United Kingdom. He
entirelv dissipates the wild estimate made by social-
istic writers that for every £1,000,000 received
by employees the capitalistic employer receives more
than £3,000,000. The facts are that wages and
salaries in the United Kingdom amount to
£930,000,000 per annum, and business profits and
dividends to £200,000,000, so that the wages and
salaries of the employed class are four and a-half
times as great as the profits and dividends of the
employing class. Now, it is notorious that wages
and salaries in Australia are much 'FSd'er
on the average than those paid in the United
Kingdom , so oil the probabilities are that
the total bears an even larger proportion
to profits and dividends than prevail in the
Mother Country .. .The wage-earners get by far the
larger share of the results of production nine shares
out of every eleven, Max,lock calculates and by the
increase of production their wages may always be
increased.”
Anyone would conclude from this that figures
were not available in Australia. That is not so.
Elaborate statistics exist for those who care to look
for them. Whatever “all the probabilities” may
be, here are the facts regarding the condition
of manufacturing enterprise in Australia. The
figures are taken from G. H. Knibbs’s ‘ Production,
Bulletin No. 4 ; Summary of Commonwealth Pro-
duction Statistics for the years 1900 to 1909.
These figures reveal: —
(o) That the value added to goods in
process of manufacture in 1909 was £42,216,493
(6) That the value of raw material was £65,193,240

The average wage paid to employees (including

was therefore £21,105,456, divided by 254,750; or
£B2 16s. Hid. per annum; otherwise, £1 11s. 10jd.
per week.
Let us see how the Capitalist gets on.
His investment is:—

Let us grant him a full profit. First, 5 p.c. on
what he paid for raw material. Then 5 p.c. on
the value of land and buildings. Then 5 p.c. on
the value of plant and machinery. Then 2i p.c.
depreciation on land and buildings (though the land
is generally appreciating in value faster than the
buildings depreciate). Then 5 p.c. depreciation on

which difference of £10,860,328 represents the capi-
talists’ excess of profit over and above 5 p.c., with
depreciation allowances. Out of it he has only to
pay the cost of advertising and canvassing for cus-
tomers, and provide for bad debts and contingencies.
If 10s. per week were to be added to the pay of
every one of the 254,750 employees, it would cost
£127,375 per week, or £6,623,500 a year. This
would still leave the capitalist with £4,236,828
more than his 5 p.c. (with generous allowances for
depreciation) ; and the appearances are that, with a
return of 5 p.c. and another 4j millions of pounds
to play with, the distressed capitalist would not do
so very badly in Australia.

Mutiny, and the Need of Stamping It Out.
Whatever the result of the Referenda, there
is a large and very unpleasant job in front
of the Labor party in Australia. That is
to deal firmly with the mutineers who, di-
rectly or indirectly, have been doing their
best to scuttle the ship which was built to
carry a valuable load of Australian aspira-
tion to a safe anchorage. There is no es-
cape from the job, however unpleasant it
may be. It is imperative, if the party aims
are' to be accomplished, or, indeed, if the
party is to survive. Out of the woe and op-
pression of all the lost years—away back
before the lash resounded along the building
of the ramparts of Nineveh; before the trad-
ing boats of Solomon took shape on the
stocks, and the workshops of Sidon moved
with toil and sweat and blood; back to the
first day that the first man hired his labor,
the world has been creeping towards Better
Things. Within the past 10 years the pro-
gress has been remarkable —and remarkable
not only by comparison. The organisation of
Labor, made the easier and surer by the. rapid
growth of the Australian national spirit, has
cleared the way for the removal of the
greater part of the lumber of evil systems
and established injustices; and the contract
for carrying on the job has been given to
the party that made it possible. The party
soon realised that, in order to keep going,
it must either abolish the House wherein
Property and Privilege were specially repre-
sented, or give the work to a Parliament
which did not include any such House,
Again and again progressive measures had
been passed in the State Chamber elected by
the people, only to be emasculated or thrown
out altogether by the Chamber representing
Propertv and Vested Interests. For nearly
20 years the Labor party has struggled to
make these Upper Houses democratic, and
has made practically no progress. For this
reason, among others, it decided at length to
extend the functions of that Parliament
which has no House of Property and Privi-
lege. So the Referenda proposals were de-
vised by the Labor Government, and endorsed
by the' Labor Conference —a Conference to
which every man in the party (and it has
to be insisted upon that there is only one La-
bor party) is pledged. What, then, is the
position of those renegades who, by open
avowal, damning silence, or secret intrigue,
have done and are doing their utmost to de-
feat the plans of the party? If the demands
revolted them, they were at perfect liberty to
step out of the line ; but to march to the front
denouncing the standard as they go, and
stirring a mutiny in the ranks, must be met
with one of two results. Either the army
must turn upon itself, or the false bearers
must be put in their proper place, which is
outside the party. There they may still
do the party some damage; but, in their
proper place, they will be recognised as op-
ponents, and fought, whereas now, being dis-
guised as leaders, they do vasily . more
damage, because they are liable to be fol-
lowed.
Optimism for the Prevention of Harbor
Collisions.
“Billy B.”: It is amazing the amount of
optimism you can squeeze out of the Sydney
Ferry cos. Squeeze is not the word —op-
timism bubbles from them. The eternal
chance of accident, the law of average, the
disastrous haphazard habits of Fate _ or
Providence, or the Devil, the risk of vigi-
lance slackened for a moment, the snap of
a steering chain, the break of an engine rod
at the very moment it is required to be in
the pink of condition —none of these things
are allowed to cloud a ferry co.’s cheerful
outlook. Accidents may happen in some
parts of the world, and in several businesses
—but not, not in Sydney Cove to a ferry
steamer. The ferryman asks you pityingly:
“Has anything serious happened to a pas-
senger ferry yet? No? Very well; then
nothing will happen.” The other day, when it
was announced that the Government proposed
to commence a State ferry service between
Sydney City and the North Shore, Acting
Chief-Secretary Floweks gave as one rea-
son for the innovation that if a disaster oc-
curred, owing to the present dangerous con-
gestion in Sydney Cove, the Government, hav-
ing been warned of the risk of accident by
its officers, would be morally responsible.
The attention of one of the ferry managers
having been drawn to the statement, he
passed along the usual remarks: —
For the last 10 years some one or other has been
continually crying out about “imminent disaster.”
Have there been any terrible disasters ? Why, no!
For an hour I have been endeavoring to call to mind
a case where a ferry-boat was responsible for the
death of a person in Sydney Cove, but for the life of
me I cannot.
The same old remarks as were made to the
Daily Telegkapii some years ago, when
even that apostle of Private Enterprise had
a scare over the possibilities roaming about
the Cove, crowded as it was and is with ferries
and launches and lighters and colliers and
big steamers, day and night, fine weather
or gale, clear night or dense fog; the same
old “Don’t worry” logic as the manager for
the Sydney Ferries dumped before the Royal
Commission on Communication between Syd-
ney and North Sydney a couple of years
ago; the same old “Don’t be cowards” gibe
as was flung at the passengers of the Iro-
quois by the captain, who thereby induced
them to come aboard and be drowned. “We
have heard,” said one of the Commission,
“of narrow escapes from accidents, owing to
the crowded state of Sydney Cove?” An-
swered the Optimist:—
Yes, so have I. I have heard of narrow escapes at
times when I have been on board. Some timid pas-
sengers were afraid their lives were in imminent
danger when the vesssls were 40ft. apart. There can
be no risk when vessels are running parallel. The
vessels are always under control.
Then the manager told the Commission that
the companies could handle more than five
times the passenger traffic at that time
(1908) moving in and out, and, if necessary,
three steamers could be run between Circular
Quay and Milson’s Point. Yet, between
February 6 and May 0 of that very year,
there had been eight minor accidents in or
about the Cove. But probably the most
remarkable instance of the amazing optim-
ism of the cos., and their managers, was a
forecast of what would happen in the event
of a collision. The manager explained that,
although his directors were not bound to
supply more than two bulkheads on each
boat, they had put in five, in order to
minimise the risk of any vessel foundering
in case of a collision. Therefore, said he :
If a vessel were cut through at the hull she would
float; and seeing that there are always two Or three
vessels within two minutes of each other, you would
have another boat alongside within two or three
minutes. . . . Another steamer could come right
alongside, so that passengers could step across with-
out difficulty
r lhere you have the whole solution —no trou-
ble, no delay; all adequate provision made
to arrest disaster at the first shriek. It is
a beautiful scheme, and the Optimist forgot
to mention that the small deck hand would
be stationed on the gunwale of the sinking
ferry, to hand the ladies aboard the rescuer,
while the captain sang comic songs to amuse
the children. Still spea,king for myself only, I
hope that, pending the building of the bridge
or subway, directions have been given that
the entertainment shall not take place in
midstream on a dirty winter night; and,
anyway, I want a good place clear of the
crash and sheer of the assaulting vessel, and
I’m hoping the co. will arrange for the 800
rr 1000 passengers to keep their heads. Then
I may have a chance to get ashore. Other-
wise, I’ve got my doubts.
Let us “ "Wowse! ”
“0.K.”: Sydney discussed the word
“Wowser” some time ago, and now they are
wrangling about it in Melbourne. The unco
godly, who can distinguish between harmless
and deadly ridicule, seem to be smelling dan-
ger in the new word. It is illuminative to the
youthful mind. It excites contempt rather
than dislike. The hard-faced man, with the
monkey shave and a Nonconformist con-
science, who didn’t object to be called a
killjoy, is evidently indisposed to have
“Wowser” stencilled on himself. I have
heard of a young lady who, whilst pleading
guilty to the fact that her father was a Wes-
leyan clergyman, protested that he was “not
a ‘Wowser.’ ” There is only one recorded
instance thus far of a man standing on a
platform to proclaim himself a “Wowser,”
and pat himself on the back for saying it. He
was the painful exception that proves the
rule, and probably his name was Judkins. At
a recent beer-trade gathering in Melbourne,
one of the speakers defined a “Wowser” as
“a hypocrite —one who is in the religious
business for place and pay ; one who seeks
notoriety.” This will never be adopted by
lexicographers, seeing that religion is the
business of all parsons, and place and
pay are legitimate considerations. No;
a “Wowser,” male or female, is one who
affects to regard common human indiscre-
tions with superhuman horror, especially
where “sexuality” is concerned. The “Wow-
ser” professes shocked astonishment at things
which everybody else does, and which he
himself would do if he hadn’t cut himself off
from temptation. A man may be an ardent
Rechabite and non-smoker, a moderate Sab-
batarian, and even an opponent of some
sorts of popular entertainment, and not
“Wowse.” But when he snorts like a hur-
ricane at the mere notion of a girl wanting
to meet a boy after dark, and speaks of “im-
morality” and “impurity” with exaggerated
indignation, he’s a “Wowser.” “Wow” is a
dictionary word. It’s an interjection, a
cry of astonishment, an exclamation of won-
der. And a “Wowser” is a professor of
false wonderment. He shrieks at “vice” as
though it were unnatural, whereas nothing
on earth is really unnatural, save “Wowser-
ism.” The grimmest Puritanism can be
accounted for. The stupidest intolerance of
comparatively youthful pleasures may be
fairly due to the blind egotism of old age.
But no excuse is possible for the person who
looks through the keyhole of a locked door,
as it were, and then tells the members of
his congregation that they would faint with
horror if he told them what he saw. He’s
a false purist, an apostle of prudery, a
common wowser.

The High Standard of State Administration.
Acting-Judge Edmunds, who lately probed
the peculiar methods of the N. S. Wales
Fire Brigades Board, thoroughly justified
the inquiry by exposing a series of what
the daily press calls '‘irregularities.” The
reoort shows that salvage money was at
different times spent on cigars and drinks
for the members, theatre and Dinner par-
ties, and motor-cars which were of little
public use, but frequently came in handy for
private pleasure trips. Also —
The members of the Board were not content with
the reasonable allowance provided, and in the fol-
lowing instances so manipulated the matter of the
allowance as to make gains which the Board’s own
resolution regarding travelling allowances did not
justify:—Mr. W. Taylor, M.L.A., a member of the
Board, on March 16, 1910, received £1 Os. 9d. as
travelling expenses for his train fare to Newcastle
and Maitland; and on June 2, 1910, £7 for return
railway fare and sleeping berths, Sydney to Mel-
bourne. In fact, Mr. Taylor had paid nothing in
this regard, for he was franked on his pass as a
member of Parliament.
Mr. W. Cracknell, a member of the Board, re-
ceived on March 16, 1910, £l, and on June 30,
1910, £3 os. for travelling expenses. Mr. Crack-
nell was not “away over-night” on either of these
occasions, and consequently was not entitled to pay-
ment for travelling expenses under the resolution
previously quoted.
It should also be mentioned that the members of
the Board charged a day’s travelling expenses for the
night of leaving Sydney as well as the morning of
return —usually 6 to 7 a.m.
Taylor subsequently refunded the money,
and the Commissioner advised Cracknell
to do the same. The secretary received
i5O 10s. for the purchase of a season rail-
way ticket, but U ung on to the money and
paid as he went, taking the chance of loss
or gain on the deal. lie also refunded.
Further, certain members “shouted” them-
selves walking boots at the Board’s ex-
pense, and one received from the funds an
ordinary suit of clothes, in lieu of a uniform
that ne claimed as captain of a volunteer
fire brigade. Either Taylor, Liberal
M.L.a. for St. George (Cabbutiiers’s old
seat), or his committee, was allowed to
hire two motor-cars from the Board on the
last State election day, for electioneering
purposes, and, while the Commissioner
agrees that the price paid was reasonable,
he strongly condemns the arrangement. In
view of all the disclosures, the Commis-
sioner seems to have taken a very merciful
view when he suggested that “the public
exposure” and “the resulting humiliation ’
ought to meet the case, “provided that the
safeguard of an effective annual audit of the
Board’s accounts is established.” An an-
nual check may meet the case; but it is
quite clear that a low moral tone pervaded
the whole establishment, and that sort of
thing is likely to have results which can’t
be checked by a person who is merely to
deal with accounts. A larger question is
also raised by the discovery of these abuses.
The State subsidises dozens of institutions
besides the Fire Brigades Board; and it
wmuld seem to be a fair thing to provide
that, in the future, there shall be no such
subsidy for any purpose whatever unless
the right is reserved to the Audit Office to
overhaul the accounts and ascertain whether
the people’s money is spent as it is supposed
to be.

Alfred Deakin made a mess of things at
Bendigo a few days ago, by touting for the
Irish Home Rule vote for the “No’ side in
the Referenda. He said :
The Irishman out of Ireland was going to take
away from these States of Australia the very power
that the Irishman in Ireland was striving for, and
had been striving for for over half a century.
The statement was received with derisive
yells. Probably the audience remembered
one thing. If it fractures the Home Rule
principle to add two or three small powers
to those already possessed by the Common-
wealth, what sort of fracture was it
when the original two or three thou-
sand powers were taken from the States
and given to the Commonwealth? Yet
Deakin was one of the three men who ac-
tually drafted the Constitution, which took
away from the States those two or three
thousand powers. So Deakin is either
a most desperate enemy of the Home Rule
which he pretends to be so eager to pre-
serve, or his present remarks stamp him as
an insincere vote-catcher, who is prepared
to use almost any bait to hook his fish.

Randolph Bedford on the gentle manners
of the monopolist, and the wisdom of hit-
ting him with a “Yes” :
The menace of these tremendous fortunes, so col-
ossal that their holders are at their wits end to find
new openings for the capital that is constantly in-
creasing! Standard Oil has eaten up all the drug-
stores in New York, and has increased the price of
pills and castor-oil; it bonded Childs s restaurants,
and now it has got the magazines. A political act pio-
bably helped the last move of the monopoly. Tapi
and the Postmaster-General are framing a Bill to
place a heavy postal tax on the advertising pages of
all magazines. In some of the concerns it is com-
puted to make a difference of £lO,OOO a year for
each The magazines have been fighting to the finish ,
and now comes the Trust. Pierpont Morgan and his
financial interests have bought up most of the muck-
raking magazines, also Harper’s publications and
Butt eric k’s, which include Everybody s and the
Adventure. The Crowell Co. has bought the Ameri-
can Magazine, which was started by Ida TarbelLj
Lincoln Steffens and others, who are reformers
or “muck-rakers,” just as you happen to be one of
the public or one of the Standard-oilers. Ida lar-
bell wrote the Standard Oil exposures. This not
only means that the reform magazines are silenced,
but it means spineless and witless fiction, and friends
of mine in New York say they don’t know where
artists and writers are going to land. The lrust
will have to have stories and pictures, but the com-
bine means agreement as to prices, absence of com-
petition and other rotten conditions. And Australia
will continue to take the dumped “remainders of
American magazines as of old, with the difference
that, whereas one has hitherto read of Rockefeller
as a cross of Judas Iscariot on Sapphira, he will
now read of him as a stained-glass cathedral-window
angel, with a halo like a cart-wheel. Again, why
have we not a decent copyright law and a duty on
American and English magazines of the non-educative
sort, and a prohibitive tax on Scraps and Dirty
Bits? Imagine a magazine-owner in Australia try-
ing to run his business at a profit while paying de-
cent prices to writers and artists, and with the op-
position of Rockefeller’s millions behind the Ameri-
can Magazine Trust.

of Fat, Melbourne Argus, lately moaned at
great length “the Federal Ministry
has given all the shipbuilding work to Syd-
ney, and explained that the Private-Enter-
prise yards on the Yarra bank could do the

and says this was all bunkum, and that
Protectionist Melbourne can do things just
as well or better. It is all very confusing.

The German Chancellor Hollweg clears
the air with regard to the cry for disarma-
ment. His view was recently expressed by
a caricaturist, who showed John Bull as
little Jack Horner, with half the globe
in his plate, and yelling because Germany
wants a little bit. There is to be disarma-
ment ; but England is to remain top dog.
That would suit us, but it does not suit Ger-
many. Whatever may be denied, it must
be admitted that Germany is hemmed in. It
is “Hands off!” in every direction. The
Monroe Doctrine covers all North and South
America. There is no outlet in Africa or
Asia. There is left Australia, and with it
just a danger that England may be driven to
barter away the Northern Territory for the
sake of peace, near home. That may seem
impossible; but Cavour gave away Nice,
Garibaldi’s birthplace, to Napoleon 111.
There is hypocrisy on the part both of Eng-
land and Germany; the thing is to strip
that off and ask—“ What could they fight
about?” It is no use deceiving ourselves on
that score. There are pretexts enough. One is
Antwerp. Germany seems determined to have
the whole of the Rhine, even to the outlets,
the principal of which are the Scheldt, with
Antwerp, and the Meuse, with Rotterdam.
Germany is hand-in-glove with Holland and
Belgium, and seems to be only waiting till'
England can be put in a hole. But why not
let Germany have its way in this? For
answer observe how neatly the es-
tuary of the Scheldt faces that of the
Thames. When Flanders was great, in the
15th century, the two were of about equal
importance. Then came the accursed Reli-
gious Wars, set off by the amiable Luther,
and Antwerp was virtually destroyed. At a
later period Holland actually blocked up the
Scheldt with sunken vessels, and Antwerp’s
traffic with the ocean was quashed. The
city did not properly spring up again till it
came under Bonaparte’s dominion. He
ranked it next to Paris, and spent two mil-
lions on it. It was to be his fulcrum against
England after the Boulogne idea had to be
given up. The plans he had about it are
the plans the German. Emperor has about it
to-day, and in this he has the backing of
Chancellor Hollwf.g and Admiral Tirpitz.
The sychophantic Age stupidly girds at
Labor men’s women-folk for being ordinarily well
dressed—“ Mrs. Fisher wore cr£pe-de-chine. Mrs.
O’Malley vieux rose foulard, Mrs. McDonald silk
voile, at a recent social function—what will the
farmers’ wives and struggling women throughout
the country think of this?” Well, they will
probably think that Mr. Fisher’s wife, or Mr.
McDonald’s, has just as much right to be well
dressed as the wife of the sweating manufacturer
or the rack-renting landlord.
The Adelaide Police Court the other day
listened to a charge laid by one man against
another of stealing his thunder—or, rather, his
lightning. Accused was a young engineer, who
ran a secret wire from his office to that of large
consumers next door, and tapped their supply.
He pleaded guilty, but hired a lawyer to argue
that it was merely a foolish trick. The P.M.
thought that it was, on the contrary, a very clever
one, and worth anything up to two years; but
apparently he considered that some consideration
was due to a pioneer, and so .made it merely a
small fine.
The “Dickens Stamp” was expounded by
Rosebery at a meeting held a few weeks ago at
the London Mansion House. Dickens left be-
tween £70,000 and £BO,OOO, of which £50,000 had
been made by readings. His family and depend-
ents were numerous, and mostly, it seems, poor
hands at business; so that, of 20 descendants
(three children and 17 grandchildren) none appear
to be well off. Some, in fact, are hard-up. Next
vear is the Dickens centenary; and Rosebery’s
idea—which has been adopted—is that every lover
of Dickens in Britain, America, Australia and
elsewhere shall then buy penny “ Dickens
stamps,” and put one in every volume of his they
possess. The money is to be invested to provide
against anything like distress among the des-
cendants. The stamps will, in due time, be on
sale in Australia.
