♦
The Backbone of the Country has at-
tended at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, in great
numbers during the past week, to study the
way the Beanstalk grows. Besides learning
more things about giant vegetable-rearing
than any A., H. and I*. Society ever dreamed
of, the Backbone has had its ears soothed
with sweet melody, its laughter stirred by
masses of fruity, well-matured humor, and
its eyes delighted by some of the finest
Limbs in the Commonwealth.
** * *
The machinations of “The Woman in the
Case” continue to provide pleasing shocks
to patrons of Sydney Criterion. The
churches are observing her narrowly, and the
view spreads among those who Wowse that
Fitch intended her to symbolise either the
Scarlet Woman or the Apocalyptic Beast.
“Liberalism,” on the other hand, sees in her
the embodiment of free ferries, cheap sugar,
municipal gas, and a “Yes-Yes” to the Re-
ferenda questions—in a word, rampageous,
home-bursting Socialism. The diagnosis of
the Clarke-Meynell management is that the
lady is a money-spinner.
** * *
“Miss Gibbs” is whqre she has been these
last 250 days and nights, or thereabouts,
and this paper has no more remarks to make
on the monotonous subject. A musical
comedy so devoid of consideration for the
harassed fowl who is expected to lay a fresh
paragraph every week is no friend of the
Inky Way’s.
“PACK AND THE BEANSTALK."
1. Jack and the Beanstalk as grandfather
knew it.
2. Jack and the Beanstalk as little Georgie saw it.
3. No time for pantaloons.
Marlow’s great Bedroom drama is still
luring the multitude into the interior of the
£30,000 phoenix which has arisen from the
ruins of Sydney’s old Paddy’s Market. “See
the shop-girls’ dormitory, the greatest bed-
room scene ever staged,” shrieks the advt.;
and Billjim has been saying 'My oath !” in
large numbers. Whether Billjim doesn’t
expect to see more than he does see at the
Adelphi is another matter.
** * *
When this issue went to press the magnets
at Spencer’s Sydney picture-house, the Ly-
ceum, were Hamlet, Prince of Denmark;
Carbine, Prince of Horses; and Sydney,
Prince of Agricultural Shows. “Plamlet”
is a fine thing; the gloomy grandeur of the
royal revenge is driven right home to the
minds of the spectators. It is remarkable,
but all of the Shakspearian tragedies are so
adaptable to the cinematograph that one
would think they had been specially written
for it. On Monday, the 24th, the Theatre-
scope will replace “Hamlet” with “The
Fatal Wedding,” the play with which Mey-
nell and Gunn first made a splash in Aus-
tralia. It is a feariul fall; but the sover-
eign public must be allowed to go to the
Devil in its own inartistic way.
** * *
West’s Pictures, Ltd., now fill three
theatres in Sydney city: The Palace, the
Glaciarium, and the Bijou. They also show
“Hamlet,” as played in Elsinore, the scene
of the tragedy. But what the patrons of
West’s are lying back for is the great and
gorgeous production of “Henry VIII.,” as
played in His Majesty’s Theatre, London, by
Tree, Bourchier and Violet Vanbrugh—said
to have been the most gorgeous and costly
production ever put on the English or any
other stage. The series of pictures labelled
“Glorious Sydney” were a fine effort, and
whoever took them managed to glorify the
already beautiful city in a most artistic
manner.
Peggy Pryde, whose first appearance had
been delayed a week through illness, bustled
on to the stage of Sydney Tivoli on Satur-
day. As soon as the roar of welcome had
subsided, and she got to work, it was hard
to realise that eight years had passed since
her last turn. Time flies ; but tne brisk
little comedienne seems to have ignored the
rattle of his biplane, and gone on singing
and chattering to her audiences. R. A.
Roberts, the quick-changer, is billed to
change his eightrcharacter 'sketch, “Ringing
the Changes,” for a piratical story, entitled
“Cruel Coppinger,” on Saturday. t Conjurer
Leipzig, Tom Dawson and Miss Yetta, who
could dodge a mouse any day by taking to
her wire, are the other headliners of the pro-
gramme who will see the week out; but
Florence Challis, of the Old Bull and Bush,
and Concertina Prince are packing their
trunks.
** * *
The circus of Philippus Wirthus and
brothers, at Prince Alfred Park (Sydney),
is somewhat different from that managed by
the late Nero, one of the most sensational
showmen in the business during the time he
was lessee of the Circus Maximus (Rome).
Nowadays fights with wild animals are diffi-
cult to stage, owing to the shortage in ani-
mals, caused by their wholesale murder with
artillery by squads of sportsmen like the
Crown Prince of Germany and Ted Roose-
velt. The same with the old reliable draw
of torchlighting the ring with Christians.
The Maximus management could get Chris-
tians about seven for sixpence; but they’re
terrible hard to find these times. In fact,
Philippus has had to cut them off the bill even
in Maoriland. But, after all, frizzling Chris-
tian isn’t everything ; and probably there were
times when manager Nero would have gladly
swapped his great torchlight turn for the
Flying Jordans, or the Adeli Sisters’ wire
act, or Lizzie, the blood-sweating hippo, from
Darkest Africa, or Hamburg, or somewhere.
Anyhow, Philippus reckons his show is
more complete than the early Dago’s, and the
writer can only say that, as far as he re-
members, Philippus is right.
* # * #■
J. C. Williamson provides a histrionic
hot-cross bun in Eugene Walter’s domestic
drama, of American extraction, “Paid in
Full,” now assisting Melbourne Princess’s to
labor back into popular favor. “Paid in
Full” is an extremely clever play, with a
fundamental error, like a Campanile foutided
on pancakes. It is mainly the sad story of
one Joseph Brooks, collector in the employ
of the Latin-American Steamship Co., a
man who can’t go straight, and whose weak
efforts towards righteousness are hampered
by a pretty wife—fancy bred and fashion-
ably inclined —and a cheap billet. The first
impression we get of Joseph Cyril Mackay
Brooks is wholly favorable and sympa-
thetic ; he is polishing the family spoons, and
pushing the vacuum cleaner over the faded
carpet of his angular flat. Now, the man
who voluntarily assists his little wife to wipe
up, and to dust the. unhappy home after his
own day’s work is done, cannot be wholly
lost. But presently Joe bursts into bad
temper and invective; he 1 hurls socialistic
diatribes at the Latin-American Steamship
Co., which institution loads him with finan-
cial responsibilities, and systematically for-
gets to raise his pay; he becomes a Peter
Bowling of abusive declamation.
* * * *
We gather from bitter theatrical experi-
ence that the character addicted to rhetori-
cal display against property and corporations
is fit only for treason, spoils, and robbing
babes of the beer-money. According to this
rule of the stage, Joe Brooks naturally col-
lapses. He pinches the Latin-American S.S.
Co.’s capital to the tune of 16,000 dollars,
puts his wife into a. swell hotel, gives his
nagging mother-in-law the time.of her life,
and then —slump ! Captain Williams, who
is the Latin-American S.S. Co., has been
aware of the peculation from the first dime.
In fact, he has been luring Joseph to his
doom, and, at the critical moment, comes
down on his victim like a howling gale. Capt.
Williams is sweet on Mrs. Brooks, and Joe’s
infamy culminates in the act of sending
his pretty wife to the boss’s bachelor quar-
ters, to draw up terms of peace at the
bachelor’s own price. In its midnight in-
terview with Mrs. Brooks, the Latin-Ameri-
can S.S. Co. explains with emphasis and lu-
cidity what a shocking bad devil it was in
its youth and early middle age; but when
the young wife successfully resists encroach-
ment, and supplicates for death before dis-
honor, Williams throws up his brief, de-
clares his only desire was to prove how good
she was, hands over documentary absolution
for Joe, and takes the limelight, swelling
with fatuous gratification over a good deed
and a clear conscience. It is at this point
that the bad currant in Eugene Walters’s
hot-cross bun assumes the dimensions of
King Richard’s hump. If the Latin-Ameri-
can S.S. Co. didn’t covet its clerk’s pretty
missus, and if it wasn’t really a vindictive old
man of the sea, and a disreputable Bully
Hayes 11., why in the name of simple human
sanity did it go to so much trouble to inveigle
Joe Brooks into embezzlement, and arrange
the domestic and professional ruination of
that clamorous insect?
* * # *
The insufferable Joe, as handled by Cyril
Mackay, was the most vital feature of Sat-
urday night’s display. Mackay’s passion
wants mellowing ; he is inclined to savage a
part like this. But his work has strength
and truth. It is a stout if somewhat over-
painted picture of a miserable sinner clothing
his weakness with rage. Evidently one
Jimsy Smith is supposed to be the chief
masculine item, but he appears almost as an
intrusion, under the somewhat hesitating
treatment of William Desmond. George Bry-
ant is Capt. Williams, Bully Hayes ll.—a
bully who has abandoned South Sea “trad-
ing,” and taken up a more lucrative and vic-
ious form of Private Enterprise piracy. Bry-
ant is admirably suited as the gruff, tough,
ancient mariner. If the sea dog appears
a little too sinister in the light of third act
developments, Walters is to blame. The
author is responsible, too, for the fact that
Susie Vaughan’s part, as the mother-in-law,
and Florrie Gleeson’s little sister seem to
come into the play with reluctance. They
are necessary, but fictitious. Katherine
Grey is the pretty wife of Joe Brooks, the
peace-offering to the Latin-American S.S.
Co. The character is drawn with intelli-
gence and subdued power; but here,
as elsewhere, Miss Grey subdues too
much —her people have something of
the monotony of figures seen in a
mist. “Paid in Pull” is never dull. On
the whole it is a performance to be grateful
for, and Saturday’s audience expressed fre-
quent fervid gratitude in the usual manner.
Reminiscences , etc., of the production of
“ Olivette ,” produced at Melbourne Princess's by
the Melbourne (amateur) Comic Opera Society.
“Right is Might,” at Melbourne King’s,
is another story of Yankee adventure spat-
tered with comic scenes and sentimentality.
Considered as a play, the action is insuffici-
ently lively and sustained, and the dramatic
incidents are apt to lose their grip. The
yarn, however, has one recommendation. It
contains no villain juggling gleefully with
the Commandments. Its nearest approach
to a sinful force is James Ilappington, a
railway boss. James wants to invade a
lady’s land with one of his railway lines.
The lady, Allene Houston, declines his re-
quest, and when he decides to ignore her
objections, and pursue his scheme, she
adopts a fighting attitude. The result is
‘ some picturesque work by a troop of cow-
boys borrowed from the cheaper American
fiction. They harass James’s operations
with vigor, and worry John Ashby, his chief
engineer. But Ashby is able now and then
to forget their annoyances. lie is in love
with the dazzling Allene, whom he woos be-
tween arguments. By 10.40 p.m. she
is quite touched and submissive. There-
upon the conflict ends, the railway is built,
and she lays her head in the vicinity of his
wish-bone. The play contains a curiosity
in the person of Harry Marshall, a cow-
puncher with an unrequited affection. This
individual, enacted by Kenneth Brampton,
mopes about in a state of semi-somnambul-
ism. He never seems merry and bright, and
probably it is his sighs that have singed the
vegetation off Rege Robins’s scenery, and
given the latter its Sahara effects. Herbert
Leigh makes James Happington a -passable
character, and Walter Dalgleish is an ac-
ceptable humorist as Cowboy Jim. Miss
Bashford’s study of Miss Lily Bell is un-
even; Miss Beatrice Day as Allene Houston
is effective; and H. R. Roberts as John
Ashby adds another to his unexampled gal-
lery of treacley heroes.
* * *
“The Belle of Brittany” still draws her
admirers to the Melbourne Royal, where the
Marquis de St. Gautier continues to ; relate
his interesting tales. Thus far Melbourne
has felt grateful for such a creation as the
comic Marquis. Everything about him is
suggestive of humor, from his oddly-ac-
coutred person to his feverish-looking nose.
Talking ,of his nose, The Bulletin cannot
recall an organ that’ excites such bold sus-
nicions. Considered in the most flattering
light, it looks like the outward and visible
sign of an inner and spirituous leaning.

know, conjugates “to love” as an irregular
verb.

Motoring Gossip.
Cheap motor-cars, like all other cheap lines of
machinery, soon become absolutely valueless, as
their faults are so generally known to everybody,
and also that the market is flooded with them in
second-hand condition. High-grade cars of the
Wolseley or Renault type are always a saleable
asset, and are generally difficult to procure second-
hand, and to anyone starting motoring, to advise
them to buy the best is a safe proposition,*
At the Opera House the Perezoffs persist
in playing games with the crockery of a res-
taurant. These recent arrivals are seven
in number, but only two are industrious in
the pursuit of jugglery. The other five
are more in the nature of ornaments. In-
cluded among Saturday’s additions were
the Zanfrellas and Eunice and Zena. Ihe
first are equilibrists, who perform, a short,
neat act with statuesque effects. The other
pair are musicians, clever enough to make
commonplace business look like a new form
of cleverness.

Taits’ Melbourne tabernacle, the Glaciar-
ium, has commenced a special production ot
“Called Back,” with vocal accompaniments
by a lady singer. Extra music is also
supplied by an energetic orchestra, while a
lecturer’s baritone describes the stirring in-
cidents in organ tones.
*# * *
During the present week Spencer has been
producing “Hamlet” at his Melbourne shop.
The film is described as “the crowning work
of motion pictures,” and is certainly instruc-
tive and elaborate. Many of the incidents
are performed in the open air, and such
scenes as Ophelia’s funeral gain in simple
power thereby. Of other recent films “His
Trust” and “His Trust Fulfilled” have been
favorites with the emotional throng. Both
portray the unnatural goodness of an an-
cient nigger belonging to the marmalade
species of Uncle Toms.

The Royal Hawaiians, recently of Syd-
ney and northern parts, will descend upon
Melbourne, at the Athenaeum, on Monday
next.
** * *
The new St. Ivilda Theatre, which the
Amalgamated Pictures, Ltd., has anchored
wdthin easy distance of the railway station,
should fill rival showmen with envy. It is
a commodious structure, fitted and furnished
with exuberance. The walls are plain
white, punctured with leaded windows in the
soothing tint of watered absinthe. The
ceiling, white, and of fibrous plaster, allows
floral forms to dance on its vaulted chest.
The D.C., capable of seating about 250, has
the sweet look of a vice-regal reserve. The
seats are of polished wood, upholstered in a
shade of stewed quince. Carpets crawl de-
murely over the floors, while a lounge, pre-
sumably for smoking purposes, retires grace-
fully to the rear. The main hall holds
about 1300, and is comfortably chaired.
Rich curtains are provided as garments for
the screen, and the light, diffused from clus-
ters of electric globes, completes the picture
of elegance.
*m * *
“The Servant in the House,”, first produced
in Adelaide by that hard-working and en-
terprising body, the Literary Theatre, proves
to be even “more so” than “The Third-Floor
Back.” The mysterious stranger comes
from Asia, and wears Asiatic robes, and is
called Manson —-which is suggestively like
the Son of Man. Also, while serving as a
butler, he claims “loyalty, obedience and
love” from his English employers, and when
the time-serving Bishop, blocked by him at
every turn, would desperately like to “see
him nailed up as an example,” Manson re-
plies that other gentlemen of similar views
have expressed a similar wish in the past!
(That passage, by the way, seems very far-
fetched to an Australian audience, which
hardly knows the English custom of literally
nailing up crows, moles, rats, bad money and
other worthless objects.) The play, set in a
vicar’s household, preserves the classic Uni-
ties. It is divided for convenience into five
acts, but at each curtain-rise the characters
are found just as they were before the inter-
val, and there is no reason why the play
should not run straight on. The Vicar has
disowned his brother, a drain-man, but has
adopted the niece. The rough customer
comes, after 15 years, to make trouble and
get his child; but Manson turns aside the
wrath of the wrathful, and generally shows
everybody the right track. At the end the
v icar has his coat off, and is going to take
his share in a perilous job of cleansing, the
stench long noticeable having been found to
come from an awful pile of mouldering bones
right under the church itself —which is pos-
sibly Symbolic. There are seven characters,
and the only unsympathetic one is the Bishop,
who is rather unnecessarily made to promote
church-building, with a view to himself col-
laring part of the fund. It is a “fat” part,
and James Anderson made the most of it.
Arthur L. Smith took Manson as young,
brown-skinned, beardless, and earnestly
simple. The drain-man’s part was a triumph
for Wilfrid Neill, his identity of both face
and voice being completely lost. Miss Zoe
Reid scored in the difficult part of the girl,
and the others, though good, were not more
than good. The play is full of quiet fun,
and there seems to be nothing to keep it
from success on the ordinary stage.
** * *
The approach of Easter has made Adelaide
even more than usually musical. The Bach
Society broke out with a really good ren-
dering of Elgar’s “Dream of Gerontius,” and
the - Orpheus followed. The rearranged
King’s Theatre has made a fresh start with
the Scarlet Troubadours. At the Royal,
Clarke and Meynell have had a season of
“The Gay Gordons.” Frank Lincoln, ob-
viously nourished on the Seymour Hicks tra-
dition, dashes easily through a romantic-
comic hero’s part, begging (for example) the
dainty girl of his heart not to stand on the
sandwiches during a love-scene. If this
kind of hero is to become frequent, some of
the tenors who can sing, but can’t act, are
going to get left. Florence Imeson plays
up to him charmingly. Tom Graves is
funny, but apt to be too elaborately and self-
consciously so. “The Gay Grisette,” with
which the co. ended its season, is of a
slightly inferior brand. Williamson’s juven-
iles have now come in to the Royal, and are
reviving memories of Dorothy Vane and
Lauri in “The Geisha.” From the Tivoli,
Julian Rose has gone Londonwards, after
telling once more the story of the wedding-
party that fainted —the Irish member of it,
for his part, feinting with his right, and
landing deponent neatly with his left. Also
gone, Fasola, of the tons of apparatus, and
the quick, careless “Nothing in it: don’t
trouble to applaud” manner.
AT POVERTY POINT.
The Photographic Society of N. S. Wales is to open
an International Exhibition of camera work at the
Pitt-street rooms of the Royal Art Society on Friday
of next week. It will be the first time that a thor-
oughly representative collection of Australasian work
has been brought under one roof; and, besides the
Australasian work, what is claimed to be the finest
collection of photographic pictures ever sent out of
Great Britain will be on view. There will be 600
pictures altogether, of which 110 will be in the
English section, 300 in the N. S. Wales division,
150 from other Australian States and Maoriland, and
30 from the islands of Polynesia. The English, col-
lection is unusually attractive; it would be impossible
to see the like of it in England under one roof, be-
cause there is no national exhibition of camera work
in the Gold Country, where each little society has
its own small show. The task of awarding prizes
and orders of merit for this mass of work will fall
on Messrs. Tyree, Henry King and Norman Lindsay.
The exact position concerning the visit or pro-
posed visit of the Beecham Opera Co. is that
George Marlow, Ltd., is holding dates open for the
co. at the Princess’s, Melbourne, and Adelphi, Sydney.
As a guarantee of good faith, those negotiating
with Marlow have deposited a sum alleged to be
£l5OO, to be forfeited if the dates are not accepted
on or before April 30. If the dates are accepted,
another £l5OO has then to be deposited as a guaran-
tee of more good faith. Beecham is to transplant
the whole of his Covent Garden Co. arid scenery,
and play 12 weeks in Melbourne, and the same in
Sydney.
Adelaide’s Literary Theatre has now formed a club
to promote the study of plays of literary and artistic
value, and to further the interests of dramatic art
generally. Having fixed its headquarters in Grenfell-
street, it proposes to strengthen the theatre’s hold
on the public by arranging a series of lectures. Paris
Nesbit will speak on Goethe’s “Faust,” Professor
Darnley Naylor on the “Medea” of Euripides, Rev.
Depledge-Sykes on Ibsen’s “Brand,” and lawyer
Ronald Finlayson on “Oscar Wilde and the Stage.”
The club is also going to discuss Maeterlinck and
Rostand and Shaw, and perform various playlets,
including Shaw’s “Dark Lady of the Sonnets, which
is practically a plea for a National Theatre. All of
which the subscribers to the theatre get without
extra charge.
Barnum Powell, author of “The Matabele Sham-
paign,” “Aids to Skiting,” and other £sdandard
works, is to lecture in Australia and Maoriland in
1912. A while ago Barnum was a comparatively
silent man, but he has to live up somehow to the
proud position of Big Chief of the Boy Skites.
On the return of Hugh Ward to Wellington (M.L.),
that city rose up and handed him three cheers for
the help he and his co. gave in establishing a chil-
dren’s hospital last year. A citizens’ welcome was
given the co. in the Town Hall, and speeches, il-
luminated addresses, songs, silver souvenirs, photos
and general hallelujah rent the air. Nothing has,
however, been done to permanently recognise Ward’s
effort for the hospital, except that the committee
proposes to name a cot or a fowlhouse, or some-
thing, after him.
If the Far North does not like the acting, it says
so. Long ago, when Charley Dillon was playing
“Richelieu” at Ravenswood, he was ’requested by
someone in his audience to “lift up his petticoats
and dance a hornpipe.”
Australian actress Alice Crawford was lately ap-
pearing with Granville Barker and Lillah McCarthy
in a series of Viennese playlets at the London Palace.
The Bendigonian, by-the-bye, is cultivating the
sweetest, noli-me-tangere type of beauty. A recent
photo shows her gazing heavenwards in expensive
furs, and looking like a Madonna spending a day or
two in Switzerland.
Comedian W. S. Percy, who will leave Australia
in June, to try his talents on the Fog people, has
been to Greymouth (M.L.), paying a visit to Tom
Pollard, who practically “made” him. Percy
hints that he may return to Australasia with a co.
of his own.

Mrs. Cordner Miles, an old-Sydney favorite on the
concert platform, now living in retirement, has
bought the music of “Die Meistersinger” (Wagner)
for the Sydney Philharmonic Societv—-“in memory
of her dead friend, Mrs. Sheridan" Moore.” This
Mrs. Moore was Flora Harris, who sang the princi-
pal soprano parts in oratorio in the ’6o’s. She
married Mr. J. Sheridan Moore, a well-known writer
of prose and verse in those days.
Though Bernard Pshaw gains no Cockney popu-
larity to speak of, he is steadily attaining a cosmo-
politan vogue. Italian versions of “Candida” and
“Arms and the Man” were recently given in Rome,
and now “The Showing-up of Blanco Posnet” has
been presented in Jap before the Japanese.
George Edwardes, as recently as February 26,
talked of producing a new musical comedy at
London Gaiety on March 4, under the title of
Pretty Peggy.” But that was the name of the
Peg Woffington comedy which George Musgrove
brought to Australia a few years ago, and in which
Nellie Stewart played the heroine—a very few years
ago, seeing that Rupert Clarke’s racing mare, Pretty
Peggy, christened after the play, is still in training.
“D.” : Remarkable how the oldsters re-appear in
the chickens of Williamson’s Juvenile Opera Co '
J“re’s a very pronounced Clayton, Fred Leslie is
faithfully reproduced, and decent imitations of Pat
Bathurst, Flo Young, Rose Musgrove, and Carrie
Moore are on view. Several of the laddies promise
to get there, but in all probability little Audrey
Duckworth will be the first to arrive. She has the
true spirit and piquancy of a comic-opera star, that
only needs maturing. The question is, is not this
talent, like wine, the better for maturing in dark-
ness ?
AJ. M. Barrie yarn. It was at the rehearsal
of a Barrie play, Barrie, among others, being on the
stage. During the rehearsal a modest male member
of the cast remarked: “Pooh, dear boy, what are
words ! I can express everything facially! ” “Well ”
said Barrie, chipping in, “go to the back of the
stage and express in your face that you have a
younger brother who was born in Shropshire, but is
now staying in a boarding-house on the South
Coast.
EVERYTHING MIXED.
MULGA BILL (after a joyful week 'at the Show):
“By Cripes (hie) they've got the exhibits
mix shed to-day!”
“Condot”: Is the writer of the par (B. 23/3/’ll)
re the Rev. Jack Costelloe quite sure tnat the Bent-
ley whom Costelloe met in America, and who had
left the boards for the pulpit, was not Walter Bent-
ley? Since the middle ’9o’s Bentley has had a
patchwork career. In ’9B he was stranded in Bris-
bane. He scratched a co. together, but it was
playing “Hamlet” with only the Hamlet. Then
he tried elocution teaching. This brought little
profit, so he essayed journalism, lecturing, and elec-
tioneering. Perhaps journalism was his best ven-
ture. In Brisbane he started a smart little paper
called Saturday Night, which was issued at about
7.30, and contained all the sporting results. Walter
was one of the first pressmen in Australia to use
carrier pigeons. At the opening of one sailing sea-
son he sent a man down to Moreton Bay on a
launch with a basket of carriers. The races fin-
ished in the Bay. The Observer went to press
without the results, and marvelled to find them
in Saturday Night. Bentley would have made a
great journalist. He had a good nose for news.
The paper stopped suddenly, however, and Bentley
went to New York, where he became rector of a
fashionable church. Writer of the Costelloe par. says
that Costelloe helped Bentley with his theatricals in
aid of the tabernacle. I know that Bentley started
a theatre class in connection with his church, and
that he and his flock performed in aid of the
funds. After a while he forsook the church, which
was very gloomy and irksome to him, and returned
to the irreligious stage.
“U.S”: Curious what accidents “fix” certain
almost historical costumes. There is a quaint case
in “The Shaughraun.” The incident happened at
Wallack’s Theatre, N’York, where J. C. Williamson
graduated. Boucicault, truly Irish, found, when the
first night arrived, that he had wholly forgotten to
provide himself with a costume for Conn. He went
to the wardrobe and picked out a scarlet hunting-
coat, used by Tony Lumpkin in “She Stoops to Con-
quer,” and Goldfinch in “The Road to Ruin.” This
had to be torn to suit Conn. Similarly did he pick
out an odd pair of hunting-boots and the hunting-
cap. And that was how Conn’s attire was settled
for all time.
“Exile” (from Noiseville) : Saw a subject of much
dispute last night (February 27) at the Coliseum
Music Hall, the most beautiful building of its kind
in London. It was a weird sketch written by W.
S. Gilbert, describing the last hours in prison
of a condemned murderer. It is the worst tabloid
of murderdrama London has ever seen, and indi-
cates that, if Gilbert had not met Arthur Sullivan,
he would have beaten Victor Hugo and Gaboriau in
the Gruesome Stakes by two laps in every mile. A
well-bred and well-dressed, though mixed, audience
heartily hooted “The Hooligan”—the first time, I
believe, the audience had ever hissed anything in its
life.
“Haresfoot”: A tribute is to be paid to a good
play. The German Emperor, in kindness to George
V., visits London next month, May. There is to
be a special performance at Drury Lane, on the
17th, in his honor. George was asked what play
the Emperor would like best, and plumped for Bul-
wer’s “Money.,” as against the whole range of the
drama. So it will be acted, by the best performers
in England. Strange to add, an act from “Money”
was selected, quite independently, as part of an
entertainment to be given on Coronation night at
Beerbohm Tree’s playhouse.
“Therobo”: Two notable features in Melbourne on
Good 1 riday—Bach’s Passion Music, according to
Matthew, by the Philharmonic; and, yet more out of
the way, v Ludovico’s Passion Music according to
John, at St. Patrick’s Cathedral. Ludovico is a
good deal older than Bach, who is abhorred by the
R.C.’s, as a Lutheran. In the Ludovico music
three priests -sang the chief solos, Fathers King,
Gibbons and Mangan.
“Sardonyx”: Isn’t it “Busk” himself (B. 30/3/’ll)
who is a bit snobbish in blaming J. C. Williamson
for bringing from England the juvenile contingent
of the “Bluebird” cast? Australian youngsters
would probably do as well, or even, as “B” sug-
gests, better; but it would only be after they had
received the requisite training. And, as J.C.W.
has already the trained kiddies to his hand, he would
be several kinds of idiot to leave them behind and
start afresh with Australian raw material.
“Snell”: Anything is possible to a man with a fad.
Hearken to a moving story from Maoriland, where
Cain and Playbill’s mammoth circus and menagerie
was performing in a tent to “crowded houses.” Pro-
fessor Thunder’s ma»row-freezing act, with three
poisonous and irreconcilable man-eating tigers, was
the sensational feature of the show. The caged ring
was being prepared for this horrifying turn, when
Agnes, the matronly tigress, awoke from her cus-
tomary lethargy, and seized on a helping of “supe.”
This attendant was bearing a set of pine steps at
the time, and, fortunately for him, Aggie took in a
portion of the timber along With her mouthful of
man. Agues for once was deaf to the stern die-
tates of Professor Thunder. The Lord helps those
who help themselves—so the tigress clung to her
‘supe. Of course excitement ran high, the uproar
wa ? terrific. Red-hot irons were introduced, and,
amidst shouting arid screams, and the clamor of
beasts, Agnes was burnt off her prey. Next day
Cain and Playbill discovered that an eminently re-
spectable resident from among their audience had
reported them, to the Society for the Prevention of
C ruelty to Animals for singeing Agnes.
. “it ud ’’v, * n one week’s Bulletin I find mention
of the Pnma Donna,” played by the children of
Charles Dickens, and the visit of the young Lord
Hopetoun. This recalls things, or brings various
things together. The “Prima Donna” was bv
Boucicault, and made a hit at the London Princess’s
with Caroline Heath (afterwards Mrs. Wilson Bar-
rett), Agnes Robertson (afterwards Mrs. Boucicault)
and our Australian friend Cathcart, in the cast.
This was under Charles Kean’s management. Lord
Hopetoun was then courting Agnes Robertson, Mrs.
Kean s ward; but she ran away to America with
Boucicault. They returned with the trump card of
The Colleen Bawn.” Years later Governor Hope-
toun would not patronise Brough and Boucicault at
the Melbourne Bijou—a strange rankle of his dad’s
old grudge. So little Aggie missed being our first
G.-G.’s mother. She was living, according to my
last information, and happy in the success of “Dot”
and Nina. “Dot” (D.G.) is in the front rank of
London producers, after learning the craft in Aus-
tralia. Another sister married John Clayton. The
elder Boucicault’s favorite son, Aubrey, was killed
in the Paddington railway accident, which occurred
while Mr. and Mrs. Boucicault were acting in “The
Shaughraun” at Drury Lane.
Apropos the music-hall’s rivalry with the theatre.
At latest advices, short plays by Sydney Grundy
and Henry Arthur Jones were being produced at
London “halls,” Tree’s “Henry VIII.” was being
shown per cinematograph at one house, the serious-
minded Granville Barker and Lillah McCarthy, his
wife, were at another, and at a third the Matheson
Langs were giving a condensed version of the
“Shrew.”
“Busk”: Failing a Brooke or a Sullivan, we have
to take classic drama through the biograph. This
week we have a new adaptation of “Virginius.” 1
fancy Shakspeare thought of this as a subject. Al-
fieri’s “Virginia” was the first notable play on the
theme, but that of Knowles was better; it was
acted in Australia by Brooke, Montgomery, and a
few others. Written for Kean, the latter made the
error of refusing it, whereon Macready snapped it,
and achieved his great success. Brooke’s choicest
tragic parts were Othello, lago, Richelieu, Vir-
ginius and Sir Giles Overreach. Barrett’s Virginius
missed the effect in the great Court scene. Brooke’s
last Australian appearance was in this part. He
had performed it at the Marylebone Theatre, Lon-
don, when only 16 years old. He was known then
as the Boy Tragedian, and was travelling under the
care of his mother. This was his only recorded Lon-
don performance till he appeared as Othello, at the
Olympic, when nearly 30.
The millennium may be expected any day
now, Judkins having bogged into the business.
The remarkable egotist from Victoria has pon-
dered over the Melbourne strike, and has decided
that all that is necessary is a “new unionism”—
an organisation “representing equally the interests
of employers and employees.” Well, what about
Wages Boards and Arbitration Courts? Don’t
they represent both parties? And that being so,
why doesn’t Judkins start kicking the Govern-
ment, to compel it to appoint Wages Boards all
round—in the Cow districts for a start?
John Madden admits that he, too, wrote
verses when he was younger. He remains proud
of his performance, asserting that his rhymes
were stolen by other bards. Of course, the
Madden poems will be unearthed. A man’s
literary past is always waiting for him round the
corner. Many Australians beside George Reid,
Parkes, Neild and McLean have found it out, and
learned that too often what is written in haste is
repented at leisure. John gave up poetry soon
after taking to law ; no doubt he realised that it
is so much easier to make a judge than a poet.
A lot of the credit for the far-reaching
decision of N.S.W. State Secretary MacDonnel to
throw open the State-subsidised country hospitals
to all medical practitioners is due to the per-
tinacity of Dr. Bobart, of Nowra, who stuck to
the agitation for years, hammering away at suc-
cessive Administrations until the point was
gained. Bobart is a Melbourne Medical School
man, who afterwards got a London degree and
entered the navy. He was surgeon on the Dart
on the Australian coast for some years, and even-
tually nailed up his shingle at Nowra.
From Australia ’Murkan Labor lecturer
Mills, M.A., and “special Commissioner for the
city of Milwaukee,” will go on to Maoriland in
June, at the invitation of the Labor party. Mills,
who is the author of (among other things) “The
Struggle for Existence,” should be in time to
wheel into a vigorous argument, which has been
started in Wellington, as to whether a workman
can live and bring up a family on £2 Bs. per week.
That is the amount paid by the City Council to
some of its employees, and, when last week they
asked for an increase that would enable them to
live without resource to charity, and without feel-
ing that they were “the scum of Labor,” one fat
councillor told them that they were suffering from
“improvidence or extravagant wives.”
The Argus has been struggling to drum
up sympathy for a white Egyptian donkey which
S. P. McKay, of Berwick, tried to import. The
blonde ass from Egypt was an expensive neddy ;
but the Commonwealth authorities ruled him out
of order, Egyptian donkeys being subject to a
nasty disease communicable not only to asses but
also to horses. The Akgus is almost as angrv
over the donkey as it was over the “Six Satyrs ”
and the Petriana, and its concern for the quad-
ruped excites a suspicion that the long-eare#i
beast was being imported to lend valuable support
to the Liberal party in the crusade against the
Referenda.
That passionless stage-producer of cold
figures, Auditor-General Israel, has a few words
to say in his last annual report about those States
the Commonwealth has so generously
treated, but which nevertheless lately decided to
demand interest on the amount owing for trans-
ferred properties. “It was pointed out,”says he,
“ in my last annual report that the sum of £68,342
is owing to the Post Office by the several States
for shipping and other telegrams, this amount
being due up to June 30. 1904. ... It does not
appear that the amounts charged can be legally
waived.” Mr. Israel is a courteous man. A less
affable one would have made insulting remarks
about shuffiesome. debtors who pose as merely
creditors.
THE ACTOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“An actor’s life’s a dog’s life,”
He said, “and that’s a fact:
If I had any children
I would not let them act.
“I’ve seen the dense crowds cheering,
Or dumb with sympathy;
But stale and uninspiring
Their plaudits are to me.
“The limelight is attractive
To those beyond its glare;
But those who know its snadows,
Can find no romance there.
“For me a simple home-life,
With children at my knee;
I’m sick of stage-light’s glitter,
And notoriety.”
He drank his beer and vanished,
To face his dreary toil.
“That bloke?” said Bung. “Oh, he is
A super at the Koyal.”

WHAT CONSTIPATION MEANS.
Constipation resembles nothing 1 so much as the
stopping of the sewerage system of a city, and
you know the outbreak of disease that would
result if this should happen. Then try to realise,
the danger to yourself if you allow your bowels to
become clogged. The poisonous matter which
should pass off is absorbed into the blood, which
is the cause of numerous diseases. When you are
troubled this way there is just one thing to do—
get a box of Chamberlain’s Tablets. They cleanse
and invigorate the stomach and bowels, and clear
the system of all foul accumulations and restore it
to a normal condition.*

SHOW-TIME CRITICISM.
B ILL: “ Musl ha' been a strayer orlright."
If Justice Sim has rightly interpreted
Maoriland’s Workers’ Compensation Act, that
measure ought to be ashamed of itself. A young
Maori, working for a contractor, was killed. He
was married to a Maori according to native eus-
tom, as his father and mother had been before
him. The wife and the father joined in an action
to recover compensation ; but Sim, J., following
a bad precedent, held that marriage by native
custom gave the wife no standing in court. So
the insurance co. saves another hit for thedivi-
dend—unless it decides that it lias better ideas of
justice than the Law, and pays the moral claim
accordingly. *

The Argus mentioned the other day that
a man was fined £5O, with the alternative of six
months in a hard, stone cell, for “ having repre-
sented himself as a medical practitioner, without
having been registered.” This method of dealing
with a non-unionist by an impartial law should
have struck the Argus as a disgraceful outrage on
the liberty of the subject. But on this occasion
the old lady somehow wrote no strident leader
condemning this enforcement of the principle of
“ preference to unionists.”

“ Hamer” : There are occasions on which
I have some fear for the Referenda. The other
day an enlightened citizen,
after a brief conversation
about the weather and the
way the fish were biting,
asked me suddenly what this
Referendum was that all the
talk was about. I suggested
that he was a stranger, but
he Somewhat indignantly
denied it. Then I started
to enlighten him by telling
him that the Referendum was
to give greater powers to the
Australian Government. He
seemed quite amazed to hear
of the existence of an AuS-<
tralian Government, but
dimly remembered having
taken part in celebrations
in honor of the establishment
of something of the sort; I
asked him if he was on the
roll. “Well,” he said, “a
man came round to collect
my name, and I suppose it
was that—unless it was the
dog-tax, or something.” A
man like that will naturally
vote “No.” He is so enlightened.
South Aus. feels pleased with itself over
its youthful military recruits. For tKitchener’s
Military College at Canberra, the State, on popu-
lation, was only entitled to send three out of 33
students; but eight entered, all passed the
doctor, and six passed the stiff examination in all
kinds of scholastic subjects. Meanwhile, Ma
came a cropper. On population, she was entitled
to 11 young officers ; but she put up 28, and the
doctors turned down 10 of them. Then in the
examination (which was the same all oyer
Australia), 12 of the remaining 18 were rejected.
True, it was a N.S.W. boy, N. E. Biden, who
headed the whole list, with nearly 10,000 out of a
possible 11,000 marks; but, as things go, large
N.S.W. and little S.A. each get six in.
The Randwick (Sydney) Asylum for
Children is to go. Built about GO years back, on
Crown land, out of a bequest by Catherine Hayes,
the institution has long outlived its usefulness;
and a sister charity, the Catherine Hayes Hospital,
has hardly a patient. Private endowments have
kept the institution going since State-aid stopped,
20 years ago; and now Minister Beeby has
decided to resume the 20 acres of land, and put
it to more effective use. His main troubles are to
decide (1) how best to use £53,000, left under
various wills for the benefit of the children of the
asylum, and (2) how to usefully use the hospital,
which cannot be disposed of. As for the land, he
proposes to plant on it a badly-needed teachers’
training college.

“ B. de D.”: The worst, case of political
ignorance that has ever fallen on me happened
while I was waiting to
have my hair sub-
edited. A huge, ill-
dressed man was having
his stuff cut down with
scissors and airing his
views on politics at the
same time. He declared
his intention to vote
“No” on the census
because if it were car-
ried all religions would
pass into the hands of
the Commonwealth.
The man with the shears
knew more than this,
but, not wishing to
offend, gently remarked
that it was open to every-
body to vote “ object.”
The colossal monument
of ignorance, however,
declared that he was
going to be on the safe
side abd vote “No” to
everything proposed by
the Labor party. After
he had gone I asked the
hair sub-editor who the
man was. “He’s a
smart chap,” he replied
—“a Self-made man. He can’t read or write, but
lie’s got two terraces of bouses.” And Alf Deakin
and Willie Watts-his-narne are rushing about the
country “appealing to the intelligence of the
electors.”
It is admitted now by officers of the Terra
Nova that the vessel was overloaded when she
departed from M.L. for the southern ice-pack
with the Scott expedition. One gale that fell upon
her with furious wind-assault and sea-battery gave
all on board so close a view of D. .Tones’s locker
that they could see the mould on the hinges. A
large quantity of paraffin, tons of coal, and a
couple of ponies were lost or thrown overboard ;
the pumps went on strike and refused to work
overtime, with the result that all hands and the
seasick scientists had to collect in buckets the
waves that poured below and heave them in the
direction Jonah went; the fires had to be extin-
guished to save the boilers; and all was dash and
bang, and occasional glimpses of the horizon, and
sodden prayers. It was a great rally, and when
it was over and done with the expedition combed
the brine out of its hair and looked round in grati-
tude for a plate to put thrippence in.
The alleged “ noble Red Man,” who only
the other day finished collecting the scalps
of frontiersmen, is now “civilised.” He has
adopted the white man’s customs, taken the white
man’s university degrees, and wears the white
man’s plug hat over his impassive countenance.
And, having reached this stage, lie writes an
article in the Outlook —which is a white man’s
review—to say that he is neither as noble nor as
wise as he was when he grunted in his wigwam
and went out, arrayed in one feather and
warpaint, to remove the skull-upholstery of the
passing stranger. He says that the Indian,
before he was “civilised,” used to sit down and
think till he almost burst with accumulated wis-
dom. The hope of each good Indian was that, by
guarding his tongue in youth, he might mature in
his age a thought which would be of service to his
people—as, for instance, no doubt, some entirely
new method of massacre. This frank criticism
may be all right for an American review ; but it
is the sort of thing ,T. Bull can’t afford to coun-
tenance. If the Egyptian starts to tear things to
pieces from his standpoint, and the flattened
Bengali tells the world how he has been undone,
and the Zulu and West African proclaim that they
were better and happier before they were saved.
Bull may be reduced to such a state that he will
plead: “Well, anyway, if I have taken your
land, inflicted missionaries on you, shelled you
with warships, made you work and ruined your
peace of mind, you must admit that I have at least
brought you the blessings of rum and rifles.”

So rapidly grows the S. M. Herald
column in which people seek life-partners (“ no
triflers”), that the day may come when the vener-
able tenant of Sydney’s tri-lateral Temple of
Journalism will go forth to the world as the S.M.
Herald and Matrimonial Gazette. A month’s
study of this “Personal and Missing Friends”
department reveals much humor and not a
little pathos. A touching thing this, with the
ring of sincerity, from a recent issue :
Honest workman with his own cottage seeks a wife
with blue eyes and a little money.
Blue eyes and a little money—a lot of romance
and a little reality. It recalls an old story of the
dying Parkes. A medical consultation had been
arranged, and Dr. Angel Money was announced.
“Angel Money ! ” exclaimed the old statesman.
“What a beautiful name! How well it makes
the best of both worlds ! ”
“ Snakebite,” from a Prohibition town:—
When the Powlett State coal-mine was opened and the
township of Wonthaggi was born, Vic. Premier John
Weary, having given up liquor himself, decided that the
infant should be brought up dry, too. Unfortunately,
when you start a new town you mostly have to start it
with old sinners, more or less; and that’s where John’s
excellent intentions came to grief. “Pigs,” or sly-prog
shops, soon got to work, and to-day drinking proceeds
in the most open way'. There are at least a dozen shops
where you can drink your glass of beer in full view of the
street. Also, several of the boarding-houses and refresh-
ment-rooms have their private cellars. The police make
futile and desultory raids, but v’ith scarcely any result.
Even if they find a room full of men and hop-fumes, they
can usually do nothing. The law forbids any person to
have more than two gallons of intoxicating liquor on the
premises ; but mostly the enterprising “pig ” has accom-
modating neighbors, and he leaves a tv'o-gallon jar with
each. Sometimes these neighbors entertain themselves
and their friends, after which the jars are filled up with
water—or anything else. Then the thirsty public comes
along and pays its6d. per glass for the muck. All of which
is a good argument for a State hotel, where decent, drink
might be bought at reasonable price. In default of a
State pub, Wonthaggi will soon out-club Mildura. One
“club” has a license already, and another with a mem-
bership of 300 is getting its beer-pump ready.

LEISURE AND LAMP POSTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
It seemed to him that young- men got the wages of
men, and they had too much money, and were not taugh,
the lesson of thrift. The hours, too, were so short that
when the evening came they had, beside their leisuret
unabated visror; and if these young people were not in-
structed, or had not someone to give them good employ-
ment and occupation, they would hang around the street
corners and lean against the lamp posts.— Prominent
Baptist.
Observe us, the black-hatted snarlers and sweaters of
white man and nigger 1
We want the penultimate coin and the very last vestige
of vigor;
Each corner and lamp post we hate, for the youth of the
land gather round it
When they should h» packed in a hall while we mangle
the Word or expound it.
We have parlor games and weak tea, and a roomful of
books—mostly piffle ;
We whisper and walk on our toes or enjoy a disconsolate
sniffle ;
With windows hermetically sealed and the sounds of
existence excluding,
We get a soporific effect most conducive to goodness and
brooding.
We shun as Hell’s beacons the lights and the shadows
and laughter delightful ;
Life lures us, yet frightens us, so we take refuge in stuffi-
ness spiteful ;
In brightly-lit halls we feel Safe from the whirlpools of
Sin at street level;
Our refuges frighten the Fiend—we have made them too
dull for the Devil.
T ie Pageant of Life we avoid, and we know not why
Youth yearns to watch it.
Why, all sorts of people go by ! How we wish we had
power to scotch it!
N it a youth ’neath the stars would be found if tve could
arrange it, dear brother !

“Exal”: My experience tallies with that
of “ Crass ” (B. 30/3/11). I have known heaps of
Bush Australians who were very accurae . > -
shots with a 22-calibre rifle—the pea-rifle >
frequent paragraph, in fact; though the resig<
tion is applied in the gun-trade to quite a i ei
type of weapon. But I have never known
bushie who could shoot worth a cent at over
yards. The fault lies not in the young Australian
eye, which is sharper than most, nor in the >oun„
Australian hand, which is steady as. ant, u m
the fact that the marksman’s shooting has been
exclusively with a light rifle at a range which does
not exact from him any acquaintance with e ova-
tion, lift, drift, jump, flip, mirage or windage a
of which have to be reckoned with in long-iange
shooting. Said one chap to me, when I spoke ot
a rifle sighted to 3000 yards: “\\ hy, mister,
you’d want a bloomin’ cannon for that distance .
Of course, it may be said that, at his own distance
and with his accustomed weapon, the bushman
would give a good account of the foe. But to this it
has to be answered that, on active service, his light
rifle would soon go to pieces, and his short range
would be of no use to him at the “ long-bowls ot
the modern military rifle.

Mount Gram Mer, sitting on the lid of a
reputedly extinct volcano, down where South
Australia’s S.E. joins on to Victoria’s S.W., is
getting thoughtful. The Blue Lake, lying in one
reputedly-extinct crater, is said to be 12ft. higher
than when the waterworks were founded in 1883.
It was within 18in. or so of its present mark at
the beginning of this century, but then; subsided.
It has now come on 9ft. in the last three years,
and inhabitants want to know just what it means
and where it intends to stop.
SOMETHING IN TILE OEFING.

The staid Melbourne Athenaeum (not to
be confounded with the Athenaeum Club) is erupt-
ing in a card and smoking room. Old and tried
members shake their grey locks solemnly, and
wonder what the world is coming to. That the
old, stodgy Melbourne Athenaeum should thus
break out is like another Fall. It has kept to
the traditions of “ the East,” where nothing
changes. The staff lives and dies in
the hoary service. Members themselves
hang on like barnacles. To-day you
will see faces there that were poring
over papers 30 years ago, sitting in
the same chairs, at the same table,
coming in every day at the same
hour, and leaving according to
the clock. Nobody ever gets to
know his fellow member. Still,
there used to be a sort of
nexus. For many years there has
been knocking about the reading-
room a queer old Suggestion Book
—long since disused—whose birth
was contemporaneous with the
christening of the institution. The
contributors thereto evidently took
themselves as seriously as Judkins
in flippant vein. About 30 years
ago a fierce battle raged within its
pages—Should the wicked “Ouida”
be admitted to the library shelves?
The then Wowsers, sad to say,
snatched a temporary victory ; but,
in the end, the innoeuons creator
of Seraph and Lord Bertie came
out triumphant. Years ago, before
the trees grew large and blocked
the view of the Akgus hoardings,
the reading-room used to lie rushed
on election nights. But even that
excitement has ended. Still, after
all, there are few better institu-
tions of its kind than the Mel-
bourne Athenaeum both in number and variety of
papers and magazines and in the extent of the
library. The shelves of the latter hold a number
of rare and valuable first editions, not to be dupli-
cated in the Commonwealth, a fact due to the per :
sonuel and scholarly standing of early members.
The old manslaughter-risking rule of the
Sydney ferry cos., that a skipper on the river
services must hand over his steering wheel to a
deckhand while he collects tickets round the
decks, was given another bad advertisement the
other day at the Water Court. According to the
evidence a sailing boat with children on board
was crossing the Parramatta River, when the
steamer Bronzewing was seen tearing down at full
speed. Having the right of way, the sailing boat
kept on until a collision looked certain, when the
boat owner let everything go, and the steamer,
without altering her course, rushed past, with
only 2ft. to spare between them. It was admitted
that those on the Bronzewing did not see the
sailing boat. The master didn’t set up the de-
fence that he had to go selling tickets instead of
looking after iiis job, but argued that, as lie was
on the lower deck when the sail boat dodged col-
lision, he wasn’t legally liable. The P.M. decided
that he was liable for not getting out of the way,
and fined him £5. In cases like this the ferry qo.,
which, despite the expostulations of the Harbor
Trust and masters themselves, requires its skip-
pers to act as collectors of fares, is never, fined a
red cent. Even if an accident happened, and 50
lives were lost, it doesn’t appear as though even
one director would get 10 years for manslaughter,
for the encouragement of the others.
“ Sardonyx ” : One thing
appears to be carefully ignored
by the women who so persist-
ently shriek about what they
call the “rank injustice” of
paying women less than men
for similar work. If it be m t
(and this the complainants
strenuously deny) that the
woman’s woilt is worse done
than the man’s, then it must
be that women themselves
keep the rates down by accept-
ing less than a man will take.
Less, indeed, than a man can
take, because he has generally
a family depending upon him,
whereas the woman has seldom
anybody to consider but her-
self. When a woman is offered
smaller pay than would be
offered a man for the same
work, the only possible reason
—supposing the woman to be as
efficient as the man —is that
other women are ready to snap
the job at the same or a lower
figure. Nutshelled, the situation
therefore amounts to this. Woman cuts down her
own wage-standard, and then reviles Man because
he won’t—or, more properly, can t —come down
to her figure.

While the Sydney dailies were splashing
black head-lines across their sheets to this effect :
“Great Shortage of Labor,” “The Difficulties
of Employers,” Sehvyeppes, Ltd., were advertising
in the S. M. Herald notifying “the numerous

“01eo”:The little Art world of Melbourne
is buzzing over what is considered a breach of good
taste on the part of a visiting painter. At his own
“party-night” he was criticising his own work,
and confessed that, viewing it where it was hung
in the gallery, it certainly did make the surround-
ing pictures look “purposeless.” The remark, no
doubt, sounds painfully egotistical ; but, then,
isn’t Egotism the soul of Art ? And, anyway,
those who condemn the man should not overlook
the other side of the question. The work had
been ordered seven years ago; and the fact that the
artist waited so long to execute the commission
speaks volumes for his sincerity and his desire to
do his best. If lie had waited 20 years, sketching,
planning, destroying and creating, so much the
better for his production. However, the people
in authority chose to view his delay as “in-
civility and inattention ’’ —asthougb the artist is to
bejudged according to the standards for the butcher
—and the work was not even acknowledged. The
picture is a big thing, from every point of view
(competent critics claim that it is one of the finest
representations of Tragedy in the world); and the
attitude of the Gallery trustees has in it something
of the sulky schoolboy. Under these circum-
stances, is a man blamable because he too
recognises that he has produced a good thing,
and demands for it the respect it warrants?
True, when one recognises the value of one’s own
work, one doesn’t necessarily have to cry that the
other fellow’s work is no good ; but the idea in
the man’s mind evidently was, not that the other
artists had painted purposeless pictures, but that
the Gallery had selected them and, presumably,
rejoiced over them.

Justice Gordon, of S. Aus., talked some
common-sense in the Criminal Court at Adelaide
lately. The accused men had been disturbed at
the door of a barber’s shop at 10.30 p.m. The
padlock was smashed, but they had not got in
when the police arrived; so counsel calmly
argued that there was no evidence of breaking in,
or even of intent to enter with a felonious pur-
pose. He showed this to be the law, as decided
by an English Judge, Alderson. Then it was
that Gordon got out the axe. “Lord Alderson
was a great Judge,” he said, “but the criminal
law has made a few strides since 1844”; and
—after restoratives had been applied to the Bar—
he gave a formal ruling, against which they could
appeal if they cared to : “That, if it were proved
that a man other than the owner or occupier were
DISQUALIFIED.
HER: “I suppose, you think yourself an accom-
plished flirt l ”
HIM: “I used to until one of the girls I
thought I was flirting with married me."
seen to break the lock of a warehouse door
between 10 and 11 p.m., evidence was afforded
by which the jury might infer that he intended to
enter the premises with intent to steal.” That
sounds like sarcasm, but it isn’t. It is a mere
statement of what is the law now, but apparently
was not the law till Gordon used his axe. The
nocturnal padlock-fraeturer can still explain that
he was merely breaking the claw of a crayfish for
supper, or trying to wake the man next door ; but
he will have to give some explanation. Mean-
while, it is officially decided that Baton Aldeison
(1844) is Dead.
TRUE LOVE AND RABBITS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Two hawkers were charged with the novel offence of
conveying rabbits through the streets “ unprotected
from dust, flies, or the rays of the sun.” . . . Fined 10s.
—yews item.
He was a handsome Rabbit-O,
His voice was neither loud nor raucous:
He sang his rabbits, sweet and low,
And never talked about the Caucus.
She was a frump whom Love had shiiked,
Her dad a man of Lib’ral habits,
Who hated ev’ryone that worked,
Including those who vended rabbits.
The Rabbit-0 had dreams of ease ;
The Lib’ral’s girl had dreams of loving ;
Her roving eye he seemed to please
When rabbits round the suburb shoving.
She marked him down (he did not know),
And shuddered when, with curses hearty,
Her breathless sire (of Fat and Co.)
With vigor d d the Labor party.
She watched her love as he wheeled by,
Nor cared a jot that he was shabby ;
She trembled when she heard him cry,
Like poor and homeless Love, “ IF ild Tabby ! ’
She felt that she must meet him soon.
But how ? ’Twas still her wits defying
When, walking out one afternoon,
Somewhere about she heard him crying.
She hurried down one dusty lane,
Then up another, long and narrow,
And found—it caused her grief and pain—
Her loved one cursing by his barrow.
“ What is the matter, dear t " she cried.
He jumped—that humble rabbit-shover ;
Then with his hat he tried to hide
Three pairs. “’Ere! Get these under cover ! "
“ It’s unpertected food ! ” he gasped,
“ An’ there’s th’ blanky sergeant, blast it !”
The Lib’ral maid her veil unclasped,
And o’er the sunbaked rabbits cast it.
The rabbit merchant nearly wept
(lie was a man of frugal habits),
When to his side she proudly stept,
And held her sunshade o'er the rabbits.
Arid so they stood when on his way
The sergeant paused and grimly eyed them,
Then passed. They told their love that day,
The six veiled rabbits prone beside them.
They went and told her dad of it,
And asked him meekly for his blessing.
Without delay he had a fit.
They left. The sight was too distressing.
Next day they wed. Dad took the blow,
Condemning in a manner hearty
True love, his girl, the ltabbit-0,
All rabbits and the Labor Party.
N.S.W.

The S. Aus. branch of the Geographical
Society is urging that all available evidence relating
to the possible fate of Leichhardt in 1848 should be
garnered while natives who were about then are still
living. Warden Lionel Gee, on his visit to Tanami
goldfield, discussed the question with many bushmen,
including “Jimmy” Cullen, who had got from old
blackfellows a yarn about a white party with “bul-
locks which stood on their hind legs and ate the
bushes.” That sounds remarkably like the goats
which Leichhardt admittedly had, and the Geographi-
cal Society thinks that the final tragedy occurred
somewhere south of the Victoria River. Carnegie’s
party in W.A. found an old-fashioned iron peg close
to the S.A. border. How does it strike “Abo.”
readers in those parts?

“The Ancient” : Some fish stories, per-
sonally vouched for: —
Years ago I once went to Broken Bay with Liv-
ingston Hopkins and William Macleod, and while
drifting for flathead towards Great Mackerel Beach
in Bill Bolton’s boat, my line was broken by a
heavy fish. We landed on the beach for lunch, and
after staying an hour or so re-crossed the bay and
resumed drifting. At the first lower I caught a big
flathead, which had in its jaw the identical wire-
mounted hook and improvised sinker that I had
lost before lunch. To the truth of this yarn there
are still four witnesses, all at least partially alive.
What are the odds against such a thing occurring?
How often has it happened since the stars were hung
out in Heaven? Bill Bolton, who is still on deck,
not long ago told it at Newport to yours truly
(whom he did not at the time recognise) as a per-
sonal experience.
My excuse for starting fish stories is that I notice
with regret that the old Bulletin office,, a holy place
to me, is now a fishing-tackle shop—which will lead
to scoffers remarking that the place always was
a resort of liars.
Walking down Queen-street, Brisbane, one day
last year, I entered Bond’s ironmongery with Jack
Hamilton, an old friend of far-northern days. While
Jack stood at the counter inspecting a new auto-
matic pistol,, there entered a long man, thin as a
shadow turned sideways, with: “I say, mister,
gimme Some schnapper hooks.” The shopman
reached down a box and handed out a sample, the
shank of which the customer put between his teeth
and calmly bit through. “Them’s too soft,” said he
gently, as he deposited the pieces on the counter.
“Let us try them bright 6 O’s.” The bright No.
6’s were produced, and shared the same fate —the
first one taken out of the box was bitten in halves
by the human leather-jacket, who departed without
making a purchase. Three living witnesses to that!
Not long ago a 14ft. 6in. tiger-shark was caught
in Port Hacking, with jaws wide enough to admit
the broadest human. (Jem Mann, the burly old-
time Melbourne oarsman, measured his shoulders
with the monster’s mouth.) In that shark’s stomach
were found a mop, a piece of soap, an unopened tin
of “bully” beef, made shiny by the big fish’s digest-
ive process, the shoulder-half of a side of bacon
and a coil of woman’s hair. The beef, when opened,
was found to be perfectly good. The Sydney Meat
Preserving Cb. probably never had a better testi-
monial.
“Mongailu” stands the responsibility for
this :
Apparently ‘‘Snooker’s” disbelief of “Conda-
mine’s” statement that 2000 rabbits can exist and
thrive for seven months on a rocky, sandy hill-top
is based merely on the ground that nothing is cor-
rect unless seen with one’s own eyes. Well, I
haven’t seen “Condamine’s” hill-top, but I believe
his statement, and support it witli another that, not
for seven months only, but for double that number
of years, rabbits have existed and thrived on a
small speck in mid-ocean called Nepean Island (not
far distant from Norfolk Island), where there is no
water, and where grass is a scarce commodity. Here
bunnv exists, thrives, and apparently enjoys life to
its fullest extent. In order to sustain that life the
animals apparently dine on one another.
THE A WEAL TO MU JIM.
The Health Faddist: “AW’, then, he a man, and give up tea ."
“Fore and Aft” spins a yarn or some-
thing:—
The curse of “The Flying Dutchman” is still upon
the seas, despite the allegation that when a piece of
the Cross was put aboard the infernal ship she
crumbled into dust. Maybe; but Vanderdecken is
still at large! I met him first when I was second on
one of the Gold Flag boats, running out of Liver-
pool to Capetown. My introduction to Van occurred
one night on the bridge where he was standing his
wheel and muttering to himself. For a while I took
no notice, but suddenly he cried aloud: “Mein Gott,
we’ll nefer round der Cape,” and spun the wheel hard
over. I forget what I said to him, but I remember
punctuating the remarks with a handspike. Down
over the wheel he cowered, whimpering, “It is no
goot-—no goot; der Cape” “Shut up, you
Dutch swine,” I roared, thoroughly enraged, and
swiped at his head. I saw the skin split open along
his forehead —but no blood flowed. It scared me bad.
and at 4 a.m., when I was relieved by the chief, I
told him of the incident. lie said, “Try a tonic;
it’s better than whisky 1” I let it go at that, but I
left the ship at Capetown. Six months later she
was lost on Roast Beef Island, off German South-
West Africa. Then came my next meeting with the
Curse —this time along the front of Darling Harbor,
Sydney. I grabbed him as he was passing, and said:
“See here, you, were you on the when she went
ashore?” “No,” he answered; “it is no goot—no
goot; nefer double der Cape.” He told me he had
crossed from London in an Orient boat and was going
back rated as an A.B. on the Waratah. That was
her last trip, and I believe Vanderdecken went with
her, just as I believe he was on the other boat.
Nevertheless, I sau> him again. He was moving
along the shipping of a big Maoriland port, looking
haggard and old. I called him, and he turned and
laughed like a skull rolling in a coffin. “Nefer
round dot Cape,” he said; “it’s no goot.” I
watched him disappear amongst the mounds of
cargo, and a chill of regret swept over me that I had
not killed him with the marline spike when I first
met him. Perhaps it was impossible.

“D. 5.,” from Pinnaroo (S.A.) :
Few people have any idea of what is being done
in the S. Aus. mallee country, situated between the
Murray and the Victorian border, or of the fortunes
that are being founded: The district was opened up
about five years ago by the construction of a light
railway, beginning near Tailein Bend, on the Ade-
laide-Melbourne line, and running easterly to within
three and a half miles of the border. Some of the
poorest men who selected (and in many cases they
were very poor) can now count their thousands.
Many who arrived with swags are already enquiring
about the price of motor-cars.
The land itself from Wow Wow Plains (now
Lameroo) on to the border and beyond, is good
wheat-growing country, easily cleared, and for the
first couple of seasons does not require ploughing;
a disc cultivator does all the tickling that is neces-
sary, the soil being loose, with a red clay sub-soil
to retain the moisture.
Among the lucky landholders are “Larry” O’Lough-
lin, an ex-State Minister, who has 10,000 acres, and
R. W. Foster, M.H.R., with 4000 acres. At Mur-
rayville, 17 miles east of Pinnaroo, some quarter-
acre township blocks were sold by auction last June
and brought up to £l6O, or £640 per acre. And
this is only one of the townships which serve what
was looked on as desert until a few years ago. The
man of great faith who battled hardest for the rail-
way and the opening up of the big district was
James Byrnes, wljo may be said to have blazed the
track into Pinnaroo. He won’t get a monument in
stone and brass. The great and growing stacks of
wheat in the station-yards and at the sidings suit
his style of beauty a lot better.
“Silas Snell”: T was alluvial mining on
a small Tasmanian field, and -my nearest neighbor
was Ned Micken. Micken was the most melancholy
man 1 have ever known. He was stoking at one
of the big mines, and lived alone in a rough bark
hut. Micken was lugubrious about everything, espe-
cially about the weather, his boots, and his meals.
His footwear and his food always disagreed with
him. But he had a romantic explanation for his
woe. One day, after sitting in my hut for four
hours, indulging in a fit of hideous depression, he
said: “I wasn’t always like this, you know. I’ve
bin crossed in love. Three weeks come Sundee Mrs.
Carrol refused to marry me.” A few days later I
noticed that Micken was carrying a thicker atmo-
sphere of gloom than usual. He put his head in at
the door as i was at tea, and said: “Bin crossed in
love agin. Miss Stevens.” Then he groaned hor-
ribly, and passed on. Within a fortnight Micken
was sunk in blackest woe. I got it through the
window this time. “Crossed in love agin. Miss
Carver.” In the course of three months Micken
was crossed in love seven times, and each misfortune
seemed to deepen his harrowing, sorrow-logged con-
dition. Then came the Sunday when yells of con-
sternation drew a presssure of population to Micken’s
hut. The melancholy man had been found hanging
by the neck. He was cut down, and prompt and
energetic treatment restored him to his miseries. His
first words on recovering speech were—“ Mrs. Dun-
stan’s agreed to marry me!” Thus cruelly robbed
of his pet sorrow, he had decided that life wasn’t
worth living.
* * * *
“.Tack Jay” : Don’t know anything about
rabbits being able to live without food or water,
but how’s this for a cat? The big steamer Kaik-
oura, bound from Capetown to Maoriland, missed
its Persian tabby on the day of sailing (11/2/’ll),
and only discovered her on arrival, 29 days later.
She had got into the powder magazine, and must
have been without food or drink all the time.
There was no sign of rat near her, and she did not
appear to have- eaten any of the explosives. Still
she was alive, though not exactly lively, and is
now doing well.
“Wang”: I am with “Palumpas” heart
and soul, pen and inkbottle, when he puts in a word
for the unfortunate State children grafting on
dairy farms. There is no exaggeration in his
statement that he met a boy of 11 on his way to
school after he (the boy) had extracted fluid from 19
cows that morning. Take my own case. I was
a State kid. I left school when I was 12 years old,
and a watching cow-cockv grabbed me for his own.
Twice a day, regularly, I had to milk a dozen cows
(and many a time I have had to milk the whole 22
in the boss’s herd); feed and yoke up the neddy;
load up the milk, and cart it off three miles to the
creamery, besides indulging in such pastimes as
lighting the fire, feeding the fowls, etc. , and nar\
a bite of breakfast till I had returned home from
the creamery. I will swear an affidavit to this any
day, and, further, will take any doubting reader to
the doorstep of the slave-driver who owned me —a
poor, helpless, friendless kid —if he likes to pay my
expenses.
OUT OF IT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When I hear of collisions and earthquakes and things
That make folks unhappy in all sorts of places,
When I read of the scamps that assassinate kings,
Or go to the dogs with the geegee that races ,
When I’m told of adventures diverting and sweet
That fall to some fellows right here in my street,
Or of fortunes that come to plump deans in old
minsters
Because they’ve been patient with snappy old
spinsters,
I am always surprised beyond measure, you see,
Because such things, somehow, don’t happen to me.
There’s Boggles, repentant, who went to the fold,
And became a good Methodist till the fit left him,
But ere he relapsed wooed a widow with gold
In an excellent heap, which (soon dying) she left
him ;
There’s Winkle, who saved from a horrible smash
A damsel delicious, although she’d no cash ;
There was Bungle, poor devil, who, dull as an ox,
eyed
A stranger, and kissed her, and died of peroxide.
Perhaps it’s as well, as I think you’ll agree,
That things of that kind never happen to me.
Then there’s Slinker, a bounder, who bounds day
and night,
Who was nabbed by the police, but got big com-
pensation
(Although he’d committed the arson all right) ;
And there’s Spoof, who gets fat by misleading a
nation ;
And as to fat Boodle —well, that makes me sick,
For he purchased a wild-cat, and still was so slick
That before you could drown a small litter of
poodles
He’d made twenty thousand by rooking the
noodles —
It seems dreadfvdly hard, it’s as hard as can be,
But things of that sort never happen to me.
Ah, well, on the whole, I’m not greatly cast down,
For there’s Bleary, who married a shrew, who so
oddly
Has cowed him to death with her terrible frown
That he’s had to stop smoking and tried to live
godly; .
There’s Clutterbuck, too, who once wrote dainty
verse,
Was gored by a bull and removed in a hearse;
While as for old Skippit, who used to cut capers,
He’s called “a great man” in respectable papers—
So I chuckle and chortle with natural glee,
For I’m glad that such things never happen to me.
MAORILAND.
FRANK MOUTON.

“Dunno”: “Pick” (B. 2/3/T1) asks if
any bloke, fellow or cow can better the view that
may be obtained from Stony Cutting, at the top of
Brown Mountains, looking down the Bemboka Val-
ley (N.S.W.). I can try. You cross an aged,
heavilv-built wooden bridge; and having paid tne
customary toll to the ancient hermit on the hill-side
shanty, stray as your mood inclines you, either along
the gold-dappled crest above, or down amidst the
deep-green shadows near the water’s edge. Reclin-
ing here in the cool on a still summer’s day, through
the heavy festoons of willow foliage that listlessly
caressed the limpid river’s surface, I have seen into
the shaded interior of the old open-built distillery
on the far bank. Occasionally across the smooth ex-
panse of shimmering blue there drifted a breath of
Bacchanalian fragrance, which but served to move
the senses to a keener appreciation of this sylvan
oasis, far from the heat and glare of the outer world.
Nor were these realms left unknown and unappreci-
ated by that world-wearied bard (now resting) whose
song has since endeared him in the hearts of those
Australians who can feel; and more than one sad-
souled, childish dreamer of the slums has here found
a dreamland of rest and romance: for strangely
enough it is situated in that unlovely environment
known as Collingwood, sometimes called by the name
of its rarer atmosphere—Abbotsford. The old bridge
has long since gone the way of all things; but
through a tangle of lank, straggling weeds, masses of
purple-splashed creeper and a frail forest of green
and silver saplings of later birth, the remains of the
old distillery still stand, its cool, lengthy lofts, and
the deep silence of its empty vats, invaded only by
the scented breezes imparted from its old friend the
hill. And now, ascend those wattle-flamed heights
and gaze into the opposite distance. Far below,
the river reappears—a winding, green-fledgtd ribbon
of blue and silver. From its further banks spread
the wide and peacefully picturesque fields and gardens
of the convent; and often from here, in the rose-
tinted twilight Oh! away, ye sad-eyed ghosts 1
Bring me a beer!

“Condot”: A semi-official effort is being
made to ascertain the heights of tall trees in Aus-
tralia. Prof. Ewart, Victorian Government Botan-
ist, has received from H. J. Elwes, F.R.S., Chelten-
ham (Eng.), a letter asking for the information. I
believe the tallest tree yet measured in Vic. is, or
was, 302 ft. high. The late Prof. Kernot measured
it. As it grew on the edge of a ridge—not the
most favorable spot for development—-probably trees
considerably taller may be found. The tallest
tree recorded on good authority in America is a se-
quoia sempervirens, which measured 340 ft. in 1896.
It is said that, on the slopes of the Baw Baw (V.)
ranges, there are many blackbutt trees 300 ft. high,
but this is guesswork. In Beech Forest, too, there are
hundreds of giants. A few years ago a six-roomed
hotel was built of the timber cut from one of these
vegetables. Another yielded 9000 six-foot palings.
When the racecourse was made at Beech Forest, the
stump of what must have been an enormous tree
was cut level and floored, and used as a grandstand.
The hail-fellow-well-met Marquis of Linlithgow,
when G.-G. of Australia, visited the forest township,
and on this grandstand danced in a set with the
wife of Jack Gardiner, the oldest inhabitant.

“Stan”: The swagmen in Queensland,
when “J. 5.8.” (B. 9/2/’ll) was on the road, must
have been mighty men if they could hump a 7ft.
matilda around. The traveller nowadays declines
anything more than a light bundle, generally con-
taining a single blanket (which is often shed with
the arrival of summer), a few changes, mosquito net
and small miscellany. Round this is wrapped the
tent and fly—not always in company—the whole lot
not exceeding 3ft. in length. Bind the lady up with
three straps—even two will do —pass a tomahawk
through the straps, and she is ready for the track.
That is the swag of Queensland.

“Sliprail”: Re snake-bite. The “mind
over matter cure,” taken in conjunction with either
strychnine, chloride of lime, or the permanganate
cure, will generally prevent a man from being
counted out. The trouble is that people make up
their minds they are done for, and the snake gets
a long handicap. Here is one instance of the
powerful antidote the mind providesA local
farmer was bitten by a snake while shepherding.
He drove the jumbucks home, paddocked them, tied
his dog up, and, as tea was on, took his place at
the head of the table, informing his family, as a
sort of grace before meat, that he had been bitten
on the leg by a snake. He absolutely refused to
be bustled. “Might be the last ifeed I will ever
get, so I might as well have a good one,” he philo-
sophically remarked. After tea he went out, oiled
the buggy, and took the horse to the dam for a
drink preparatory to driving 12 miles to town. His
wife tried to get him straight to the doctor’s sur-
gery, but he insisted on “shouting” for a couple
of friends, reminding the half-frantic woman that
it might be his last chance of doing so. The doctor
applied the usual remedies, and advised him not to
go to sleep, but the casual coolly informed the medi-
cal man that he had not been out of bed after 10
o’clock for 40 years, and that he didn’t intend to
start altering his habits because he had happened
to be bitten by a reptile. Next morning he was
as right as pie. Moral: Once bitten, keep spry.
