The S. Aus. Ornithological Association
: lias turned out several pairs of mallee fowl (Leipoa
ocellata) on Kangaroo Island, in the great national
reserve at Cape Borda. The mound-raising bird is
being rapidly wiped out by foxes on the mainland,
and, as no other continent owns him, it is thought
that the island is his only chance of being seen by
posterity. The Government and the Marine Board
helped in the good work.

“Vagabond”: “Bash” is barking up the
wrong gum when he laughs the loud ha-ha of scorn
at the carters and milk-ohs who decorate their gee-
gee’s topknots with sun-hats (B. 16/3/’ll). If you
caught one of the gents, mentioned and held him
down while you read him “Bash’s” remarks about
“circulatory congestion,” and “evaporation of per-
spiration,” and “blocking radiation,” and then re-
vived him with a long beer and got him confidential,
he would explain that he would really as soon expect
his four-legged friend to develop housemaid’s knee
as sunstroke. Possibly he puts the horse-umbrella
up because it is fashionable nowadays—as an orna-
ment, it is rather classy and looks well with the
brass plates and things he spends so much spare time
in polishing up. But more likely he does it for the
same good reason that the brainy boss of a big
Yankee store paid attention to the poo-er dumb
animals’ comfort —to get on the soft side of his
women and old-women men customers. It wouldn’t
surprise me a ha’porth if the milk- and bottle-oh
blokes had found that the ladies preferred to give
encouragement, and orders, and bottles to the kind
man who was so thoughtful for his poor horse, in-
stead of to that callous ruffian who didn’t care if the
sun was hot enough to fry his nag’s head to a
fricassee, or heat up his brain-box till the inside
melted and bubbled over and ran out of his nose.
“Jonnel” : The Mount Morgan system of
“chewing” or “running the cutter” is, as far as I can
ascertain, unique in Australia. A party or syndi-
cate of. miners on their way home from toil take
turn about to provide the nimble sprat; a billy-can
is cleaned of tea tannin at a convenient trough, and,
in return for the sixpence, is filled by Mr. Public
House. The syndicate does the rest. Old hands carry
a cheese-rind in their crib bags, and carefully and
knowingly “cheese” round William Can’s top to the
depth of an inch or more, some wily soaker in the
dim past having discovered that cheese prevents the
useless and wasteful “head” rising. It beats me how
a publican can afford to fill some of the oil-drums
that are passed over as billies, but woe to the one
who discriminates. By the way, can anybody give
the origin-of the saying, “Running the cutter”?

“Johnnie” : While going to boil the billy
near Stonehenge (N.S.W.) last week, I happened to
see a fine specimen of the tiger variety of snake get-
ting into his happy home. Proceeded to burst the
home with a shovel. After despatching father, I
spotted ma’s tail. A few shovelfulls more, and I had
sent her the same way. My Sherlock Holmes sus-
picions being aroused, I dissected the lady, and
brought to light 38 baby snakes from nine to 12
inches long. So I finished 40 “tigers” in five
minutes —and it was a fortnight from the last pub,
too.
“Yalgun”: It happened this way, but
quicker. Fencer on Orara River (N.S.W.) split-
ting ironbark rails. Lifted one. Dropped it.
Yelled, “ Hell’s bellows. Black snake bit me
finger.” Seized axe, cut it off. Mate poured rum
down injured man’s funnel, and ran him up and
down for 1 hour 36 minutes. Declared him cured.
Went back to collect severed knuckle-bone. Found
the ironbark splinter embedded in the flesh. So
long.

“L.S.” (from the MeSolomon Islands) .
I’d go so far as to bet drinks that it was the Scotch
who discovered the Solomon Islands and not the
Spaniards. For one thing, the hraw strong accent
of the nigger bears me out;
for another, the niggers’ fav-
orite ' instrument bears a
marked resemblance to the
bagpipes. Anyhow, the is-
lands have improved the
Macs-; —made them brighter
and given them a taste for
humor in between whiskies.
There was, for instance, the
case of the irrepressible
McZ., a Resident Magistrate,
who looked on the wine irre-
spective of color. When
H.M.S. Cambrian called to
take Mac to another island
to conduct an enquiry, the
commander instructed him to
be on board at 8 sharp. Mac
turned up at 8.10. The com-
mander, who was furious,
greeted him with: “Mr.
MacZ, there is one man in
the world can keep this ship
waiting—his Majesty King
Edward.” MacZ placed his
hand over his heart, blushed
modestly, and made a deep
bow. “Sir,” he said, “you
flatter me.” I am inclined to
think the natives will even-
tually become Presbyterian,
judging by the reception
given lately to a Wusslean
missionary, who started in
to convert them. The Rev.
Wire Whiskers, at the head
of his mission boys, descend-
ed upon the devil-devil house,
and marched round and
round singing hymns. In re-
taliation, the niggers sur-
rounded the chapel next time
the Rev. W. Whiskers was
praying for the conversion of
the heathen into good Ker-
istians, with the tray-bit
habit well developed, and
deafened the audience by
lambasting empty kerosene
cans and other unmusical in-
struments. The parson and
his congregation tried to sing
the row down, but after 25
minutes’ battle the towel was
thrown in, and the niggers,
who hadn’t worked so hard
since they chased the last
Wesleyan missionary over 10
miles o f rough country,
crowned the victory with a
feast. The Rev. Whiskers
wasn’t on the menu, but a few
days later he was urged to scoot if he didn’t want to
follow the soup next time. So he scot. That brings
me to this point—Were the Scotch people cannibals
at any time in past history ? I’m a long way off,
but I would be much obliged for reliable informa-
tion on this matter.
DROVER’S GIRL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She smiles a brave farewell, though her lips refuse
to speak;
He feels her small hand tremble as he holds it
tight;
lie sees the color fade from her sun-browned cheek
As he speaks about his journey in the morning-
light.
Oh ! the drover
Is a rover
The wide land over
On the highways and byways that a bushman
knows,
And his line of least resistance
Leads afar into the distance. . . .
But there’s someone’s heart will follow him
where’er he goes!
By the little wayside towns and the shanties lone,
Across the ’lignum swamps and the dried-up
creeks,
Through miles of dreary scrub—where the sheoaks
moan,
From night-camp to night-camp througli long,
long weeks
He is shifting,
And drifting
Through the dust-clouds lifting—
Oh! the drover is a sailor and the bush his sea,
And, in port a lass is waiting
With a faith all unabating,
“But the boldest have gone down in a gale! ’’
says she.
She gets a letter (written by a flickering light),
She reads it in the twilight as she walks alone;
And once more her cheeks are flushed and her eyes
are bright
As she reads about a cottage “when the droving’s
done.”
Oil! the drover
Is a rover
The wide land over!
But he’s dreaming of someone where the star
lamps burn;
And her sky is growing clearer ;
For each night-camp finds him nearer—
God speed her drover-lover to a safe return!
N.S.W.

A prophet hath honor in his own country
—if he has a good press agent. Thus on Tait’s
return to Canada, after the Sunshine and Ricli-
m jnd catastrophes, this scream has been travelling
r mnd :
During the period of his engagement in Victoria he
placed the railway system on a sound and assured hasis,
and he leaves it in a condition unexcelled by that of any
system elsewhere. What he achieved, in the face of many
difficulties occasioned hy the near-sighted and slipshod
p >licv pursued before his arrival, is abundantly acknow-
-1 :dged by the authorities of Victoria.
In fact, Tait’s policy was so far-sighted that
already the railways not only find themselves
utterly unable to handle the traffic by reason of
the starved condition of the rolling stock, but declare
that the shortage is so tremendous that it will be
impossible to supply the requirements from local
works in time to save huge waste and loss.
GEORGE FOWLDS, MINISTER OF
EDUCATION (ML.).
James Gear, tlie meat man, who died at
Wellington (M.L.)'recently, was one of the old
adventurers who rushed to the Gabriel’s Gully
(Otago) goldfields from Australia in 18G1. He
arrived in Melbourne from the Old Dart in 1857,
and worked as a butcher till a few years later the
gold fever sent him across the Tasman. At
Gabriel’s he had no luck, so he made his way to
Wellington, where he returned to the dead-
carcase trade, and worked up one of the biggest
businesses in the quaking islands—the < ear Meat
Preserving and Freezing Co., Ltd. He was 75
when Death called round with his order.
Leaving Sydney l»y the Mooltan, on May
6, for a trip to London. John Jack Leete, the
manager of the Tivoli Theatre (Sydney). Over
20 years ago Harry Rickards imported him from
Egypt, where he had been having his fill of ad-
venture and damaging his health. As a boy
Leete went from Britain to Egypt, thence
to Zinzibar, and back again to Egypt, where
he entered the railway service
and rose, in 1881, to be travel-
ling inspector. In the 1882
Egyptian war he was attached
to Lord Charles Beresford at
Alexandria as interpreter, and
afterwards was sent to Ismalia
to take charge of the loco, de-
partment under Major Hollis,
of the Royal Engineers, then
director of railways. Leete
says that it was Commander
Maude, of H.M.S. Temeraire,
and not Fisher (afterwards
First Sea Lord), who originated
the armored train. Maude
suggested that Leete should fix
up a train that would carry a
gun an 1 protect its crew, and
Leete said lie could do his
['art if Maude could overcome
the recoil of the gun. Maude
replied that there was no diffi-
culty in that, and on reaching
headquarters reported the pro-
posal to Captain John Fisher. Next
day Maude was transferred to the
Mediterranean, and Fisher came to the
workshops and had the ironclad put in hand at
once.

crest, if George V. creates him a baronet, as is sus-
pected : “A loan I did it! ”
A Melbourne bookmaker preserves the
skull of the convict Maloney, hanged in Tasmania
after committing countless crimes, and buried in
the old Port Arthur cemetery.
Died lately, in London, H. J. Thompson,
an Australian-born journalist who was among the
first of the tribe to move into the big city of the
Fog, where for some years he represented The
Bulletin.
A suit for slander and assault which a
“ Liberal ” organiser brought the other day had a
diverting finish. The official assignee stepped in
and garnisheed the damages. So the opponent of
lawlessness and confiscation merely shed the gore
of his nose on behalf of ravening creditors.
The Ayrshire (Scotch) Miners’ Union
will banquet Prime Minister Fisher during his
visit to George V.. in commemoration of the fight
he put up as a district secretary of the union
in a trade dispute. Fisher was afterwards black-
listed by the employers, and came to Australia to
look for a job. He got it.

The busy people who want to—presumably
—glorify God and advance Christianity by build-
ing a sky-raking spire on top of St.’ Thomas’s
Church, at North Sydney, mention the fact that
George, when out here in 1881 with his brother
princeling in the Bacchante, helped to lay the
foundation stone; wherefore the spire, to be
erected to the glory of God, would be a fitting
“kingly memorial”—and this paper won’t dis-
pute the statement. Really, a more interesting
thing about North Sydney’s St. Thomas’s is that
the Rev. W. B. Clarke, Australia’s first great
geologist, was its first minister—back in 1846.
Ararat (Vic.), the town which, years ago,
produced the gentle political dove, has lost its
most ancient identity, Janies Maclean. He
arrived from Ireland in ’57, and for nearly half a
century worked and worried as shire secretary.
Eventually he was deposed by old age, and
became numbered among the people with straight
official pasts. In lfis early youth he attended the
Monaghan Preparatory School, where Charles
Gavan Duffy was also learning a thing or two.
Joseph John Rochester, who died the
other day at Paddington (Sydney), was about the
last, bar Ted Belisario, of a remarkable group of
“business sports,” whose chief was the late
George Hill, owner of Marvel and other big
things, from pubs and theatres to coal and blue-
metal mines. In the days of Foley’s White
Horse Hotel and Boxing Hall, Rochester was a
handsome figure at the ringside, and frequently
acted as referee or timekeeper. As usual, how-
ever, he suffered for being a*“ good fellow,” and
when George Hill died and left things poorly, lie
who bad been the rich man’s factotum had to go
and hustle. And, on the whole, he was a niiglitv
poor hustler.
A. S. Meek, the modest man who knows
more about the birds, butterflies and beetles of
Psew Guinea and the South Sea Islands than any-
body else, left Thursday Island about 14 months
ago on his biggest and most dangerous expedi-
tion. He proposed to explore the Snow Moun-
tains of Dutch New Guinea and put it through
his naturalist’s net, covering grounds which only
one white party had ever got near. Not a word
had been heard of him for nine months until
Saturday, when he wired from Thursday Island:
“Left Merauke on the 6th ; arrived here 13th;
leaving for Moresby at end of month. Think the
trip has been a success. Besides meeting much
else of interest, found two new Birds of Para-
dise. Lost none of the party by accident, but
four ‘ boys ’ died from beri-beri. Received much
assistance from Dutch officials.” Presumably the
two new birds will go to enrich the collection of
Rothschild, who has first call on Meek’s “finds.”
Rev. T. H. Sprott, who is to wear the
next outfit of bishop’s millinery at Wellington
(M.L.), has been administering spiritual food in
the Dominion for 24 years past; so be ought by
this time to be a fairly good Maorilander. Any-
how, his election is a good sign of the decay of the
importing habit in a quite unexpected quarter.
Before he was a Maorilander he was an Irishman.

Edmondson, the “Tantanoola tiger,”
threw himself on the mercy of the S. Australian
court, arid was rewarded with six years for his
weird brand of sheep-stealing. Years ago, when
the “ tiger ” first began to worry local sheepmen
(there really was a wild beast of some sort in
those days) a pub at Tantanoola was christened
“The Tiger,” and Edmondson carved the sign
for it—a figure of the beast. He was sitting
under it when he was arrested.
Moberley Bell, of London Times, was a
brother-in-law of Senator Drinkwater Chataway, of
Bananaland, having married a daughter of the late
Rev. James Chataway. Drink water has contri-
buted an occasional prosy article to the Times.
The late J. Y. Chataway, his brother, who was
Minister for Agriculture in Bananaland’s Con-
tinuous Ministry, was a frequent writer for the
Times, and also ran the Sugar Journal in
Mackay. He was tin ardent advocate of black
labor, and the surviving Chataway is a small
piece saved from the wreck of the same party.
There were two proud men at Cockatoo
Island on the day the YVarrego was launched;
but neither of them got in the limelight. One
was Cutler, the dock superintendent. Everybody
wanted a word from him; but it was the day of
the talkers, not the doers. The other was Jack
Storey, State member for Balmain, who kept on
worrying the Federal Ministers until he per-
suaded them that the alleged “ impossible ” job
of building ships in Australia was quite possible.
Those were the two —the man who persuaded the
Government that the thing could be done, and the
man who did it.
Major Harry Tomkinson, just dead in
Adelaide, was a son of one of Adelaide’s best
known families. His father, ex-bank manager
and M.L.C., remained till the end of a long life a
hard-shell Conservative who wrote regularly to
the Register to say that the Time was not yet
Ripe, and was so inflexibly honest that he always
repudiated the humorous name of “Liberal ’
which like-minded politicians were just beginning
to assume. The Major was different: he was
educated in England, a pursuer of Costs, and in his
spare time A.D.C. to commandants Owen, Downes
and Gordon. He had a tremendous capacity for
meeting with horse and other acciden ts. The most
startling was when he woke one night in his rooms,
over a city shop, to find the place on fire. It had
smouldered a long while, and just as a few jugs
from the bathroom had extinguished it he trod on
a spot where the beams had burnt through, and
landed, a badly shaken man, in the Shop below.
Latterly he had been an invalid, and the end
came at 50.
Major George Wynne, of the Sydney
Daily Telegraph editorial dept., who departs
Cqronationwards with a contingent of State
military cadets, has done a good deal of roving in his
time. At the age of 13 he ducked off to sea aboard
an old wool clipper before liis father, Watkin
Wynne, could move for a habeas corpus writ. The
ship was a long, narrow and shallow contraption,
built originally for the Peruvian blockade-running
profession, and alleged to be one of the first success-
ful submarines. According to Wynne, she dived
under at Sydney Heads and returned to the sur-
face off the Lizard (Cornwall). Her captain was
one of the sea tyrants who helped to make Frank
Bullen’s life a misery at one period, and he didn’t
make any special exception in Wynne’s case.
“Get aloft,” he used to bellow, “and if y’ must
fall, fall overboard. I don’t want no blanky mess
on my decks.” After a spell in London, Wynne
shipped back to Australia and took up press work
with the old Star, and thence passed to the Tele-
graph. When the Boxer trouble started in China
he went out to quell it with the N. S. Wales naval
contingent, wrote some fine stuff for his paper,
and hammered at official doors so consistently
that Li Hung Chang had to climb out the back
window to avoid being interviewed twice a week
by “ Wynne of Australia.” Next, he joined the
Commonwealth Light Horse that helped Cohen-
stein force Chow labor on Boerland ; and now he
is going to introduce the other George to a sec-
tion of Australia’s infantile defence.

“ Monkbarns ” in the graveyard :
Jack Edwards, the Melbourne amateur actor, whose
memory has been revived by recent pars., excelled most
as Ollapod, in “The Poor Gentleman.” What a raev
comedy that was ! Jefferson acted Ollapod, but I don’t
believe his dry humor would be as racv as Jack’s unction.
Edwards may have died of Bohemianism. as alleged ; for
that was in the days of Aspinall and Dick Ireland. Bill
really he was assiduous in his legal biz. He would be
punctual at court to grapple with his favorite rivals, long
Jimmy McKean and Sam Lyons; while Gillott would
look in occasionally and Best was just beginning'. In
business, Jack was John Edw'ards the Younger.
His chief clerk was his father, John Edwards
the Elder, and a most reverend elder at that.
Attorneys nowadays are as good as or better than men
like Edwards, McKean and Lyons were ; but there is no
unction in court —all is as dry as the remainder biscuit
after a voyage. Jack’s other contemporaries, such as
Read and Frank Stephen (Chief Justice Madden’s dad-in-
law), had their peculiar unction. Magistrates now are
more precise and maiter-of fact; they won’t stand anv of
the comedian business. With Edwards and McKean on
the job the Collingwood Police Court became as funny
as Lord Chief Baron Nicholson’s Judge and Jury at the
London Coal-hole or the Cogers' Club.
The lute Jawbone Neild, an Englishman,
thought that his compatriots, unlike the Scotch and
the Irish, did not sufficiently cohere when abroad.
“I am English,” he said, “but no Englishman
will therefore vote for me. Were I Scotch
or Irish, 1 would get all the Scotch or all the Irish
vote.” Hence the departed colonel’s Society of
St. George, with its dinners, whereat, amid the
strains of inspiriting music, “ the Roast Beef of
Old England ” was ceremoniously borne aloft on
a lordly dish.
Captain Macarthur Onslow, of the 16th
Lancers, who has been appointed to the instruc-
tional staff of the Maoriland Defence Depart-
ment, is one of the Camden (N.S. W.) Onslows. He
was through the S’African campaign that was
fought for cheap Chow mine-labor—which turned
out too dear to keep—and got his captain’s stripe
or button, or whatever the implement is, in 1904.
The military spirit runs in the family. Col.
Onslow, M.L.A., is at present fighting in N. S.
Wales, shoulder to shoulder with Corporal Bruce
Smith and his A.D.C., Solemn Lee, to prevent
young Australia shooting himself with a double-
barrelled “Yes!”
Within about a month Sydney has lost its
two spikiest sets of face ornaments. Ex-Senator
Neild owned the better-known pair; he had
been cultivating his

tising purposes for
over quarter of a

The new “ batch ” of S. Australian K.C.’s
is curiously small. Since the death of Kingston.
Stuart and Baker there have been only six “silks ”
in the State—Syinon, Downer. Nesbit, Dash-
wood. Murray and Grundy. The new men are
A. W. Piper and J. R. Anderson ; and why the
powers have passed over Patrick Glynn, Federal
politician, inter-State pleader and literary expert,
is a dark mystery. Piper and Anderson are both
old State schoolboys who got into Prince Alfred
College with scholarships and worked their way
up. Both are now about 45, and have tried un-
successfully to get into Parliament. But there
the resemblance ends. “Jim” Anderson is
chairman of the Adelaide Racing Club and
of the S. A. Football League, and ex-partner
and son-in-law of Judge Gordon. All thieves
who can his fees afford rely on his orations.
Piper is of a different type, rather frequent in S.A
—son of a Methodist minister, and warm sup-
porter of all sports except racing. His law is all
on the Civil side. Although he is content to be
known as junior partner in the firm of Bakewell
and Stow, when the Perth City Council fell out
with the local gas co. over the question of pur-
chase-money, it was Piper who was sent for,
regardless of expense, to arbitrate. The differ-
ence between the two new K.C.’s is perhaps
quickest shown by this : Anderson is “Jim” on
everybody’s tongue, but men who know Piper
fairly well would be puzzled to tell you whether
he is Alfred or Abinadab.
THE LIBERAL CELLAR-MAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An occasion so great must awe the most light-hearted,
and the one consideration I must crave is that you shall
pardon me if the very nature of this subject and the
nanner in which one and all approach it renders me
d ill, dry and uninteresting.— Deakin, in an Anti-
lie/'erend a speech.
Nay, Alfred, you are never dull !
How can you say such things?
Yon soar and dive like any gull
That still retains its wings ;
Your speeches never could be dry,
They fizzle like champagne
(Perhaps that is tlie reason why
No body they contain).
Each is a sparkling drink, no less,
And spits like any cat ;
Each for an hour may effervesce—
But, then, it must go fiat;
The morning sheet, howe’er it tries
To tightly cork each line,
Ne’er brings to us the bead which flies
From Deakin’s Ileady Wine.
When Deakin, Eib'ral Cellar-man,
Hands round his lovely booze,
No single soul is there who can
The flowing howl refuse ;
But only dull and stupid men
Will keep it on the shelf;
The stuff is only decent when
Alf pours it out himself.

The co. says its total funds are £66,561. The
amount is made up as follows: —
Total accumulated funds .. .. £29,726
Office property written up (1908) .. £10,095
Ditto (1901) 5,500
15,595
Office property written down (1902) .. 5,500
10,095
Sale of accident business and goodwill 15,000
Capital .. .. .. .. .. 11,740
Total funds £66,561
The co. was started in Brisbane, and its earlier
history was, to say the least of it, very peculiar. That
in fact, may be guessed from the way in which the
book-value of property was written up twice, and
then written down again. The net result of these
purely book-keeping transactions is that the funds
have been inflated to the extent of £10,095. The
property—office premises—may, nevertheless, be worth
£10,095 more than was paid. That, however, does
not alter the fact that of the total funds of £66,561,
as they appear in the published accounts, only
£29,726 has been provided by the insurance busi-
ness. Like this:—•
Total funds £66,561
Goodwill sold (paper) £15,000
.Share capital .. .. .. 11,740
Assets written up 10,095
36,835
Funds provided by the business £29,726
This is a very poor accumulation for an insurance
co. which has been in existence for 16 years. Still
it may be quite sufficient to meet the liabilities
which have accumulated under the policies. On that
point, of course, it would be necessary to have the
report of an actuary’s investigation before expressing
any opinion. Without that a life insurance co.’s
balance-sheet doesn’t take us very far. The last in-
vestigation was made by T. W. Bremner at the end
of 1907 ; and, though no doubt a report was issued,
all that the directors’ ! report said about it in the
following year was: “The quinquennial valuation of
the co.’s liabilities was completed by Mr. T. W.
Bremner, F.F.A., A.1.A., during the year.” '
The present accounts are not satisfactory, because
they do not disclose anything with regard to the
written-up properties or the nature of the £15,000
represented by shares which the co. got on the sale
of its accident business. There is a line among the
assets: “Investment in public co., £16,250.” From
this it appears that the co. has put £1250 of cash
into the subsidiary co. Now, so far as £15,000
of the total is concerned, it is represented by shares
which are bringing in nothing, which cannot be sold
for another eight years, and which are limited as to
dividend for the same period to p.c. The shares,
offered in the market, would probably not fetch
£5OOO. To-morrow the co. could, if it chose, form
another co. with a capital of £50,000 to acquire the
rights to grow cucumbers on the office window-sills,
and it could then enter up: “Investment in public
co. (No. 2), £49,994,” and add the amount to the
alleged funds. This is no way to handle a serious
business like life insurance.
Yet the position may be quite sound. It certainly
ought to be a lot sounder than if this were a mutual
office, because at any rate hgre is £11,740 of share
capital to come on, and a mutual office wouldn’t have
that. Also, it is quite probable that the property
asset was a lucky purchase. Nevertheless, the ex-
penses are so high that it is simply impossible for
the policy-holders to get good results at present,
whatever they may do bv-and-bye. Lumping together
Industrial and Ordinary figures, because they can’t
be separated, this shows the proportion of total out-
goings (apart from claims) :
Outgoings (Apart from Claims) Compared with
Income.
1903.. £BO 10 6 per cent. 1907.. £57 6 2 per cent.
1904.. 7610 1 „ 1908.. 571110
1905.. 66 18 11 „ 1909.. 60 8 9 „
1906.. 62 6 4 „ 1910.. 601710
That, as mentioned above, includes Ordinary business,
which certainly shouldn’t cost more than 25 p.c. ; so
the expense rate in the Industrial division must be
appalling. And nothing is more certain than this—-
that if the premiums go in expenses, they can’t be
available to pay claims. Not that the co. does not
pay every cent that it undertakes to pay. No doubt
it does all that. But, in view of the high expense
rate, what a ridiculously small amount that must be
compared with what the same amount of premium
ought to purchase!
The Metropolitan Accident, Fire, and
General Insurance Co. (Sydney), which took over
the accident business of the Australian Metropolitan
Life Assurance Co., and gave for it the absurdly
high price of £15,000 in £1 fully-paid shares:—
Towards De-
Loss. Dividends. Reserve's, licit.
Dec., 1909.. £773 Nil £15,773
Dec., 1910.. 1322 Nil 17,095
In the foregoing the co. is given credit for the re-
serve to cover unexpired risks ; this fund is in credit
at present £ll9l. It is a very doubtful
reserve; and without it the present deficit
is £18,286. The co. started on a bad
basis. At the end of 1909 it had a capital of
£17,728, of which £15,000 was “paper.” That is
to say, only 10,913 shares had been issued for cash;
on this lot ss. per share had been paid, leaving a
liability of 15s. That left 222,787 shares available;
and a prospectus was issued offering these to the
public. The 1.0. A., however, got in first; and the
appeal of the Metropolitan was successful to only a
very small extent. Of the 222,787 shares available,
only 9411 had been taken up as at December 31,
and of this number 1154 were not formally allotted
until January 6 of this year. These shares, like the
others, are paid to only 55., so the present capital,
apart from the “paper,” is £5047. Undoubtedly
the £15,000 paid for the goodwill blocked, and still
blocks, any rush for the shares. The business trans-
ferred was infinitesimal; all that this co. under re-
view really got in return for the free shares it issued
was the use of the parent co.’s organisation, mainly
industrial. It would have been far better had the
co., on making an appeal for the extra capital, taken
the bull by the horns and reduced the amount of
loading to £5OOO at the very outside. All that
was done, however, was to arrange with the holders
of the 15,000 shares to bar the sale of the shares
and limit the div. payable on them to 3J p.c. p.a.,
non-preferential and non-cumulative, for 10 years
from January 1, 1909. It was not a sufficient
concession. The co. has passed through two years
without a hope of a div., so the parent co. has not
given away a thing so far as those two years are
concerned; and it isn’t likely that a div. will be
payable for another two or three years at least.
Certainly the profit and loss deficit should be wiped
out first. Further, in view of the difficulty in plac-
ing the shares, the concession with regard to the sale
of the vendors’ shares doesn’t seem worth much
either. _ The vendor co. was far too greedy; and its
greed is a heavy handicap on the infant co. It is
not too late even now to save the situation by scis-
soring £lO,OOO off the “paper.” If that were done,
the 5000 shares might be worth something consider-
able. If it isn’t done, it is quite likely that the
15,000 will be worth nothing.
The extent and progress* of the business are further
shown hereunder:—
Pre- Ratio of Ratio of
miums. Claims. Expenses. Claims. Expenses.
1909 .. £7038 £2804 £5007 39'9 p.c. 71 “2 p.c.
1910 .. 8279 4004 5933 48'3 „ 71'6 ~
The expenses in proportion to income are tremend-
ously heavy, though it has to be noted that the
pretty heavy expense of trying to get in more capital
has been apparently charged up to profit and loss. On
the other hand, the expense rate, for the first year
especially, is under normal, because many—probably
most—-of. the risks did not run the full 12 months,
whereas credit is taken for the full 12 months’ pre-
mium. Good management and fair luck may pull
the co. through ; if it dies, the only possible verdict
of the Coroner’s Court will be that its parents tied
a stone round its neck and dragged it under.

This bank, like some others, didn’t lop off enough
capital when it did a bit of pruning after the great
trouble. It lost a great deal more than it con-
fessed at the time; so those losses have had to be
made good out of subsequent profits. It was a mis-
take; and it was all the harder to repair because
Queensland didn’t recover nearly as rapidly as had
been expected. The new management, therefore, had
a long stern-chase. For years the bank only paid 24
p.c. ; and that was a handicap in itself. When a
bank can only do that much it is a pretty good in-
dication that the figures are not what they pretend
to be in the balance-sheet—that the capital isn’t
there, and hence that the alleged assets are not
there either. And that means two things—one that
the bank probably can’t get as much deposits as it
could do with, and the other that, owing to the sus-
picion with which it is viewed, it must keep more
money in the till than a bank which isn’t viewed
with suspicion. Some managers have said—“ Well,
here goes, and I’ll chance it,” and have put up the
dividend, practically with the intention of bluffing
the public into believing that the position was better
than it really was. Sometimes that policy has come
off: bluff would be an abandoned art if it didn’t
come off sometimes. Edward Denny Day, of the
Royal of Queensland, isn’t that sort of manager.
He just put up with the handicap and plugged
ahead; and this paper for one congratulates him
upon being at last in a position to pay 5 p.c. ; and
it believes that he wouldn’t pay 5 p.c. if he wasn’t
absolutely certain that he could afford it. And, as
5 p.c. is a very fair return, it may also nrobably
be taken for granted that, in E. D. Day’s opinion,
the capital of the bank is now just about intact, the
previously-undisclosed losses having been made good
either out of ordinary business profits or out of re-
coveries in value.
For the first time for many years the declared
profits exceed 5 p.c. on the capital and reserves com-
bined. Recent results: —

The capital is now £523,217, as against £513,632
12 months ago. The increase is mainly due to the
issue of a few more pref. shares; the "pref. capital
now totals £54,080. Other balance-sheet figures:—
Mar., TO. Mar.,’ll.

The total liabilities are thus £1,453,595; and, as
mentioned just now, the capital is £523,217, so that
the bapk still has the use of less than £3 of the
public’s money for every £1 of the share capital,
apart from reserves. This means, in the ordinary
way, comparatively small profits; but it also means
small risks. Further, the liquid assets in proportion
to total liabilities are still very large—just over 40
p.c. This also tends towards comparatively small
profits, and it also implies small risks. Generally,
then, the position looks very secure; in every respect
security appears to be the first consideration.

This is a fairly ancient institution, consisting largely
of lords and Viscounts and such folks with well-lined
purses. The capital is £1,000,000, but only
£200,000 has been called up; so the lords, vis-
counts, etc., are liable for £BOO,OOO. It is, no
doubt, this large calling power on fat purses which
makes it easy for the co. to borrow cheaply. Its
liabilities at the last balance totalled £805,738, of
which two-thirds was on debentures. The latter have
a currency mostly of five years, and that gives the
co. time to turn round in case a slump should strike
Maoriland, where most of the co.’s loan and mort-
gage business is done. As R. Bedford wrote the other
day, the most striking characteristic of Australia (and
with Maoriland it’s the same) is resilience. Things
bounce up again. The trouble with the financial co.
which works on current accounts or short-dated loans
is that the lenders want their money back before
things have had time to bounce up again ; and that
means big losses on account of forced sales. Cer-
tainly, if you take money for five years certain you
are liable to have it lying idle on your hands some
of the time; but that hasn’t been the case for the
past few years. Maoriland has been borrowing hard,
and London has been a greedy taker of short loans.
These things have no doubt helped to fatten the co.’s
profits lately. Its risks certainly are pretty sub-
stantial. Besides its own money it has to protect
the before-mentioned £805,738 of other people’s
money ; and £810,202 of, the total is in various ad-
vances—loans on wool, etc., on mortgage, or on
customers’ current account. In addition, there is
£193,134 in “investments in sundry securities and
shares in colonial companies.” The last is a vague
item. Cash runs to £77,067, and premises, plant,
and merchandise to another £115,183.
It was remarked just now that the most striking
Australasian characteristic is resilience. But if a
mortgage co. relies upon prices always recovering
their oid level after a fall, it may hit an unpleasant
snag. Prices, for instance, may be—and in Maori-
land have been —gradually inflated by a furious
public borrowing policy. When that borrowing stops,
and instead of easy money flowing in, hard-earned
money has to be sent out to pay either interest or
principal, prices must inevitably shrivel. It is 20
years since the Victorian boom, for instance, and the
old prices haven’t yet been reached, and probably
never will be reached again. The mortgage co.
which sees this, and, during a public borrowing
boom, insists on wide margins accordingly, is the
one that will get the least into the soup.

The bulk of the reserves is in a heap labelled “In-
surance and Repairs Suspense Account” ; and on top
of this heap £4149 has dropped like the gentle dew
from Heaven during the past six months. Whether it
is a real reserve fund, or only a set-off against cur-
rent depreciation, the man who post-mortems the ac-
counts doesn’t know, and it isn’t intended that he
should know. It seems pretty certain, however,
that this is more than a depreciation fund, because
something else of unspecified size is written off
against depreciation, and deducted from the book-
value. Very big changes have taken place in the
past 10 years. This shows the chief of them: —
1900. 1910.
Capital £131,250 £218,750
Creditors 48,662 23,619
Ships and wharves .. .. 157,170 267,719
Cash and hook debts .. 43,529 31,672
Sundries of all sorts .. .. 12,230 18,303
So the co. has £294,075 net of assets now as against
£164,267 10 years ago; but of that increase of
£129,808, new capital stands for £87,500. It is
impossible, however, to say whether ship property,
etc., is valued on the same basis now as it was in
past years. Sweeping the eye down the co.’s history
it will be seen that, for years previous to 1898, 6
p.c. was paid ; then it got to 8 p.c. ; then in the bad
year 1903, there was a temporary return to 6 p.c. ;
and ever since the new capital was got in 8 p.c. ap-
pears to have been very comfortably earned. Further,
the extremely fruitful North Coast is making rapid
progress; and, though the railways will cut in for
some of the traffic, it seems certain that, unless the
northern bar harbors go to wreck altogether, the
North Coast Co. will continue to do a large carrying
trade—especially of export produce.

That might be put a little differently. There is a
small marine insurance fund not included in the re-
serves mentioned in the table; and the £124 shown
as written off for last year has been appropriated to
that fund. It might, therefore, be just as correct to
say that, on the accounts, the net profits for the year
were £8387, and that the total visible reserves are
£7719. The co. is 10 years old, but it is only during
the last four years that the shares have been on the
market; probably the intention was to give the
partners, or chief partners, a better chance to turn
some of their holding into money. Anyway, Mr.
John Paul, who was chairman in 1907, when the
shares were first talked about on ’Change, left the
Board during the following year, along with J. E.
Paul and Dr. F. P. Paul, “consequent upon their
ceasing to hold the necessary qualification”; and
E. D. Gray, who then became chairman, has been
followed by W. Lambert. The management of the
practically-reconstituted co. evidently sees large
things ahead, and it has been pushing out. First, it
bought a business in Melbourne, though that caused
trouble for a while, and southern trade competitors
began to hit back; a London branch was opened;
and “negotiations have been entered into for the
purchase of the freehold of the Sussex-street (Syd-
ney) property at a satisfactory figure.” All this
has, of course, meant money, and most of it has
come from the bank. The principal items have
moved this way:—

The great bulk of the co.’s resources is thus in two
items —merchandise and book debts, and whether
they are worth more or less than they are repre-
sented to be, or just as much, there is no way of
telling. If the stock has been over-valued, the profits
have been overstated; for the one depends entirely
upon the other. It is much the same in all industrial
ventures; one wants to know the management as
well as the figures. No doubt the quality of the
management is sufficiently told by the figures if the
same management has been at the helm over a long
run of years. But in a case of this sort, where the
management is practically new, an over-sanguine
view as to the value of the stock or the book debts
wouldn’t be disclosed by the accounts yet a while.
On the other hand, certainly, the management may
be pessimistic—in which case it is possible that the
published figures are under the mark. That is the
risk the investor takes. In any case, however, he
will get comfort from the fact that there is no good-
will item whatever, so that the whole of capital, re-
serves and liabilities is represented by something that
you can take in your hand and look at.

Between 1901 and 1911 the capital was reduced by
£12,500, that sum, besides large amoupts of re-
serves, being written off on a re-valuation of assets.
These assets consist very largely of a river plant—
six steamers and nine barges. The co. does a con-
siderable Murray and Murrumbidgee trade, with
something on the Darling; and, in the nature of
things, that trade is as uncertain as the rivers.
Moreover, it may now be taken to be the settled
policy of the country that the claims of irrigation
on the uncertain waters of the, rivers come first.
There will be other things for P., W. and Co. to do,
no doubt; but the river business doesn’t seem to
have much of a future, and the river assets will pro-
bably continuously shrink in value, and will have
to be written off out of either profits or capital.
Besides its carrying business, the co. handles pro-
duce and some merchandise —almost every river boat
is a hawking boat—and no doubt it is this part of
the business that is mainly responsible for the large
amount of book debts, amounting almost to tl»e
total of the capital ( £50,000),
The Commonwealth Insurance Co., formed
about six years ago in Melbourne, and the Insurance
Office of Australia, formed last year in Sydney, will
probably soon be running in double harness. Nego-
tiations are in progress for the absorption of the
Commonwealth Co.
Naturally the Ore Concentration (1905)
Co., which owns the Elmore zinc-flotation patents,
doesn’t like the idea of the Zinc Corporation throwing
out the Elmore pans for the Minerals Separation pro-
cess. The co. has issued a circular to shareholders on
this and other matters. “The Zinc Corporation, which,
in January, 1908, had tried and abandoned several
other processes, and was on the verge of failure, has
since made a working profit of fully £250,000 by
adopting the Elmore process.” But now the Zinc-
Corporation had announced the change mentioned
above, “although it was on the failure of the
former [the Minerals Separation Co.’s process] that
the Elmore process had been so successfully adopted.”
The circular proceeds:—“The reason given for this
is that a change in the character of some of the
tailings to be treated in future has made the Min-
erals Separation process more desirable. In the
opinion of your Board it would, at very small
cost, have been perfectly simple to so adjust the
existing successful working (should any modification
have been found actually necessary) to meet the ex-
pected change in a portion of the material to be
treated. No proper opportunity has been afforded
to demonstrate this.. In the circular above re-
ferred to, it is stated that an incidental advantage
to Zinc Corporation shareholders in the change re-
ferred to will be the avoiding of a lawsuit threat-
ened by the Minerals Separation Co. This lawsuit
is not one of infringement against the Elmore pro-
cess, but your Directors understand, arises from an
undertaking given by the Zinc Corporation, when first
starting to experiment with the Minerals Separation
Co.’s process, that no other flotation process would
ever be used. This most unusual condition has
been the basis of a legal claim, which is now ap-
parently ended by the agreement on the part of the
Zinc Corporation to return to the Minerals Separa-
tion Co.’s process. Under these circumstances your
Board has issued a writ in Australia against the
Zinc Corporation for infringement of tne Elmore
Patents, and has of course claimed royalties from
them for past and future working.” if this yarn
about an agreement never to use any but the
Minerals Separation process is true, it doesn’t say
much for the management of the Zinc Corporation
in those days, whatever the then virtues of the pro-
cess may have been.
The chairman of the Broken Hill Junc-
tion talks rubbish in the half-yearly report. “The
unsatisfactory results attached to milling operations
are primarily due to the depleted state of the labor
market at the time, and the further necessity for
economy by the introduction of a number of labor-
saving appliances.” The mill is poor and the ore
is poor, and wretched results were got when there
was no scarcity of men. And, anyhow, how can
skilled workmen be expected to be available for
works which are shut down three-fourths of their
time? It is further stated that 100,000 tons (esti-
mated) of high-grade ore have been uncovered above
the 400 ft. level, mostly in the crushed section. Be-
fore any importance is attached to those 100,000
tons, a reliable estimate should be made of the cost
of extracting the stuff. Mining ore from crushed
sections is a good deal like working in quicksand.
The position of the co. on January 31 was that it
owed £5705, and had about £BOO of cash and
£l7OO of more or less liquid assets. There was,
however, 2s. 6d. callable on the 200,000 shares.
Great Cobar (N.S.W.) increased its out-
put to 6248 tons copper last year, as compared with
4855 tons for 1909. Some metallurgical difficulties
were overcome, so that the co. treated more of its
own ore, besides making considerable purchases from
outside mines. Still there is no reason for rejoicing
over a smelting campaign that merely conquers tech-
nical difficulties, and is unable to show adequate
profits. There certainly is a promise now that costs
are to be reduced; and if a dividend comes in conse-
quence it will be very welcome after a wait of four
years. First, however, there is a lot to be done in
the way of straightening out the tangled finances.
The best news offering is that reserves have jumped
ahead of all previous estimates, partly because of
discoveries below 1100 ft.; but the ore doesn’t part
with its copper so easily as it used to.

The Bigelow Patent Rock Breaker commends
itself to mine managers who welcome an effective
machine which is cheaper to maintain and repair
than Gyratory and other crushers. This machine is
of the well-known Blake type, but lias some ad-
ditional improvements which have justified in prac-
tice all the claims advanced by the patentee. The
work of crushing is done on the down-stroke of the
pitman instead of the up-stroke; there is no strain
on the pitman cap; two large side rods resist the
thrust of the toggles, and enable the jaws to be
regulated within a large range; the back toggle is
so made as to resist all legitimate strains of crush-
ing, but will shear under exceptional conditions and
save the machine. The shaft and eccentric bearings
are self-lubricating; the jaws are convex and con-
cave, and with the cheek plates are made of steel
and are reversible. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have installed a great
number "of these machines, and hold the sole manu-
facturing rights for the Commonwealth.* IS

either belt, steam, or electrically driven, should not
fail to inspect the Reavell Air Compressor, when they
will be impressed by its simplicity, compactness and
portability. Owing to the working parts being en-
tirely enclosed it can be placed to work on any part
of the mine, either on the surface or underground.
The number of repeat orders which Messrs. Reavell
have received from the mining community proves

* * * *
Bookham, l'ass (N.S.W.), continues to
drive the shallow 75ft. level north and south; but
prospects are poor. The mineralised quartz carries a
little free gold, the best stone being on the hanging-
wall side. Picked stuff from a very limited area
lias given rich returns; but the average of the reef
is low. It is, nevertheless, a well-defined fissure
reef, in porphyry, and may be worth deeper pros-
pecting.
* * * *
Central Red, White and Blue, Bendigo, is
still receiving the puff pathetique. The co. hit the
last leg of the saddle in the 400 ft. crosscut the other
day, and shares promptly put up a new record.
Their descent to more modest latitudes didn’t arise
from any nervousness over the jumping case, as was
so aptly told, but was due to the quality of the
stone and in part to some faulting. What the
average housemaid, who is backing the market, won’t
digest is that the Central Blue formation is a
saddle. What the average newspaper puffer doesn’t
101 l is that the saddle isn’t a big one, that distinct
shoots of gold exist in it, and that any impoverish-
ment of these shoots means that the market will
fall like a house of cards. The idea is that because
the Little 180 has good gold the shoot will extend
from it uninterruptedly to the Central Blue. Good
luck to it if it does; but while no one can say this
is not going to happen, history of the line doesn’t
point to the fulfilment of expectations of the kind.
* * * #
Jumbunua Coal (Vic.) tells a sorry tale
as the result of. Peter Mcßride’s Powlett State coal
mine. Six months back Jumbunna’s half-year’s out-
put was up to 35,500 tons; now it is down to
20,050 tons, the mine having to be put on one shift.
What knocked the stuffing out of the Board was the
reduction in the price of Powlett slack. On this
point the Board avers that Stanley Hunter is selling
under the cost of production. If this be the case,
there must be a fat profit on the Powlett big coal,
or someone is telling yarns fit only for the smoking-
room, for the statement is made on first-rate au-
thority that the State mine is now, in the aggregate,
> ielding good profits. Of course Jumbunna has felt
the pinch over its lost railway contracts. That loss
Has inevitable, directly the Government decided to
make the railway service complete by furnishing
it with a coal mine, thus putting the Commissioners
independent of any ring or vend. Between one
thing and another, the co. lost £1768 for the past
half-year. The result must astonish Boyd and Mur-
ray, the co.’s chief spokesmen, who used to walk
about as if they didn’t care a D for Government op-
position. Now they have got it, the same old stiff
back isn’t showing. Nevertheless, the Board still
has hopes, to judge by the outlay on plant, and the
dead-work done during the past half-year.
*. * * #
What is happening to Duke and Main
beads Consols, Maryborough (Vic.), is what befalls
.■very deep lead show nowadays. Since the mine got
mi the pay list, the co. has announced divs. as regu-
larly as the cocks crow. Now the gutter is widen-
ing, and cutting-up drives are not indicating the
old good-value wash. The zenith was attained
when the Cathcart scramble was on. Wise men
then sold, and the speckers have been pressing prices
down ever since. There is plenty of wash in the
mine, but the runs of gold are not so good as here-
mfore. That threatens the sequence of dividends;
and once a break occurs, calls get perilously close,
because of the way Victorian directors habitually
disburse well towards the last penny of profits. The
co. has shut down its No. 2 shaft, and its explora-
tions in the shallow ground aren’t adding to the
weight of the petty cash till. Down the lead the
Burnt Creek is hard up, and the quality of its
wash doesn’t help the Duke Leads directors to re-
build castles in that direction.
Caralulup (Vic.) is the present home of
(he Loddon Deep Leads, promoted years back in Lon-
don by Bottomley, if this writer remembers aright.
Anyway, it has been a Bottomley -sort of sink for
cash. Its first venturing was on some site selected
by geologists and confirmed by directors. But every-
thing became a ghastly failure. Hence the flit to
Caralulup and the sinking of a shaft there. To
demonstrate the absolute ignorance of the Londoner
where deep lead mining is concerned, chairman E.
T. Holloway, when appealing for more cash, said,
“the report of the Government indicates there is
wash of vast thickness, and that a sample of gold or
sediment off the bed rock had been taken.” He
says, too, that with the shaft 330 ft. deep, they will
be able to deal with the wash not only in the imme-
diate locality, but four or five miles away. A state-
ment of this kind, made about a Queensland property
and a Government report, would be sure to attract
ibe attention of the Mines Department; but there
isn’t any bustling official body in Victoria to analyse
I! olloway pills of this type. Holloway may have quoted
iis “brief” correctly; but, for all that, the truth
is that thickness of overlying drifts is not quite the
same thing as thickness of gold-bearing wash. And
as to a “sample” of the “sediment,” all alluvial
men know that the best gold is nearly always got in
ihe lowest 18 inches of the wash. It would be a. fair
Cling for the Victorian Mines Department to take
sieps to post ; Bull on that fact if it wants to save
us deep lead mining from absolute putridity.

>.ver the omission of some qualifying words from the
I rospectus of a tin-mining show up Mitta Mitta way.
The cure for all that lies in fixing more definitely a
personal responsibility for reports that appear in
prospectuses. When one reads that the workings
•iver a length of 700 ft., and that splendid tin oxide
i .is been disclosed in this area wherever opened
■ p, the wonder is that good things have gone beg-
ging so long in north-east Victoria. The investor
lias heard of the Mt. Wills tin smash, just as he
k nows of the land boom crash ; so he isn’t likely to
s’-riously risk his pence in lode tin mining in that
!• icality. What the row may do is to force Peter
Mcßride to see the need for revising the law relating
io mining ccs. and their flotation.
H. E. Rowe, of the Long Tunnel, Wal-
I alia (Vic.), is a pastmaster in the art of dragging
' '-rrings across the track. He says now that what is
" rong at the Tunnel is shortage of labor. A doubt-
ing Stock Exchange rejoins that more gold and
more stone is the present necessity. Bottom levels
-■ far have shown broken material, and there isn’t
the stock of ore above them that befits a claim of the
age and standing of the Tunnel. That isn t the
fault of the management, but is the thing that
happens sooner or later to all deep mining. What
with the Extended practically a derelict, Walhalla.
mining depends on the old veteran. It is to Rowe s
credit, despite all his wordiness and fluster, that he put
£2O 000 to reserve when times were good, otherwise
a profit of £609 for six months, with mining at
3000 ft., would put a damper even on Henry Hart-
rick’s enthusiasm.
Doolette astonished the natives and others
by turning down the Corinthian, in the Bullfinch
country. In the days when the Bullfinch enthusiasts
saw pink, Doolette and his party were thought
to have had this show thrown at them at the ridicu-
lous price of £25,000. The treatment difficulties
have evidently pulled them up. * #
Some of the Bullfinch people are feeling
sorry that they ever heard of London. They sold
50,000 shares at par for working capital, and the
“working capital” has had no use up to date but to
pay the London Board, as the mine gets all it wants
by foolishly railing rich ore to Kalgoorlie. Not a
copper has come from London. The underwriters got
the call on 50,000 shares at par for two years, and
that means that they always have a guaranteed
“bear” of 50,000. On the mine the policy of drag-
ging out rich ore—all right when won from develop-
ment—becomes rotten when won from stoping, but
it has been persisted in by London orders, against
Morgan’s advice. One small stope is mullocked up,
with 2oz. ore standing on both sides of it. Dorrie
Doolette, back again, has been hammering at the
directors to get them to change their policy, and
they have at last agreed to do so.
From Randolph Bedford: Chaffinch case
proved one thing, and that was that the show was not
salted. In the words of Police-Magistrate Roe: “No
man could believe after hearing the evidence that the
ore had been in the trench by any but natural
means.” It is as legitimate to bear shares as to
bull them; but it is illegal to cry “Salt” where
there is no salt for the purpose of wrecking a stock
already beared. The evidence showed that 57 bags
of ore were bagged from the trench before the* schist
ore vein cut out and left only the practically barren
quartz, and that the existence of these 57 bags was
concealed and not reported until the market had been
finished with, when it was reported by letter, though
the insinuations of salting had been reported by
wire. The Government, by the prosecution, no
matter how poorly it was conducted, performer! this
service to the Westralian mining industry—it estab-
lished the fact that the find was genuine.
“R. 8.”: “Oofbird” (B. 30/3/T1) wants
me to reply to certain questions. re the Bull Ant.
Certainly. All the statements made by me as to the
“surrounding schist that carries gold” and the
“2joz. hornblendic rock which caused Grut to turn
the Bull Ant up again with great velocity after pre-
viously turning it down,” were taken from Grut’s
report, which caused the show to be floated. I did
not sample the Bull Ant —had neither right nor
necessity to do it; and as I heard Grut spoken of
as a very frigid and careful pessimist, 1 accepted
his statement, not then knowing that Mr. Grut’s
end of a mine is the assay furnace, and not the
sampling sheet. The only reports I have heard of
on the Bull Ant are Grut’s and Henderson’s, which
everybody accepted at the time, as you must accept
the statements of responsible men until you have a
chance to disprove, them. On the strength of these
reports I applied for a few hundred shares, and later
sold them with great enthusiasm at a loss. “Oof-
bird” is wise after the event; he had better question
the experts on whose sampling and report the show
was floated.
For the first quarter of this year Westralia
produced 332,09 <oz. fine gold. The total
is 23,7630 z. less than for the correspond-
ing term in 1910. The decline in the value of
the ore treated, which has been noticeable since
1903, continues. Last year 2,884,298 tons were
crushed for £6,041,254, equal to 41s. 9d. per ton
of 22401 b. This shows a shrinkage compared with
1909 of 220,706 tons, and a decrease in value of
£654,892, or Is. per ton treated. In order to
minimise as far as possible the ill-effect on profits,
it has been found necessary from time to time to lay
out a lot of capital in new and more efficient cost-
reducing machinery; but with increased working
depth and harder ore, expenditure is a little more
now than it was a year or two ago, and is likely
to go up bit by bit in the future.
Bull Ant shareholders (W.A.) have kicked
over the traces and refused to take up scrip sent out
from London, alleging that the delay in delivery
releases them from their contract. Lawyers’ letters
have also been sent to London, demanding the return
of cash subscribed, on the grounds that some of the
statements on which the delusion was floated were a
gross misrepresentation of facts.

decided to pass out: Pine Hill South, Cornishman,

Ida H. (W.A.) continues to do well. Last
month’s profit amounted to over £l3OO, and the

* * * *
Mount Jackson (W.A.) has lately been
having some of its gold-bearing produce exhibited in
the shop windows of Southern Cross; but in view of
past performances and the double uncertainty as to
water supply and permanency of gold deposits, in-
vestors and speculators had better sniff at any flota-
tion which is not backed up by experts who have a
reputation to lose.
The report and balance-sheet of Tindal’s
(W.A.) tells a very different tale from that told share-
holders at the annual meeting. In addressing this
impoverished gathering, Chairman Dawson said that
the average value of the ore treated during the 12
months was 6dwt., and that the profit was £4519,
an amount sufficient to pay four dividends of 6d. per
share if so much money had not been absorbed, etc.
Words, words, words ! The ore handled averaged
sdwt., not 6dwt., and the year’s expenditure
( £23,369) exceeded the receipts by £65. Even this
result was only achieved by an almost entire stop-
page of development; had the mine not been shabbily
treated in this respect, the co. would have made a
much bigger loss. The manager’s estimate for the
current six months is that, after allowing £250 a
month for development (equal to a few feet- a
week), a profit of £2OOO will be realised; but as
all sorts of other expenditure has to come off this pro-
spective “profit,” and as the present attenuated
stopes will be depleted much faster than new ones
(if any) are likely to be got ready, the chances of
shareholders getting anything at all are still far off.
In fact, The Bulletin goes further, and adheres to
its old opinion that, if worked fairly, the mine is
unpayable under present conditions.
Sand Queen, at Comet Yale (W.A.),
shows a working profit for the past year of £4683 ;
and after deducting £lB3l depreciation,. and adding
£lOO9 brought forward from the previous year, a
credit of £3861 remains. So far the reef has been
proved to 200 ft., where it is worth about £3 per
ton. Ore reserves are estimated at 20,000 tons.
The management proposes to deepen the shaft to
300 ft. without delay, and if the reef is found to
maintain its average at this depth the monthly out-
put is to be increased to £4500.
Queensland’s gold-mining divs. for the first
quarter of the year got down to £73,341 ; and that
included Mt. Morgan’s £50,000, which wasn’t all
from gold.
Good rain has fallen at Hampden and Mt.
Klliott, in the Cloncurry district. Nearly sin. fell
at Hampden in less than an hour, and at Mt. Elliott
there was a downpour of 2Jin. The Elliott reser-
voirs are overflowing, and a plentiful supply of water
is assured for months ahead. As is usual when the
rain falls, the railway line from Cloncurry to Mt.
Elliott was washed away, and no trains arrived at
Elliott for two days. (
Chillagoe and the Etheridge are still in
trouble, owing to further washaways on the line that
crawls up the mountains at the back of Cairns.
* * * *
Brilliant Deeps, Charters Towers, is driv-
ing a crosscut from the shaft to open up deeper
levels of the Brilliant reef. The present workings
extend above and below the 2413 ft. level; and al-
though some good crushings have been obtained, the
varying quality of the stone and defective ventilation
have made the show unremunerative. It is hoped
that the crosscut will open up a block of payable
stone .that was proved by a winze from No. 3 level.
Brilliant Extended, to the east of the Deeps, is pros-
pecting at a vertical depth of 2800 ft., with indif-
ferent results. Still, there is payable stone only a
short distance overhead, so the reef may improve
again. Last month’s return was worth £9060, from
4250 tons stone and 4500 tons tailings. This gave a
profit of £1255. At 4s. per share the Extended has
a market value of £50,000 —nearly all of it specu-
lative.
Two latest fortnightly returns from the
New Brilliant Freehold, Charters Towers, make up
1545 tons for £6769. The last lot averaged £4
165., so the stopes are yielding pretty well. Still,
an output of anything like £6OOO a month cant be
kept going unless the deep levels show much better
form. No. 4, which is the deepest, is 748 ft. west
from the crosscut, and the most hopeful sign is that
the face is in diorite, with some plumbago showing
on the hanging wall. No. 1, or the top level, which
coincides with a very good level (No. 7) worked
in Mills’ United, has proved the best, and the hang-
ing wall reef has also supplied large quantities of
payable stone. These two separate but nearly par-
allel reefs, together with a cross reef and somfe blocks
on the Brilliant, constitute the very moderate re-
serves. The Brilliant stone will now help to keep
the mill going, for a connection has been made with
the main hauling shaft. #
Oaks Rush, which owns the deepest work-
ings on North Queensland’s newest field, has now got
its main shaft down 144 ft. Mineralised leaders
occur in an extensive decomposed formation; and the
hanging wall and footwall sections are being pros-
pected by driving along both sides, and by sinking a
winze below the 100 ft. level. The shaft itself is going
down in an auriferous formation, picked samples
from which give fairly good results. Between 300
and 400 tons have been raised in the progress of de-
velopment; but it is uncertain when the battery
will start, because, owing to the phenomenally wet
season, the carriage of supplies- from the coast is
very irregular.
During March Gympie produced 13,556
tons for <4220z. gold. Dividends, £9587; calls,
£2933. The chief producers were: Scottish Gympie,
6800 tons for 17700 z. (div. £4125) ; No. 2 S. Gt.
Eastern, 1700 tons for 16820 z. (div. £3600) ; No. 1
N. Columbia Smithfield, 1196 tons for 6500 z. (div.
£800) ; Columbia Extended, 619 tons for 5580 z.
(div. £1062) ; No. 3 N. Columbia, 614 tons for
5370 z. (profit, about £800) ; No. 1 N. Victory,
200 tons for 4730 z. S. Glanmire and Monkland
should soon cut the Power reef if it lives through
from No. 2 S. Gt. Eastern. This reef has given
several dividends to the latter co. No. 2 S. Gt.
Eastern requires more boiler power to continue sink-
ing to the depth it wants to reach, about 2000 ft.
Oriental Consols, the most S.E. property on field,
has obtained £5OO subsidy from the Government to
crosscut further east. The No. 3 N. Columbia has pur-
chased a local 20-head battery for £450, and shows
much improved prospects. This property is worth
watching; it is the most easterly, and also the deep-
est, mine on the northern end of the field. No. 1 N.
Victory snould soon figure on the dividend list. The
Gt. Northern, at the Two-mile end, is now fairly
under weigh again. It is hoped the management
will make an effort to prove the lower slates, for
such proof will be of value all round. The long
struggle to prove this property is followed with a
good deal of interest.
Mt. Bischoff (Tas.) shares have gone to
over £47, which brings the .total valuation to
£564,000. Just at present the old co. is shelling
out 6s. per month, or £3 12s. a year, or, say, 8 p.c.
p.a. on market price. Last month 19,208 tons were
treated for 90 tons of oxide, so. Bischoff’s monthly
£3600 div. is at the rate of £4O per ton of the oxide
produced. Certainly it is a matter for congratula-
tion that one of our lowest-grade propositions should
give such good results; but it is about time to do
some hard thinking when the market price is pushed
up so high that only 8 p.c. is returned during the
height of a boom in the erratic metal on which the
whole thing depends.
Waihi (M.L.) report to be presented
to the London meeting in May, shows a profit of
£559,000, about £56,000 below the previous year’s.
Dividends absorbed £396,000. The flare-up among
M.L. shareholders, who have been “landed” with
scrip which is worth about a third of what it
was 12 months ago, has had one result at least: they
are nowadays getting progress reports promptly.
The relative present importance of Maori-
land’s quartz-mining fields is shown by the latest
returns' for a month: —Ohinemuri, £154,000;
Thames, £717; Coromandel, £670; Reefton
(South Island), about £12,000. But Thames and
Coromandel both live in the hope of striking big
patches, as they have done before.
According to 11. R. Merton and Co., Lon-
don, the total visible supplies of copper on February
28 were 82,387 tons. This, however, is without
reckoning German stocks, of which no official figures
were available. Mertons reckon that there has been
a decline in Hamburg stocks of 4000 tons, and say
that, if this is so, the -figures may be considered to
indicate a stronger market. The principal feature
of the month was the big falling off in shipments
from America, which totalled only 18,670 tons, as
compared with 29,581 tons in January. Shipments
from other countries, however, were only slightly
less, and the shrinkage in total supplies was largely
balanced by the falling off in deliveries, which
dropped from 42,390 to 31,717 + ons.
