QUALITY—BUCHANAN WHISKY
“BLACK & WHITE.”
A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, April 17, 1911.
My Dear Arini, —
The record Easter Show was opened with
a corkscrew at the official luncheon. Gov-
ernors, toasts, and top-hats prevailed. Later
on, a magnificent beef parade bellowed
round the Ground. Governor-General Dud-
ley and Governor Chelmsford preceded this
with songs of thanksgiving, served up in
official speeches.
Henry VIII., whose head-chopping compe-
tition created such a scare in feminine circles,
has every reason to regret that his own
funeral took - place so soon. He had
an eye for fat beef, so this 1911
Show would have delighted him. This
reflection is a criss-cross digression.
The Flemish strain, noted in some
of the great, broad-breasted draught
horses, recalled one of Henry’s best-remem-
bered compliments to my sex. “She is like
a great Flanders mare!” Anne of Cleves
contrived to keep her head, but her com-
patriots seem to have lost their tails. No-
thing large enough to whisk away a Royal
Agricultural fly was left to this special ex-
hibit of elephantine build. It must be
ticklish work to persuade one of these huge
beasts to have its tail tied up in a ribbon
coiffure, with a bob, the size of a door-knob,
in the middle.
Bathurst has always been horsey and
doggy. Lees, McPhillamys, and Machatties
have held their own over the fences for two
generations. In years gone by, Miss Kate
Lee, daughter of the late John Lee, of Kelso,
was noted for her fine seat in the saddle. I
think Miss Lee married one of the McPhil-
lamys. The Religious Editor, who knows
most things, is unfortunately backing horses
at Ilandwick as I close this letter. He
might have told me whether Miss Kate
McPhillamy, who scooped the special prize
(amateur equestrienne) at the Show, is a
junior limb of the Lee-McPhillamy family
tree.
Doncaster Day at Randwick brought
black velvet into first place. It didn’t
create any wild enthusiasm in the mere spec-
tator. A grey sky, or artificial light, is
needed to give the velvet gown its most al-
luring air. In the sharp, bright light' of
Saturday’s sunshine, the rich black pile
looked hot, and showed every microscopic
speck of dust. This season’s obsession is
the tailored severity of velvet coats and
skirts. Even the prodigal use of wide silk
braid and other rich upholstery can’t alter
the fact that velvet is a law unto itself. It
has to be worn under certain conditions.
The trailing folds of this luxurious nap be-
long to the pose of the lady who is going to
have her two-liundred-guinea portrait painted
by an expensive artist. Gainsborough and
Vandyck doted on the artistic possibilities of
velvet. They are too dead to rise out of
their tombs and tear the short, narrow velvet
1911 skirt, with its duet of large and pro-
gressive feet, into shreds.
The Society bride has bolted from the old
and dull rule that condemned the newly-wed
to go away and hide themselves amongst
scenic attractions. The trousseau formeidy
worn for the edification of waiters in foreign
hotels is now reserved for home use. The
Australian bride who intends to make a so-
cial splash arranges her wedding within
cooee of a big race meeting. She dazzles
her friends and confounds her foes by ap-
pearing in all the glitter of her new dia-
monds, and the silken garb of the up-to-to-
morrow style in eelskin fashions. Mrs.
Anthony Hordern, in mole charmeuse, with
fascinating fixings, and Mrs. Jack Osborne,
one of the newest brides of this month, in a
pale pastel tone of faint daffodil, with one of
this moment’s fancies in a round velvet hat,
were in the front seats of fickle fashion on
Doncaster Day.
The hat as observed at Randwick is a
variegated fad. It’s either very large, with
towering plumes—seven guineas is consid-
ered a modest sum for this season’s head-
gear—or it’s so exactly like a velvet tea-
cosy that the hidden head might be a tea-
pot, for any proof positive to the contrary.
Many of Sydney’s best-dressed women won’t
put their heads into these absurdities, thanks
to a keen sense of form and color. Mrs.
Horsfall, whose artistic feeling for clothes
has never run off the track, carefully avoids
the candle-snuffer caps.
Mrs. Alex Gordon, in smoke-grey ninon,
showed touches of rose charmeuse under the
cloud-effect tunic. Her hat, rather small
and flat on her head, repeated the smoke
and sunset effect of her frock.
Mole color continues its reign in the most
expensive dress circles. It is dull and unbe-
coming. This is a minor matter in the
strenuous clutching at the odd and ugly,
the phase of this year’s tyranny of tears
for the short and fat. The bean-pole figure
is the only form of female architecture that
now ventures to obtrude its waist-line in the
public eye. The plump can only sigh for
wider skirts.
The short and fat man conceal their suf-
ferings from the cold criticism of the super-
latively thin. But the Itontgen-ray glance
of the fashionable costumiere pierces their
offending flesh like a red-hot pin. One of
Sydney’s “very plump” had an agitating
interview with “Madame” re race frocks. “I
can't wear a hobble, of course,” says Mrs.
Avoirdupois. “Well, why not try a bottle
costumepurred Madame. The client
looked down over her simple self. “No,”
she sighed, “that’s no help. I want
it for afternoon wear. If it were for a
fancy-dress ball, I might take it and go as
‘Bottled Stout.’ ”
The Frank Clarkes and a single sprig of
the Madden tribe—Miss Leslie —are rapidly
revolving round this week’s Victorian visi-
tors’ list. The Ernest Raleighs make a
good-looking race-week duet. Mrs. Raleigh
deserves a tributary ink-drop. She has, up
to date, refused to bag her head in a theatre
cap. Seen recently in the Criterion stalls,
her beautifully-dressed fair hair, with a pink
ribbon meandering through its curls, was an
oasis in a desert of coiffure shrieks. Quite
near it was the latest atrocity. An emerald-
green, close-fitting skull cap—like a bathing
cap—entirely covered the head of an other-
wise elegantly-garbed woman. White caps
of equal ferocity, and daring blue blunders
in the same style, raged round stalls and
un-dress circle. The 1911 Society woman,
with some brilliant exceptions, is apparently
getting her sartorial ideas from Hogarth’s
worst caricatures.
Mrs. Steer Bowker may exceed the line
limit of the ostentatiously slim, but she joy-
fully keeps her youthful cream-satin com-
plexion. On Doncaster Day she was the
youngest-looking matron who side-stepped a
gi own-up daughter into the members’ stand.
Wool is richly represented at the Easter
dress parade. From Yabtree, out Tarcutta
way, came Mr. and Mrs. R. Horsley. Coomo
Coomo, in Quirindi sheep circles, contributed
Mr. and Mrs. J. M. Allison. From Beanbali
station (Coonamble) arrived Mr. W. Will-
cox and Miss Willcox. The Forbes branch
of the McPhillamy family was represented
by Mr. and Mrs. O. S. McPhillamy, of War-
roo. Mr. and Mrs. J. L. Brown, from Bow-
enfels, also trotted Sydneywards and un-
packed their Saratogas at Petty’s Hotel.
Mr. and Mrs. Jack Davies (Scone), and
Mr. and Mrs. Kinleside (from other pastoral
parts), have made an Easter pilgrimage to
this city. The John McMasters, who hail
from Binnia Downs, Mr. and Mrs. Hope
Osborne (Jugiong) ; Mrs. and Miss Charles
Mackinnon (Dandaloo), Mr. and Lady Bea-
trice Kerr-Clark, the Agar Wynnes, and the
(Melbourne) Lorimers have also descended
Avith much luggage on Sydney’s best-known
hotels. Elastic sides for residential bos-
telries in Easter week are the new desire of
prayerful proprietors. Can no budding archi-
tect suggest some new and rapid method of
fitting hotel fire-escapes with portable bed
and breakfast cages?
Included in the Victorian invasion of this
city are Mrs. George Buchanan and her
matrimonial acquisition. Everyone remem-
bers the Aliwa thrill at Flemington a couple
of years ago. The fact that the owner, Who
raced under the name “M. Gordon,” Avas one
of the Robertsons of Mt. Mitchell, was the
social surprise-packet of Cup week, 1908.
Mrs. Buchanan generally follows the cut of
the tailor-made. At one Easter outing,
however, her smartly-built coat and skirt
was guilty of a minor digression. It ap-
peared in conjunction with a smallish hat,
tenanted by a long feather Avith a frivolous
curl in its tail.
The Public Schools Exhibit at the Easter
ShoAV supplied fresh proofs that Squeers
Avas merely before his time. At Dotheboys
Hall, in his regime, “window” became a
hard-boiled fact. It was badly spelt—in hur-
ried moments- —but it had to be well cleaned.
The modern educational diet is an improved
version of the Squeers combination of
mind and muscle. It attacks the Young
Idea in two places. Nature studies intro-
duce the State school infant to agricultural
tools as mental stimulants. He is then
confronted Avith a spade and a bald patch
of earth. He vigorously assaults the verb
“to dig.” CamperdoAvn Infants plunged in
pumpkins, and grew large, aggressive-looking
marrows for the Show. Miss McGregor,
their school marm, enthuses on spade and
hoe drill.
Miss Reilly mothers six or seven hundred
infants (from four to eight years young)
at the Leichhardt Public School. Her ef-
forts to introduce the Australian child to the
history of his own land have made her kin-
dergarten a gathering of incurable optim-
ists. “This bit of the world belongs to
us,” was the Leichhardt baby’s defiance to
foreign foes. It placed this legend over its
school exhibit, in bold, black letters. The
materialised expression of tender youth, this
kindergarten show tracked the history of
N.S.W. in plasticene models, from the first
landing of the white man to the disgorging
of wool and wheat, per railway trucks, at
Darling Harbor to-day. Clancy of the
Overflow, “gone a-droving down the Cooper,”
got his plasticene sheep perilously near a
permanent way of white tape. An up-
country picnic party of white rabbits, tear-
fully contemplating a wheat field, through a
wire-netted fence, was an affecting spectacle
to the man from out back. Pioneer’s hut,
farmer’s homestead, aboriginals with their
hair standing on end (owing to the deter-
mined efforts of Captain Cook’s cutter to
navigate their front garden), a bullock team
dragging a load of avool across the plains—
here was the Leichhardt infants’ challenge
to the Australian imagination.
Mr. Hope Johnston, of the Annandale
tartan clan, set out on a bold adventure
when he founded a Pioneers’ Club in Syd-
ney. Explorers and notable first-aids to
settlement —Captain Cook, John Oxley, Cap-
tain Flinders, R.N., Rear-Admiral Hind-
marsh, Joseph Banks, Surveyor-General
Mitchell, Edward Henty, William Lawson —
were amongst those present, in oils, at the
official opening. In the “First Fleet” con-
tingent, Judge-Advocate Collins and others
scratch a sore place.
Of the Mulgoa Cox family, really nasty
things can be said by men who buy miles of
rabbit-proof wire-netting. George Cox, in
the younger part of the nineteenth century,
seems to have pioneered in pet rabbits.
Writing to a boy friend in the Cobbitty-
llassail autocracy, he was guilty of the fol-
lowing : —-
You said you would like to have a bantam hen,
which I will give you with pleasure. The rabbits
are not doing quite so well as we could wish, but
for all that 1 shall not despair.
Charles Smith’s memory deserves a Moreton
Bay fig-leaf from the Pioneer Club. An
early inhabitant of Wollongong, he is al-
leged to have been one of the first believers
in home-grown Australian hats. He tried
them on his young daughters, who, in the
era of early-Victorian cottage bonnets, made
a dashing departure from imported millinery
by wearing broad-brimmed “cabbage-trees.”
This spirited conduct made an indelible im-
pression on whiskerless youth. “A fashion
new to me,” mused James Hassall, as in old
age he scraped together the ashes of his boy-
hood.
Miss Alfreda Lamb, who is going to marry
Lieut. Ilaire-Foster, intends to gravitate
round Coronation parts. She will leave
Sydney in the German steamer Seydlitz on
April 22. Miss Lamb is one of this city’s
admitted belles. Nature, a spiteful jade to
some of us, gave Alfreda a cream-and-roses
complexion, the correct shade in golden-
brown hair and a sweet temper. This triple
alliance of charms will stay with a step-
sister, Mrs. Tickle, in the Cold-land capital.
The German school of music has perman-
ently captured Lily Delany, a bright girl
whose father, the late J. A. Delany, did so
much for choir music in this city. Berlin
is reported to be Lily’s (or does she now
transpose it into Lillie?) headquarters at
the present moment.
Josephine Marks is another Sydney girl
who means to set a Teutonic trade-mark on
her large soprano voice. Her ultimate des-
tination is an inarticulate German town. Its
name broke up Attorney-General Holman’s
musical monologue at the recent Josephine
Marks concert, and Mr. Holman admitted
he couldn’t circumnavigate its heavy-weight
gutterals. I can only add a tributary tear.
The Australian girl has acquired the
habit of making her presence felt, in art and
music. Miss Bessie Gibson, of Queensland,
is named, with polite approval, by several
Fogland papers. As a miniature painter, she
is accused of adding to clever technique the
elusive art of catching a likeness. The
Royal Academy and Salon have long opened
their sacred portals to Bessie Gibson’s brush
work. The last London mail brought, as
a belated news item, word of her inclusion
amongst artists invited to show their work
at the International Exhibition in Rome.
The first Australian military station for
“wireless” poked a wet nose into a pall of
fine rain on Easter Sunday. South Head’s
R.A.A. contingent achieved this triumphant
rejuvenation of the old Camera Obscura
shanty. Disguised in a coat of new paint,
and its roof neatly patched, its inside hums
with the buzz of “wireless” remarks. An
innocent-looking glass jar, that might do
duty for pickled onions, is filled with bottled
lightning. “Pit-pit-pit” says the electric
spark. The operator reads the message.
Feminine knowledge of air waves reels be-
fore the glass-jar language. It’s as easy to
understand as an early. Egyptian epitaph in
unknown letters. Mephistopheles, Telefunken
and Co. are chief directors of this Easter-
camp attraction.
Elinor Foster, now representing . Claire
Forster in “The Woman in the Case,” is an
unusually upstanding importation. She is
six feet high in her walking shoes, and one of
the Amazons of the mummers’ craft. It’s part
of her stage business, as Claire, to go to the
Devil in a convincing creation in cream satin
with fringed tunic effects. There are the
usual champagne accessories. When H. G.
Wells calls his gentle Margaret (in the “New
Machiavelli”) “a protesting blue flower upon
a cinder-heap,” he head-lines a nature too
fine for its environment. Miss Foster’s
quarrel with fate is as to her size. She
protests she is not too fine, but often too
large, for her environment.
“When I went on tour with Benson’s
Company,” she remarks in parenthesis, “I
was frequently too tall for the parts. With
Sir Herbert Tree’s company—also on tour —
I played Roma in ‘The Eternal City,’ and
thought my fortune was made. It wasn’t,
however! Professor Gilbert Murray’s play
from Euripides gave my six feet another
chance. As a Greek goddess I felt less at
loggerheads with my height. In a recent
American tour with ‘Mr. Hopkinson,’ I had
a very good part, the Duchess. A Duchess
can be tall, so I was quite happy.” On
Friday night Miss Foster, after having her
head banged against the sofa by Margaret
Rolfe at the Criterion, will get into a blue
and silver gown, and depart in peace to the
Union Club Ball.
Miss Eadith Walker’s determined attempt
to introduce Australia at the Nice carnival
included a floral kangaroo. He has been
called hard names by globe-trotters of every
denomination. French writers who men-
tioned the Australian emblem hurled a fresh
accusation at him. They described him as
a calf. The French language gets out of
its depth when it meets anything so pre-
historic as an old man kangaroo.
A small dinner party in Suburbia last
week had the proverbial “bad quarter of an
hour” before feeding time, brightened by the
domestic help. There was only one guest to
arrive. Teams of working bullocks won’t
tear her name out of my inky fingers. Miss
X represents dead languages with distinc-
tion. She is too modest to show an ankle-
bone to a live shoeblack. She is a learned
edition of the . higher proprieties in their se-
verest moods; but she was unfortunately
late for dinner. “Has Miss X come yet?”
asked the hostess. The imported hand-
maiden showed sudden signs of intelligence.
She replied: “Yes, ’m, but she’s just taking
her clothes off!”
Mr. Frank Coffee, whom I mentioned last
week, isn’t a naturalised Australian, though
he is every other sort of Australian that he
ought to be.
Joseph Bernard takefe “Sincerity” as the
name of his new waltz. Paling and Co. pub-
lish it, and Miss Blanche Browne lends a
smiling countenance to the cover. The
“Sincerity” is a melodious and rhythmic en-
couragement to waltzers to go on dancing to
the last chord.
“And others” fills an aching void. It
comes to relieve the strain from which every
harvester of social corroborees must suffer
in blue and inky interludes. “Amongst
those present” has almost departed this life.
“And others” belongs to the higher affinities.
It arrives with a heavy thud, at the end of
a Society dance. Thus —“There were pre-
sent my Lord Fitzbodle, my Lady Flighty,
and others who are unworthy to be men-
tioned in this highly respectable paper,”
Paderewski’s wife is responsible for the
poultry farm intrusion in an elevated career
of classic chords. The French Government
has now set the official seal of its approval
on this exploit in feminine fowl-culture. Ac-
cording to British Australasian, Madame
Paderewski is branded for life as Chevaliere
du Merite Agricole. The Australian wo-
man who runs a poultry farm is content
with much simpler epithets. Her best
friends call her a fool.
A versatile Japanese artist in London,
who hides behind the name Yosliio Markino,
has found a new title for Englishwomen. He
addresses them as “John Bullesses,” in the
English Review. He says“ This is my
home-made English for the Englishwoman.”
Yoshio prostrates himself before the muscle
of John Bulless. He backs up his own
opinion by introducing to print an almond-
eyed friend. “One of my friends has been
all over the world; he says, “Comparing the
English women with others, I find out the
former’s meat looks much harder.” This
naive admiration is tempered by a mild
Asiatic explosion on the low-cut evening
gown. Yoshio agrees—quite unconsciously—
with Mr. Judkins. He considers the Euro-
pean ball-room too immoral for Japanese!
As I close this letter, spasms of rain are
mingling their tear-drops with those of the
Holiday Girl. Her Easter Monday frock is
a limp memory of Saturday’s bright promise.
Yours, with a broad nib,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, April 17, 1911.
My Dear Yarrie,—
A recent official garden party would have been
a colorless function but for the latest Chinese
ambassador, who attended in full dress and
underneath a hat whose red silk tassel suggested
Etna in eruDtion. His tunic, or smoking jacket,
was embroidered with improbable poultry in blue,
scarlet and gold ; while a wide neck-to-knee box-
quilt suggested that he had either recently shrunk
or made provision for future expansion. As
he rustled by, I noticed that the boots he was
wearing beneath all this Oriental magnificence
were the latest European cloth tops. 1 felt
defrauded on detecting the mixture. Does the
educated Chinaman take his fancy-dress seriously,
or does he regard it as a solemn official joke which
does not extend as far as his boots?
The bush-nursing scheme, hatched by Lady
Dudley, having got through the worst of its very
early chickenhood, has just grown a second
feather; the little Countess personally installed
the second nurse at Gunbower on Sunday. Now
the committee sees its way to send a third to
Powlett coalfield. The three are to receive from
the G.-G.’s lady brooches in the design of a beech
leaf to commemorate the starting of the scheme at
Beech Forest.
Ballarat is in the throes of creating a District
Nursing Society. All the leading women of the
South-street city put on their new autumn clothes
and made for the City Hall last week to witness
the launching of the scheme. The energetic
Melbourne District Nursing Society sent up its
most influential members to lend a well-gloved
band. President Mrs. Hood, his Honor’s wife,
limped along in spite of an injured ankle. Mrs.
Alfred Deakin, who is on the committee of the
Melbourne D.N.S., likewise attended. She is
heart and soul in the movement, and Ballarat, of
course, is the Deakins’ second home. Melbourne
Vice-President Mrs. Lang was another who
journeyed up; and the Mayoress of Geelong
accompanied the vice-president of the Geelong
D.N.S. The town which is so falsely accused
of being asleep has had a nurse for the
poor for four years, and its society is
now flourishing. _ Craig’s Hotel entertained all
these visitors during their stay as a special dona-
tion to the good cause. There are two or three
18-carat nurses available to take up the work
directly those in charge of the necessary financial
arrangements are ready.
The Alfred Hospital Fair preparations had
their climax on Saturday at the Exhibition. The
debt on the big healing-house is the cause of the
whole charitable commotion. Governor Car-
michael was to have turned the oratorical key ;
but, unhappily, the genial man developed a sore
throat and had to shut himself up in the house
that turns a pale face to the Yarra and all its
deadbeats. In his absence, the Carmichael lady
made the necessary remarks, and gave those pre-
sent permission to spend whatever money they
had brought with thehi, and anything more that
they could borrow. Gaud Mayor Davey, with a
bunch of violets in his buttonhole, explained the
case for the hospital and how urgent was the need
to borrow a lot.
A number of Druids,'hung with horse-collars of
gold, made a stolid semi-circle on the platform.
Grand President Jones spoke for the “Order”;
and auditor Flack, vice-president of the Alfred
Hospital, voiced the thanks of the debt-burthened
institution. Then the Fair was opened and the
birds began to sing—mostly about raffles. With
her, Lady Carmichael had grown-up niece Ena,
in purple velvet and ermjne furs, and small niece
Mary, enveloped in white.cloth. Mrs. Mayoress
Davey wore white plumps in her hat, and black
garbing. At a convenient moment, a small child,
with a very short frock and a very long curtsey,
passed on- the usual presentation bouquets.
Twenty stalls or so, representing an old street
in an old English town, hold, the loot of months.
Scouts assail one everywhere with books of raffle
tickets, and penny-a-whirl tables block the traffic.
Suburban mayoresses are sprinkled about in
alarming profusion, to head off the Sassiety dame
who has money to spend.
Somewhere in the outer wilds of the Oval may-
pole dances happen at intervals. On Saturday
the dancers marched through the hall in groups.
The Irish colleens looked cute in red petticoats, red
handkerchiefs tied under their small chins, and a
green jacket apiece. Programmes of various
kinds are being wedged in ; but personally I never
could listen to a concert in the Exhibition while a
bazaar was raging profitably at the other end of
the great barn.
The Buchanan-Cumming wedding last Thurs-
day was the smartest and most charming of recent
orange-blossomings. Muriel Cumming, the last
of handsome old Tom Cumming’s daughters, has
always been a favorite, and her proud.' father,
leading a pale and solemn-faced bride .up the
aisle, looked as if he had spent a good ••ynany
mournful hours thinking over the fact thajf her
bridegroom would take her off to Scotland. <jThe
bride was wrapped in so much priceless lace tjjiat
she would have offered a tempting investment to
an enterprising firm of brigands. The court traift,
of her gown was a wonderful web of Limerick, a
family heirloom. It was the end of Mrs." Jack
Cumming’s presentation dress, when that young
lady was Miss May Wragge and made her bow to
the Queen. At this its latest outing Mrs. Jack
Cumming’s daughter Margie was its bearer.
Two other infants followed—Mrs. Gerald Cum-
ming’s daughter Mary and little May Robertson.
The trio were frocked in lavender and white,
with May-flowers in their hair. The other mem-
ber of the escort was a kilted warrior of the
Lauchlan McKinnon clan. There were no grown-
up maids of honor, and the two uniformed grooms-
men—Captain Rome and Guy Madden —had the
limelight to themselves. Bridegroom Captain
Kenneth Gray Buchanan wore the handsome
uniform of his regiment, the Seaforth High-
landers.
The wedding was purposely delayed half an
hour to allow the G.-G. Dudley and his Countess
time to get from the Sydney express to the
reserved front seats. The G.-G. had on a com-
fortable travelling suit of grey, and the Countess
was in cream lace, with hat and parasol to
match. Mrs. Cumming was gowned in black,-
and wore joyful pink roses in her bonnet.
Mrs. Jack Cumming’s dress was a dream
of white Limerick lace. Mrs. Robertson, a
sister of the clan, waved blue plumes in her black
hat.
The wedding was in Scots’ Church, in Collins-
street; and Dr. Marshall, huge and benign, per-
formed the lengthy ceremony. Willy Steele, at
the organ, rolled out “Star of Eve” on the ’cello
stop while the vestry formalities were being
scribbled through. The reception and refresh-
ment of the guests happened at Maritime, Mrs.
Fred Payne's house in South Yarra. .As Hie bride
came out of the church on her way thither a
sunshower descended like a hatful of diamonds.
The Dudleys lent The Bungalow, Sydney, for the
honeymooning, and the Countess sent the bride
off with a beautiful fan, with sticks of mother-of-
pearl.
Instead of the usual shower bouquet, or Early
Victorian posy,” the bride carried one huge
creamy magnolia bloom in its own leaves.
Though Carmichael and his popular lady are
to be wrested from us, Secretary Victor Hood,
who is almost a naturalised Australian, is to re-
main in these parts as Chamberlain to the ap-
proaching Excellency-General. He has a nod-
ding acquaintance with most parts of this con-
tinent, and is welcomed in all the State capitals.
The Carmichaels went to see “The Belle” at
the Royal, and took quite a large party with
them. The circle was packed with those who love
to be near a Governor. The stalls are, nowadays,
the refuge of fashion ; but the vice-regal people
are loyal to the elevated perch, so, on command
nights, Sassiety goes upstairs instead of shivering
in full undress in the cataract of draughts which
pours over the footlights. The Carmichael lady
enveloped herself in black and many diamonds.
The “Belle” is a gorgeous study in hats.
There is nothing necessarily very old-fashioned
about an eighteenth century hat. It is merely,
as likely as not, the hat of the year-before-last—
though, where Society’s hats are concerned, the
space between the present and the year before
last is an eternity. Gertrude Gilliam as the
Belle wears various early Brittany models, from a
dainty cap of lace (the newest thing of 1911) to a
nightmare of an inverted waste-paper basket,
wrapped round with lace and butter-color ribbons.
Victorian artists have been waving valedictory
brushes at Governor Carmichael. His Ex. has
taken a genuine, unofficial-interest in the creations
and even the crimes of local painters. He has
attended their displays with a courage that not
even some of their productions could daunt. He
has talked art with then), often at the risk of
ignoring a buttered social engagement. Occa-
sionally he has bought their pictures ; he has even
commissioned a few when- his Scawtch caution
was dozing. Artists will really regret- his de-
parture. He has been a good patron, without a
prejudice and without a pose.
“ Yes ” and “No ” howled at one another in
the Melbourne Town Hall one night last week,
and left so much political fog on the premises that
it required the full strength of the Nationalßose
Society’s display of perfumed blooms to clear the
atmosphere on the following day.
Experts say this year’s show was poor ; but
to the ordinary flower-lover the petals and per-
fume were exquisite beyond description. There
was no stiffness in. the arrangement of the ex-.
Dibits ; the big hall gave one the impression of a
tangled garden. The society, which is now in its
seventh year, is made up of the most exclusive
and enthusiastic rose-garden owners in Australia.
At ope time, nothing but the bloom was allowed in
the show. Nowadays, however, lady members
spend hours in decorating the hall with greenery,
which serves humbly as a background to the
royally exclusive flowers. The red roses domi-
nated the stands this year, with “General
McArthur” in the lead.
As a result of having rival camps of musicians
on the premises, Melbourne folk are being kept
well supplied with melody. The Orchestral
Society concerted, on a recent Tuesday night, in
the Town Hall, under the baton of J. W. Dawson.
Goldmark’s “Rustic Wedding”—a beautiful
symphony hitherto unknown in these parts —was
the biggest plum with which the programme was
baited. To the orchestra’s accompaniment, F. R.
Collier sang Wolfram’s “Star of Eve” solo from
“ Tannhauser,” and the Toreador song from
“Carmen,” which latter roused the big audience
to encore pitch. But, of course, the audience
which could not be made to sit up and applaud
violently by a good rendering of the bull defiance
might be regarded as so much lifeless clay.
A new golf links at Cheltenham is gathering in
the golfers who have taken up the ancient and
profane game too late to secure house-room else-
where. All the established clubs are mournfully
repelling the energetic person who wants to clout
a small ball for miles and miles and do a little
hoeing in between. Sandringham, where cham-
pion the Hon. Michael Scott practised without
ceasing:, cannot receive another member; and it is
the same story on all greens.
lor women the red golfing coat no longer marks
one off from the non-golfing rabble who stay at
home and putt their own greens on the gas-stove.
Every shop is selling red sweaters and knitted
jackets; and women who would cheerfully use a
brassie to poke the fire with, hurry to and from
suburban shops in the vivid garment which used
to distinguish the haughty female linker.
Margaret Cuthbertson, an energetic woman
who holds the important post of senior woman-
inspector of factories, spends her hours of ease
playing bowls. Bowls used to be considered the
pastime of benevolent old gentlemen ; but the
middle-aged man and the young man found out
that it had a lot of points about it, and started to
compete with their fathers and grandfathers on
the billiard-table greens. Finally women took it
up ; and then they played for the championshin,
which Miss Cuthbertson won. The St. Vincent’s
(South Melbourne) Bowling Club presented her
with a really useful trophy last Saturday—a com-
fortable Chesterfield sofa, on which she can rest
after seeing to the comfort of others.
Fashionable weddings are just now as plentiful
as gorse blooms. There seems to be a furious
competition in silk and lace, bridesmaids and
rose leaves, and all the frocky surroundings'of a
swaggah matrimonial coupling. The orchestra
that soothes the diners at the Grand is thinking
of putting the Wedding March on its programme
every day ; there seems always to be a breakfast-
ing bride on the premises. Miss Hamilton,
daughter of Education-Department Hamilton,
was gaily toasted there on Saturday evening. The
for-better,-or-worse partner was Mr. Cox, of Ade-
t Sixty gu'ests gathered round the groaning
board and relieved it of some of the breakfast—
which, of bourse, was really supper.
A. bride-to-be, who. “parties” shortly at the
tall Spring-street hotel, is Miss Alice Driscoll,
one of Bendigo’s sweet girls. She says “ I will ”
to Mr. William C. Paul, a Sydneysider, on
Wednesday.
At a quiet, business-like wedding at All Saints’,
Prahran, on the 10th, Adeline, youngest blossom
of William Raleigh, became Mrs. Fred Mann.
The groom, a man-o’-law, was one time Master of
the Melbourne Hunt Club Hounds. The three
bridesmaids were blooms from the Raleigh,
Officer and Ryan gardens. The bride comes of a
family of hard riders, and hardly ever missed a
run ; her favorite mount was an old hunter of Lord
Hopetoun’s. Sister Hilda (who was chief brides-
maid) is also a well-known and handsome figure in
chase after the deceived and misguided
hounds. -.J': .. ' j ■
It had been stated that on the completion of
Miss Gertrude Gilliam’s matrimonial undertaking
with Mr. A. C. Lyons, she exist; on the
usual bliss and muffins in a Sydney suburb! It is
now understood that Sydney will not be the
address of the lady of Lyons. After she is safely
married, she will fly to a nest near the British
capital. The retreat is alongside Hammersmith
Bridge, and has the Thames for one of its
neighbors
Fan Dango has returned quietly to Australia
with her husband, and is now playing a demure
squatteress part near Berwick. She wandered
down to Melbourne for the first matinee of “The
Waltz Dream” to applaud Lottie Sargent, who
has succeeded to her old part of Franzi.
The newest Sassiety baby is the Fairbairn-
Balmain acquisition, which arrived in time
for Good Friday buns. The youngster is just now
shouting its orders at Dunraven, the Fairbairns'
Toorak house, but is shortly to be bundled off
to Coolat, to watch the gambols of the station
lambs and grow fat in peace.
..Please don’t think that, because I told you that
Miss Mollie Carew has “ been engaged by J.C.W.
to play Miss Nellie Wilson’s parts,” that Miss
Nellie Wilson isn’t going to play any more parts.
There are, in fact, innumerable parts that Miss
Wflson can play, and play well; but as she can’t
play all of them, .T.C. W. has engaged Miss Mollie
to play some of those that Miss Nellie would play
if she were several Wilsons instead of only one.
Mrs. “Olive Morel!” Kelly and her little
Willie are now pinned to the edge of the London
social whirl. They have secured aflat at Knights-
bridge, where the lady regularly dispenses food
and aerated conversation.
- Miss Asquith Baker is to hold a picture
exhibition in the top room of the Athenaeum after
the MtCobbin show moves out. Invitations are
■flying through the post for the opening on
Tuesday, April 25. After that the multitude
may flock in and gaze until May 5. . ,
Before she left for England, the lately-widowed
Mrs. L. L. Lewis—Rosa Dunn of the Melbourne
stage of the late ’so’s and early ’6o’s—presented a
library of 400 volumes, mostly standard works, to
the women students of Melbourne University.
Among the books is an autograph volume pre-
sented to Mrs. Lewis by Mathew Arnold. The
library will be called the “Rosa L. L. Lewis,’.’
and will be held in trust by the University’s
Princess Ida Club.
Dorothy Humphries, one of the many sweet-
voiced Australians, was appointed chief soloist at
St. Pat.’s Cathedral a week or two ago. She is a
pupil of Freddie Beard.
In the complete electric cooker, women who
are suffering from the prolonged scarcity of
Marianne think they see their deliverance from
one house trouble, at least. People in other parts
of the world where electric power is available are
gradually overcoming prejudices and utilising the
mysterious “juice” for small domestic needs.
Westralia is ahead in this respect. At Kalgoorlie
housekeepers use electricity for more than light-
ing, and deal in units instead of feet. One
Groper lady, who has long forgotten what it is like
to command a maid, tells me she sets her ordinary
alarm clock for a certain hour—say 7. The clock
has an electric attachment with her electric stove,
so that when the alarm goes off the stove glows
under the kettle of water which has been left
ready overnight. By the time the labor-saving
scientist comes downstairs, the morning tea is
simply dancing to be made. The only trouble iis
that a lot of people must be induced to use elec-
tricity in order to make it cheaper than gas.
Talking of Marianne the almost extinct. I met
the other day a wealthy bu t cookless woman who was
on the point of collapse through having sat for
eight hours without refreshments in a registry
office. In that time one hundred would-be em-
ployers of cooks had called in—and exactly tvio
would-be cooks. One of them was a tired woman
with a baby, the other a broken-down factory-
hand. All the time the hungry and hopeless
seeker was_tortured by thoughts of a foodless hus-
band boiling-over at home, and two helpless
gadabout daughters without the slightest kitchen
experience. Soon these unhappy women will
either have to learn how to cook, or else eat their
food raw.
From my Adelaide correspondent
The Adelaide Literary Theatre opened its season with
“The Servant in the House” on Saturday night. The
play isn’t an easy one, but there were no complaints from
the front. Miss Olive Proctor satisfactorily filled the gap
left by Miss Maud Plunkett’s desertion to professionalism.
Lovely woman and her fashions are attracting no end
of attemion just now—and some abuse. F’rinstanee, the
Municipal Tramway Trust has reluctantly, not to’ say
swearfully, arranged matters so that its cais can be
boarded without the aid of a stepladder or hoist. Said
one civic dignitary, when the council received the report
of the innovation: “It’s all owing to hobble skirts.” A
city hotel boasts a bar-lady who wears the harem variety
—in tweed—and has much to say in favor of its comfort.
The S.P.C.A. has lost the services of Mrs. Secretary
Ennis. This active animal-lover first pitched her tent in
South Australia in the days of horse cars, and at once
waged indignant warfare against authorities and passen-
gers who allowed the poor brutes to haul over-crowded
trams along ill-repaired rails. It was hi a luge measure
the result of her efforts that inspectors were appointed
to limit the number of passengers. Her place is to be
taken by the wife of medico O’Leary, of Glenelg. She’s
equally an animal enthusiast, and can be relied upon to
carry through anything she takes in hand.
The Bach Society took possession of the Town Hall on
Tuesday, and gave a worthy rendering of “The Dream of
Gerontius.” Eugene Alderman led the orchestra, and
George Gardiner, Mus. Bae., presided over the destinies
of the organ ; and we’ve every reason to be proud of both
of them.
Saturday’s A.R.O. races were practically a replica of the
S.A.J.G. fixture of the week before—good weather, good
racing, rather medley frocking, and one biggish dividend
to the tune of £2l 7s. Clerics and bookmakers rave in
vain—totalisator investments show a steady, upward
move.
The death of Major Harry Tomkinson opens up some
pages of early-day history. His father, Mr. Samuel Tom-
kinson, was one oflthe old school of politicians and pub-
lic men. He had strong Conservative opinions and an
Old-World punctilious manner, and he made his home a
brilliant social centre. The Tomkinsons were one of the
first to pitch their summer tent at Mount Lofty ; but
the comfortable family home there has been on the
market for some time.
Mrs. de Neufville Lucas, whose husband was one time
in the 40th Regiment, died last week. ' She had totalled
50 years of residence in the Commonwealth.
You’ve already heard about the “ Gay Gordons’ ” motor
smash, doubtless. Luckily, the calamity occurred near a
hospital and a doctor—J. A. G. Hamilton. He’d have a
fellow-feeling for the sufferers, as he was knocked dow n
himself by a motor t’other day. He escaped with nothing
worse than a shaking and a damaged suit of clothes.
Theosophistjs are descending on us in force for a confer-
ence, or congregation, or something, and the result of
their communings will probably provide local divines
with texts for a twelvemonth. Nevertheless, the
’osophy in question is on the increase, and a good many
people wbo don’t profess it cast a curious eye over its
official publications.
The Mooltan threw' Adelaide into a mild fever of alarm
by arriving at the Semaphore with a case of smallpox
board. A message by wireless forewarned the Health
uthorities, who attended in force and ordered the Ade-
laide passengers, numbering- 72, to Torrens Island.
Thirty-six allowed themselves to be bathed in formalin
and vaccinated, whereupon they were suffered to join
their families on the mainland. The rest, including
the ‘'case” —a jockey, from India, named Butters—
were detained. A grim instance of the workings of the
Tyrant Socialism !
Sincerely yours,
JOHANNA.
A Melbourne auctioneer’s advt. concern-
ing a sale at Paseoevale mentions that “this
beautiful valley and surrounding hills were
selected by John Pascoe Fawkner in the early
days of Melbourne’s foundation,” It is a true
bill. Fawkner went out just beyond Essendon,
and took up land, intending to live the life of a
farmer alongside the city he had helped to found ;
but the discovery of gold altered his plans, as it
did those of many others, and he never tickled
the soil thereabouts.

POLITICAL
POINTS
“Ramoth Gilead”: Australians are a
lucky people, according to Alfred Deakin. . the
regulation of wages and conditions of labor on
railways,” be says, “will put the freights and
charges and the number of trains you are to run, all
under the control of men who don t have to con-
tribute to them.” Now, if the people of Australia
don’t have to contribute to the upkeep of their
railways, who does? Do the Japs do it ? Or do
the Hindus send us a yearly remittance for that
purpose, or the Hottentots? Or does Alfred the
Insufferable keep them jigging out of his private
income? Or do they just grow, like trees and
boils? Or what?
IN THE REFERENDUM RING.
With respect to monopolies, Mr. Bruce Smith showed
that no business open to opposition could be called a
monopoly.—S.M. HERALD.

’e's just as willing to take a hit as give

Sydney Telegraph, gleefully enumerating
its ridiculous posse of “No” shriekers, con-
cluded the woeful list with “Mr. B. J. Grogan,
Mr. W. Donnelly, and other members.” If seven
unsuccessful attempts to woo the electors entitle
Mr. Grogan to he dubbed a member, Jock Gar-
land, with his few gleams of success and his
M.L.C.-ship, must be several different brands of
Prime Minister. Likewise the swain who has
hung up his hat seven times to unresponsive
maids becomes automatically a Mormon of high
degree, and the rifleman who misses the target
oftenest wins the King's Prize. Also, the man
who never entered the smoking mine at Mount
Kembla naturally saved the most lives.
When the people who ask the Common-
wealth whether it would be presumptuous enough
to alter the Constitution, “the work, of the
greatest intellects of Australia,” it might be worth
while asking them, in return, to tell what they
know about these “great intellects -Moore,
Henry, Loton, Piesse, Henning Howard Taylor,
M. J. Clarke, A. Y. Hassell, Grant, and Sholl.
That i« 10 and there are 10 other “ great intel-
lects ” that thev might say something about after
they have finished with the other little lot.
There were some great intellects in the Conven-
tion • but if the bulk of the members owned great
intellects they have been forgotten in amazingly
quick time.

Melbourne Argus has been at its old game
of printing under the black heading, “Labor
Rowdyism,” the worst patches of lively Referen-
dum meetings addressed by Tories. This is de-
signed to show how liberty of speech is restricted
by the Rude and Vulgar Party. But vyhat right
has the sootv ARGUS to call the Labor kettle
black? An alleged Labor crowd occasionally
refuses a particularly exasperating No-era healing
by making loud noises; but the Argus habitually
refuses the other side a hearing by butchering
their remarks with a blue pencil.
After all, perhaps, Dealdn deserves pity
more than anything else just now. He has advo-
cated almost every one of the present Referenda
proposals; many of them lie has tried to make
law, but has been blocked by the Constitution.
So, in tlie ordinary course, he would be fighting
for the proposals. But nowadays poor Deakins
Liberal party is only the tail of the lory .ass,
and it has to go the way that Fairbairn, Willie
Kellv and other fat and futile gentlemen drag
it. The voice is the voice of Deakin ; but the
noise is the noise of hee-haw.
The Age is bitterly angry with Fisher for
going to England, classing his iniquity with that of
Tom Bent when the stout statesman sent his notori-
ous batch of commissioners picnicking to a Private-
Enterprise exhibition in London at the country s
expense. Buthf Eisher had declined to v sanction
the sending of a contingent of Australian M s.P.
to the Coronation, what sort of a traitorous wretch
would he have been in the columns of the daily-
press, and how passionately would Australian
electors have been besought to say “No to the
Constitution-reforming proposals of a disloyal
Scotch devil, whose plain intention was to dis-
claim the Throne and disgrace the Person !

Another Australian, J. C. Manifold, a
Western District (Vic.) squatter, has returned
from the Cold Land with a better opinion
of his country and its legislation than be had when
he left it. He talked with many British .com-
mercial men, who admitted
the superlative excellence
of this country’s produce, liiit
declared that they had to
sell the goods to consumers
under other names. Aus-
tralian butter, lie says, is
absolutely the best on the
market ; but it isn’t sold as
Australian. “French roll”
butter is very popular with
J. 'Bull at present —and some
of it Manifold identified as
from ‘Koroit. Similarly with
meat. Retailers who handle
it den’y that it comes from
Australia. In all of which
one sees the natural conse-
quences of two things. The
Stinking Fish party has habi-
tually vilified Australia for
narrow, selfish political pur-
poses ; and, instead of those
squeals being droWned by a
mighty Australian roar, each
little Stale has piped on
its own account. If every
Australian who has Aystra-
lian goods—butter, for instance—to soil had
calleddt Australian, and shouted at the top of his
voice that Australian butter was the best in the
world, the J. Bull customer might have been con-
vinced. But the butter has been “ Victorian,” or
“Queensland.” just as the apples have been
“Tasmanian”; and the forde of the advt. has
beep lost. And the loss is all ;fo be charged 'up to
State Fright and the Little Australians.
Wonthaggi, the township that has sprung
up at the Powlett (Vic.) State coal-mine, probably
enjoys a world’s municipal record. At its first
municipal election the other day nine nominees of
the Political Labor Council were returned for the
nine seats.
ALFRED'S BUSY DAY.
Mr Deakin, when seen with reference to Admiral
Henderson’s naval defence scheme, said he was not yet
in a position to express an opinion upon the Hotter. lie
had been kept so busy with other subjects that he had
not had time to peruse the report. —Daily Paper.
When the Federal Convention which
framed the Constitution met at Adelaide it sat
for its picture. In the front row were Kingston
(president), Baker (chairman of committees), J.
P Abbott, Graham Berry, E. N. Braddon, Reid,
Turner, Forrest, Zeal, Adye Douglas and Brown.
Of that lot Kingston, Baker, Abbott, Berry,
Braddon, Douglas and Brojvn are dead—all that
remain are Reid (out of politics), 1 urner (ditto),
Forrest (out of office and never likely to return),
and Zeal (representative of Fatrnan). ihe “No-
No ” crowd cries : 4 * Hands otf the Constitution .
In other words : “ Abolish self-government, and
-let the dead men rule ! ”
Melbourne Age broke'out into a diagram,
the other day, to show how absurd it would
be to attempt to govern Australia from
Yassberra. It would be as ridiculous, the
diagram showed, as having the head-office
for Timbuetoo it. London. Yet somehow
the Union Bank of Australia and the Bank
of Australasia manage to make a fair success of
things witli their head-offices in London and their
business in Australasia —and Australasia is sevei al
hundred yards further away from London than
even Timbuetoo is. In fact, they make such a
sufecess of things that their dividends are larger
thaii those paid by any other Australian banks.
Kidman, bloated cattle owner and im-
porter of London ’busdrivers, thinks Australia
should welcome the Meat r lrust; and the remark
suggests a reason for some of the frenzied oppo
sir,ion to the Referenda proposals. Perhaps there
are a lot more like Kidman who really hanker
after the Trust, and reckon, if they extend the
glad hand, to get in on the ground floor. Which
might be sweet for Kidman and the others, but
quite the opposite for the Australian consumer.
Kidman, anyway, has announced that “there will
be no more cheap meat in Australia, and that s a
sentiment that will appeal to the Swift-Armor
crowd.

Telkgpaph looked in at the Municipal Fish
Market at Redfern and found Socialism in full
blast. But instead of rushing back to the narrow
office in King-street and feverishly publishing the
fact that the Circular Quay Ferry Tiger was loose
at the Bottle Neck suburb, the unaccountable
sheet remained in the clean, well-lighted building
and looked on approvingly while the Council s
auctioneer sold many baskets of sea-food out of
which Pirvate Enterprise formerly hooked tat
commissions. The only thing which gave the
D.T. any pain was the reflection that a number ot
fishermen, being in debt to their agents, are
unable to send their catches to the Tiger to be
sold. Then it forgot itself altogether, and asked
the Council if it would help the lame dog over the
stile by lending the catchers of fish the money
wherewith to pay off their private liabilities, so
that they might be free to come and trade with
the Socialistic Tiger.
The London Chamber of Commerce has
made a pamphlet of G. H. Reid’s recent address
on “ The Commonwealth of Australia.” lnere
is nothing in the booklet strikingly quotable
except perhaps this, in which the old man who
couldn’t be serious recalls his own old political
tricks :
In politics I do not suppose there are many here who
would advance quite'as rapidly as we Australians have
done. . .. You know there was a time when we were quite
as conservative as you are still —-(Laughter) we weie
always about to be ruined— (Laugh rp r)— just as you are
(Laughter)—but we have thrived w underfill]}'.
And, no doubt, if the Old Man Who Couldn t
Be Serious hadn’t been provided with the com-
fortable job in London, he would have been just
now publicly prophesying more ruin, and getting
ready at the same time to privately enjoy more
prosperity.
Swinburne, of the Melbourne Gas Co.,
says that practically all Australia is in favor of
regulating monopolies; still the people should
vote “No” because of the danger of unification.
This paper has much the same opinion about gas.
True, you can cook a dinner with it, and thus it
might be regarded as a desirable convenience.
But inasmuch as one might also use it to roast his
mother-in-law or a census Collector, it is obviously
something to be immediately abolished.
Has anyone noticed a violent rush of capi-
tal from the country of Victor Emmanuel and
the Pope? The cable says that the Italian
Government proposes to make a State monopo y
of the insurance business, the profits to be devoted
to old age and invalid pensions; and, according
to Fat, that ought to burst up the marriage tie
and drive capital out of the country in one act.

When Mr. Holman was framed, thousands of votes
which went to ttie Labor party last April were choked
off.” In dismissing: Mr. Packer the Labor League turned
awav thousands of men of independent opinion. By
muzzling or dismissing its members, one by one, the Labor
party will, by-and-bye, reduce itself to a condition of
potted solidarity—a company held together hy each
man’s terror of another’s denunciation.— Argus, S/A/JI.
The Prophetess of B ish arose and waved her voice on
high ;
Large boots were on her frantic feet and frenzy in her
eye;
Her face was beetroot red with joy, and as she leapt she
yelled
“ Gorsave the gag ! ” In her right hand her trusty gamp
she held
And swung it splendidly aloft in ecstasy, and cried,
••Freedom is dead!” Her eager grin was really rudely
wide
As she gave tongue in mad delight and wildly screamed,
“ Hooray !
Freedom is stiff, and— why, we've won ten thousand votes
this day! ”
Thus now the Hag prepares to greet new voters with a
smile,
Sheds bogus tears at Labor’s deeds, and shrieks for joy
the while ;
Groans that the cake of Liberty is dough without a
doubt,
And grins that from the wreck there’ll be fine salvage for
the Stout.
Thus now the Prophetess, by faith, sees glimmering hope
ahead,
Since Truth (th y say) is butchered quite, and Freedom’s
fallen dead.
Hist, Brethren, hearken to the Voice the while it. shout's
“ Hooray !
We’re brtter off with Freedom stiff —it brings nntie votes

VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.
IN COMING DAYS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Out of every seven loaves consumed in Great Britain six
are imported. —Recent political speech.
When Britain’s future scribes recount the tale
Of how her glory fell,
They will not sing, till women’s cheeks grow pale,
Of bursting shot and shell;
Nor chant in glowingwords and epic phrase
The pulsing blood-song of some Day of Days;
The listeners will not hearken, rapt and dumb,
While minstrels lift the lay
That tells of marching feet and clam’rous drum
And furious lust of fray,
And how a torrent ran of hero gore,
As erst at Crecy’s field and Agincourt.
Not so ! Not so ! In quite another style
The story strange will run ;
’Twill tell of how a proud beleagured isle
Heard nought of foreign gun,
But bowed her neck and bent her beaten head
Because her warriors lacked a warrior’s bread ;
llow day by day the hostile ships of steel
Made tight their circling hold ;
How week by week there came no welcome keel
With food to stay the bold ;
And Britain’s lands, forgotten by'the’sh'are,
Could give no help when Britain’s board was bare.
And thus Britannia’s “ march o’er mountain waves”
The scribes shall write no more ;
And Famine’s victims, sleeping in their graves
Upon an untilled shore,
Shall witness bear : Not by Bellona’s frown
Did England die—Her Imports Got Her Down !
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
PEACE PROPOSALS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Decent boys are to be taken from their homes and com-
pelled to associate with larrikins. I oppose and abomi-
nate the whole business. £24,000,000 is to be spent to
build a floating defiance of Christianity.— Rev. W. S.
Rollings at the Melbourne Baptist Congress, which there-
upon carried a motion in favor of perfect peace.
Australia doesn’t want to do
These things that make the Baptists buzz ;
She doesn’t want to spend a few
Sweet million pounds on ship and crew ;
She doesn’t care
To fight; but, there,
Suppose the other fellow does ?
We don’t aspire to take Japan,
Nor long to fill her fields with dead ;
But if the brown or yellow man
A raid on Melbourne town should plan,
Would R. assuage
The heathen’s rage
By hurling Bibles at his head ?
Does he propose to gather in
The dour invader, and in prim
And pious tones rebuke his sin,
Or with great sacerdotal din
And holy airs
And roar of pray’rs
To wind up by converting him?
With plague of horrid doubts we’re curst.
The Baptists all our fears to drown
Must get the Japs converted first.
For if the thing should be reversed,
What hope is there
'Twill happen ere
Our White Australia is dun brown ?
VICTORIA.

REGULARITY OF THE BOWELS.
The First Line of Defence Against 11l-
Health.
Constipation is the root of nine-tenths of the
sickness of man and a large proportion of the
sickness of women. It’s a simple tiling of itself,
but, like many simple things, it may grow and
become complicated.
When the bowels are clogged the waste mattei
decays and ferments and enters the blood, and is
carried to all parts of the body, producing head-
aches, bilious-
ness, sleepless-
ness, heartburn,
loss of appetite,
indigestion, neu-
ralgia, rheuma-
tism and various
other ailments
disturbing tl e
heart and nei-
vo u s system ;
and if con-
tinued is
liable to
cause ii •
flammation
of the bow-
els, liver
and ki d-
nevs.
To main-
t.iin a heal-
thy system
the bowels
should operate at least once every 24 hours. This
is one of Nature’s wise provisions which is too
often ignored, and the result is untold suffering.
Women and children are the greatest offenders,
but why such should be the case is a problem to
be solved. _ .
Nature often requires a little assistance, and if
this assistance is given at the first indication much
distress and suffering may be averted. Motheis,
especially, should guard the health of the r
children, and inculcate regular habits from
infancy.
As a family remedy for costiveness, Dr. Morse s
Indian Root Pills have a wide reputation. They
are mild in their action, causing neither weakness
or sickness, and do not gripe.
To overcome constipation, take from one to four
pills regularly until the bowels move daily, and
are restored to healthy action.*

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The A.J.C. meeting at Randwick was
suiced with infinite variety as regards weather.
Saturday was an ideal day; Monday was a cross
between a cow and a deluge. Rain came in -meets
and drifted in waves across the course and the
"•rounds; but, in spite of it, over 20,000 people
tripped out to Randwick in damp tram-cars, and
stood in the pelting rain to watch mud-covered horses
and jockeys pass in a series of processions. And
they called it sport! Saturday was a day of gor-
geous dresses, Monday was a day of macintoshes
and sodden umbrellas; but when the grand dames
got into the shelter of the grand-stands, they un-
buttoned the macintoshes, removed the ponchos, laid
the waterproof rugs aside, and showed a certain
amount of damp finery. The public was said to have
held its own on Saturday—which is matter on which
much doubt exists —but the rain on Monday was a
golden rain for the ringmen, and they, at were
happy. It was a day when the ordinary conditions
count for nothing; a day on which the race was to
the strong anil not to the fleet. Brute strength and
ability to send a shower of mud flying througli the
air while travelling at a fair pace was the thing
that counted on the awful Easter Monday. And that
was a quality as to which the public had no informa-
tion. In some parts of Mtioriland, where the rain
rainetli every day, the backer knows how various
steeds perform under various conditions. In sun-
kissed Sydney, no one, barring a trainer, knows
what a horse can do with heavy going; so the fool-
public, which could not resist the temptation to bet,
was left floundering in the mud, trying to find out
exactly where in the slush-covered procession the
horses were that carried its coin.
The first race of interest was the Autumn
Stakes on Saturday. Some of the salt of horsedom
stripped for this event, including those nags of high
degree—Trafalgar, Flavian and' Comedy King, Lady
Medallist and Eric, Patronatus and Lord' Foote.
Kric had established a hold on the popular fancy
by his win from Adagio at the Hawkesbury Autumn
Handicap the week before; but that was discounten-
anced by the fact that he was not then up • against
anything like the quality he had to meet in the
A.J.C. event. So the public wiped him off the list,
and he went out at about 25 to one against. “Comedy
King, Comedy King, Comedy King!” howled the
public, seething round the peneillers, who blandly re-
marked five to four ON. “What about Trafalgar?”
“Five to two against.” “Right; mine’s Trafalgar!”
As for the Maoriland flier, Lady Medallist, Patrick
Noses was so sure of her inability to set Randwick
on fire, that he opened his heart and let loose at
sevens. For the rest, any old thing ,up to 25 to one
against. Comedy King and Lord Foote got away
first,. and then with a rush lflce a sand-storm out
West, Lady Medallist went to the front and made a
fearsome pace, leading by four lengths past the li
mile mark. Behind her, held well, in hand, thundered
Comedy King, with Eric, the Hawkesbury winner,
in the offing. It was a pretty race. Lady Medallist
increased the pace and the lead, and Eric and Trafal-
gar ranged up alongside Comedy King. At the home
turn Eric dropped back, and Comedy King and
Trafalgar closed up on the flying mare, and they
turned into the straight bunched together. At the
Leger Stand they were running a neck-and-neck race;
then Comedy King, with plenty of reserve power,
drew easily away. Trafalgar came with a great
rush, but the King beat him off easily, and won by
half a length. ,
Still, the fact that in the minor events a couple
of horses carrying a fair swag of public money man.
aged to lead the others home made the fool-public
believe it had had rather a good day. So when
Monday came a lot of it went out with high hopes
that in the bogged course the worst would be as
good as the best, and anybody had a chance. Which
was true enough; but, all the same, the fact led
to the undoing of the public. The event of the
day was, of course, the Sydney Cup, that gilded
plum on which the A.J.C. laid a coating of £4OOO.
No less than eight-and-twenty went out for this
swag of coin, and on that sodden course any of them
was likely to win. But the public, in spite of
rumors of a bad hoof, planked its coin on The
Parisian, against which a cynical ring laid only
threes. At Eric, the , “’Oxbro’” winner, they
bawled 25’s. And Adagio could get 50’s. John
Moore’s Moorilla they offered 100 to one against, till
somebody came along arid worked a commission,
and then he dropped to 60, like a flash. But it
was only the stable. The public had nothing to
do with it, and the stable wasn’t risking too much
on that impossible day’s conditions. So the brute
went out at anything between 60 and 70 against.
Moorilla came to the front at the turn for home, and
led the way from Eric, with Flavinius, Blue Gem and
Mindful. Into the straight they thundered ; but it
was a one-horse race, for Moorilla was increasing her
load all the time. Byplay, which had been the
last of the leading bunch to enter the straight,
came with a great rush, and left Eric practically
standing still. It was a magnificent finish, but he
had come too late ; and though he cut down the dis-
tance considerably, Moorilla landed at the post with
three lengths to spare, with. Eric eight lengths be-
hind the pursuing Byplay.
An amazing feature of the Doncaster was the fact
that the whole of the final acceptors, 30 in num-
ber, went to the starter. The public had planked
its cash on Bright Laddie and Gold Thread, witn a
fair amount on Nuwara Eliya, at sixes, sevens and
eights. Flavinius, with 9st. 71b. up, started at
20’s against. The race itself was a wild jumble, in
which it was almost impossible to distinguish the
movements of the- various, colors. But at the half-
mile post Lager was in front, closely attended by
Gold Thread, Grist and Bright Laddie, with Miss
Corelli, Sunlike and Broadsword leading the main
field Lager was still ahead at the home turn, but
there Gold Thread and Bright Laddie drew up in an
ominous fashion, with Broadsword hovering horribly
on the horizon. It was not till right at the finish
that Broadsword launched his stroke ; then he flashed
to the front, past the galloping Sunlike and Bright
Laddie, who had both left the struggling Lager right
behind. Flavinius came with a mighty rush, but
Sunlike, opening out, drew level with Broadsword’s
girths as the latter swept over the winning line. A
ftne race to watch, but a disastrous one for the pub
lie For Broadsword had started at 12’s, and everyone
knows how few people there must be on a horse
when Christian Noses slings 12 s at Ins name.
The Sydney Cup was a regular boil-over. Appar-
ently no" one but Mr. Moore, who leases and races
Moorilla, dreamed of putting money on the mare , and,
in the face of her wretched showing in the Leger
on Saturday, few men would have wasted acceptance
money on her. But Mr. Moore did and he has
his reward in the shape of a brilliant win of the
richest race ever run in N. S. Wales. The Publ
that had plunged on The Parisian was bitterly dis-
appointed,ls for the five-year-old was just helpless in
the P mud Moorilla had the race practically won a
good furlong from home, where she ' vl \ a
lengths, and increasing her pace as she led. Moorii
is bv the way, full sister to the Maoriland mare
Lady" Medallist, and is by St. Alwyne from D iP lo " ia >
by Medallion. The race was run in 3mm. 36isec.,
the slowest time since 189 a, when . A. bong s
Patronatus crawled in first in 3min. 3 H se j'
Moorilla and Byplay, which ran second, and Jacamar,
wh?ch won the hirst Nursery Handicap, were all
bred at Messrs. Moses’s Combadello stud farm.
Hugh Denison raked in the LlOOO added money
which goes with the Champagne Stakes, his Posadas
winning from 16 others by two lengths, in Imm.
181 sec. Posadas made a splendid run, and must have
unusual strength. About last at the half-way, lie
I started to struggle through the field, going in pursuit
of each in turn with a succession of wonderful
spurts, and passing them all one by one, till, at the
straight, Wilari, the last in front, was overtaken
and feft behind. Posadas started at 16 s, and Bor-
rowsov and Respect carried the public cash Borrow-
sov did, indeed, finish third, but Respect was no-
where. # *
Next Saturday will answer the question
whether Sydney Kemp is a dark horse or merely a
good advertiser, as on that date he meets Harry
Feared, champion sculler of Australia, on the old
Parramatta. Sydney is the son of an ex-champion
of the world, and has been brought up to the sport
from his cradle by one of the best trainers living,
so between hereditary' instinct, special training and
a few other advantages, he should be something
extra-special. But the Pearce followers do not ap-
pear to be worrying, and seem to consider that the
£lOO stake is as good as in Pearces pocket. Ihe
gate is to be divided equally. The pity of it is
that the Parramatta course does not yield much
gate; it provides practically a free show except tor
the comparatively small crowd that patronises the
official steamers following the race. The men who
provide the fun get nothing from the crowds that
swarm the banks.
There is nine years’ difference in the ages
of the two scullers, Pearce being 31 and Kemp 22.
The younger man weighs only list. 71b. to Pearce s
12st. 91b., giving Pearce , the advantage of Ist. 21b.
Pearce has had a somewhat distinguished career in
the racing shell. After being beaten by Barry, the
English champion, he turned the tables on his victor.
He was quite a youth when he accomplished that
feat, however. Among the men he has beaten are
Thoroughgood, “Wanganui” Webb, Mitchell and Day,
the last twice. He also defeated Hagney; but no one
attaches much importance to that. His walloping of
Webb, Barry, and Haekett (of Canada), are, how-
ever, important. His defeats were at the sculls of
Dick Tressider and Arnst, alias Dick; and, there is
a widespread belief that his only superior is the
present champion of the world. Kemp, on the other
hand, has done practically nothing. Certainly he
has mopped up a lot of good things in country handi-
caps of late, and has been side-stepped by some of
the second rank; but a man may be side-stepped
for other reasons than fear. On the other hand, it
has to be admitted that he has improved out of
sight during the past year. If he should overthrow
Pearce he will}' immediately call upon Dick the
Champion to defend his title.

The selectors of the Rugby League in
Sydney are in for a bad time. They are in the same
predicament as the flirt who received from every one
of her 16 lovers and admirers a- dress to be wofn at
the same ball. They have overmuch to choose from.
There was an excess of good material last season, but
the fearsome stampede from the Union ranks has in-
tensified the trouble this season, and there is bound
to be riot and language anyhow they choose. The
old hands consider that they should have a prefer-
ence, and the new ones “trust a bloke will get a
fair deal.” And between those two there is room
for >infinite trouble. The League’s competition opens
on April 28. The troubles of the selectors-are further
increased by the advent of a number of brawny
Maori Sanders, all desirable young men who ate not
backward in pushing their claims forward. Apd the
rush for inclusion in the English-going team is going
to be stupendous.
* * * ■*
The Rugby Union, on the other hand, is
so weakened that it has had to make sweeping geo-
graphical changes, and enlarge the districts enorm-
ously. Sydney District has been wiped out and
annexed to Eastern Suburbs. South Sydney District
remains the same, but the club is said to have almost
disappeared. Western Suburbs.;, has,, been extended
right up to Richmond and Windsor,' and now takes
in the . Hawkesbury Agricultural College. Balmain
has been stretched out miles and miles, and prac-
tically runs up the Parramatta River till it reaches
Ryde and the Western Suburbs boundary. St.
George, which includes the Illawarra line suburbs,
for a time seriously proposed to amalgamate with
Newtown; but Newtown, being a working man s
suburb, felt it required no assistance from the out-
side and preferred to hold on to its own. So St.
George has to do the best it can. Those extensions
of territory will probably produce enough players
to make a decent showing, so it looks as though the
Union will last at least one year longer.
The Union’s troubles were increased somewhat by
the adoption of the “amateur definition,” which was
practically forced on it by the other amateur bodies.
Hitherto the Union allowed men who were profes-
sionals in other branches of sport to play as football
amateurs; but when the Union adopted the defini-
tion ” that chance of roping in professional athletes,
who could be relied upon to be in good fettle, van-
ished. All pros, were allowed up to January of this
vear to make up their minds whether they would
throw up the pro. side of their business or turn lily-
white Most of ’em decided to stay black as ever ;
so the men who will play in the Union competitions
will be all amateurs —lily-white through and through
and cricket is the only sport in which amateurs
and professionals will meet on an equal footing.
How tight a place the Union is in is revealed by
the proposal to abolish “Country Week, the channel
through which the metropolis used to draw to itself
and the chance of glory the promising young country
plavers It is now proposed that the l mon s selec-
tors should visit the country districts and watch the
games on the local grounds and make their selections
accordingly. If will be most unfortunate if “Country-
Week” is abolished; it was a circumstance much
looked forward to by the outside Rugbyites.
* * * *
The South Aus. Football League lias been
resting on the fence. Half of it wants to renew its
present inter-State match arrangement with the Vic.
Association. The other half says it ought to play
Vic. League or nobody. Just at present it is taking
a legal opinion as to whether, being a member of
the Australian Football Council, it can properly
deal with the Ass., which is an outsider. Meanwhile,
it has knocked out the local Baseball' League by re-
fusing leave to the American-game players to have
a match on each big oval during the hour before
football begins. The reason alleged is injury to the
turf —which sounds like an excuse rather than a
reason. Also, an “Amateur Football League” has
been formed. The game in S.A., under a strictly-
enforced District system, is' pretty nearly pure, so
there is no striking need for the new body. But the
League consistently refuses to admit the University
—as not being a District —in spite of precedents in
Melbourne and Sydney and local precedents in
lacrosse and cricket; and this Amateur business is
the ’Varsity’s way of hitting back,
■Hammond, the aviator, is in Sydney, and
at time of writing is preparing to soar. So
far Sydney has had but little aviation.
Houdini certainly made some flights; but no
one seemed to take him very seriously, though
Heaven knows why. Only the bravest of the
brave would have ventured aloft in the terrible
aeroplane he had—one of the crankiest of the
crankiest Wright type. And the fact that he kept
out of Heaven while soaring on the thing is all the
more credit to his skill —or luck. But if Sydney
has been neglected in this line in the past, the future
prospects are fairly bright. Some Yankee fliers are
roaming round the world, and at present are under-
stood to be in Japan, astonishing the little brown
men, and •in due time, if they survive, they will
arrive here, and show us things. But what all good
Australians are looking forward to is the day when
our own fliers, men who will be available for scout-
ing purposes in the defence scheme, will take, to the
air and ride upon the morning calm. #
The advent of Anthony Hordern’s hydro-
plane Kangaroo marks the beginning of a new
era in motor-boating in Sydney. The graceful Fair-
banks liad no chance against the new flier, even
had the propeller not been twisted and battered
on the rocks off Shark Island. Mr. Hordern’s boat
took a little time to get properly going, but when
it got, how did it get! Fairbanks started with a
bound, and all in a flash was 250 yards in front.
Then the hydroplane rose; and, although Fairbanks
was doing 23 miles an hour, the hydroplane shot
past it as though the cedar boat had been standing
still. Kangaroo is listed as a 33-knot craft, and
must have been doing that speed easily when it
passed Fairbanks. But further down the harbor,
against rougher water, the speed was reduced, and
the average for the whole run was 27 miles an hour.
Said that Arthur Davies had not been in his boat
for 12 months till he stepped on board to measure
himself with Kangaroo. Fancy a man having a
glorious boat like that, and going out only once in
12 months! It’s immoral, or blasphemous, or
something. Kangaroo’s next appearance under rac-
ing conditions will probably be on the 29th, when
the 30-foot championship of the club will be con-
tested ; and it will be worth while going a long way
to see this stumpy craft rushing over the water as
fast as an average railway train.
Some English naval officers recently left
their ships off Bassorah, on the Persian Gulf, and
made a motor-boat trip up the Sbat-el-Arab to the
confluence of the Euphrates and Tigris. There the
boats parted, two going up the Tigris, and the other
ascending the Euphrates. Both parties were as-
tonished to find that the'sight of their boats slither-
ing along without sails or oars aroused no wonder-
ment among the natives. It was explained when
the two launches arrived at Bagdad, and the local
club turned out and gave them a hearty welcome.
The clubmen were all Turks and Arabs, and handled
their launches well. The only white men afloat
were a pair of Germans, but the bulk of the motors
were of English and Yankee make. The launch
which ascended the Euphrates had a more adven-
turous run, and got aground several times. The
object was to reach and explore the ruins of Baby-
lon ; but so many interesting places and so much
good shooting were met on the way that time ran
out long before Hilleh was reached, and the party
had to return. #
In Sydney last week, the Tasmanian
bowlers were smitten hip and thigh. N.S.W. first
defeated them by 98 to 83, 114 to 73, and 103 to
77; then Bananaland joined in the trouble, and wiped
them out to the tune of 113 to 77, 131 to 66, a.nd
96 to 82. The last was their best effort in the
series of half a dozen matches. However, the Fly-
speck band left fully satisfied that the - wiping-out
was justified on form, and jbubbling over with ad-
miration for the green swards of Sydney. *
Australia’s fine left-handed cricketer, W.
J. Whitty, had his appendix docked at Albion Park
(N.S.W!) last week. The docking, this paper is
glad to hear, was successful. Frank Laver also
underwent the same ordeal a while back, and doesn t
seem any the worse for the loss. And The Bulletin
is grieved that Edmund Fisher, the Ancient In-
habitant of its Melbourne office, lias had to leave oil
writing sporting and dramatic paragraphs ioi a
while, and go into hospital to be cured of the same
’ids. ; *
The Commonwealth junior cadet team
left the rest of the Empire out of sight in the
struggle for the Meath rifle-shooting trophy, with a
score of 1266. England, certainly, was close up
with 1259 ; but the others were nowhere. E. Price,
a South Melbourne youth, who captained the side,
also gathered in the trophy, for the highest points
throughout the competition; and Australia, there-
fore, takes all the glory surrounding tlie contes-
Maoriland’s figurehead, Islington, is some-
thing extra at tennis, and has been winning games
in Wellington with that well-known crack player,
Miss Nunnelly, as a partner.
If Clarence Weber was merely out for ad-
vertisement in his challenge to Jack Johnson, he ha*
struck a snag, for “Showy” Baker, of Sydney, has
offered to take Massa Johnson’s place and meet the
Melbourne Adonis in an all-in scrap, gouging and
biting only barred. It would be a wildly exciting
contest; and as both men are gentlemen and thorough
sportsmen, it would be carried out with as little
brutality as such an event could be. In fact, it is
safe to say that, with these two as principals, there
would be as little fear of anything as there
would be with any two athletes in the world. But,
all the same, there is not much chance of the thing
coming off. The mere fact that in the interest of
fair play Baker would have to be allowed to wear
skin gloves only, means that’ the police would pro-
hibit it. So, if it is only an ad., Weber is on fairly
safe ground.
* * * *
“Boshter Bill" is a champion once more..
He recently took a first prize with some sort of
wheat grown on his farm on the Peel River. He has
evidently turned his boxing glove to a plough-share
to some purpose.
Young J. G. Walsh put up a glittering
marksmanship record at Peak Hill (N.S.W.) last
week, when he scored 31 consecutive bulls -eyes at
600 yards, in semi-darkness. This phenomenon is
only 19 years of age, and for that reason should be
a fair-sized beam in the eyes of other riflemen for
quite a long while yet.
Fight followers are interested in the pro-
gress of one W. Sonter, a Northern Rivers (N.S.W.)
20-year-old pug, who has wiped the floor with 20
opponents in 12 months. Sonter is now coming to
Sydney to try his glove among the bigger fry, and
there are some who regard him as “the Hope. He
is a mighty hitter, and at Inverell he biffed someone
into unconsciousness for a whole five hours.
* * * * '
“Snell”: I see that Clarence Weber has
chosen Mike Williams as his opponent in a grand
public contest between wrestler and boxer, '-fai-
ence’s advertised yearning was to demonstrate which
of the two popular forms of athletics is the bet-
ter for purposes of self-defence. A vital struggle
between himself and Michael Williams,- Esq., wm
prove nothing except that Mike is an elderly, heav.i -
footed, slow-going, fourth-rate boxer ; and those facts
were already as obvious as a red brewery. Webei
is the best thing in “rasslers” that we have in
the Commonwealth, and for a fair-dinkum try-out
between the catch-as-cats-can man and the exponent
of plug it would be necessary to fit him- with a
better heavy-weight boxer than we have in Australia
to-day, whereas Williams is many removes from our
available best. If Clarence downs Michael, the
contest will serve no purpose but to shattei the
foolish opinions of those few who believe that a
rather slow, old, unscientific boxer, hampered with
Marquis rules, is good enough for a very clever,
quick, strong young wrestler, allowed all the devilish
inventions of jiu-jitsu.
