Jack Johnson, who was in the jug for
motor-hogging, was released at sunrise on Easter
Sunday, and was in a skate of feverish delight
thereat. He didn’t like the- jug. Although he is
champion bruiser he is a diplomat and knows how
to manage the opposite sex. The fust tiling he
exclaimed was that the only fly in his ointment was
that he would not be able to get to his mothers in
time to eat some of her buns and pumpkin pie. A
man who can write like that froni behind a prison
wall is bound to be popular with the negresses.
p.g,—On second thoughts Johnson isn t a diplomat.
This yarn was sent to his mother, and not to his
wife, and no doubt by this time he has discovered
that the publication of that letter to his mother
is regarded as an unfriendly action, and a slur on
his wife’s cooking. A man’s first flattery is due
to his wife.)

His mother she lay cold,
As in the grave behind the house she lay ;
And I put him then in train,
Though people said I was insane

“ Gomer ” : “ Death isn’t always the pur-
suer” (B. 30/3/’ 11). Here is a case in point. I
once saw a crowd of excited Connaught people on
the wharf at Londonderry. The women were
weeping pitifully, and the men were in an almost
dangerous state of frenzy. Upon inquiry, I found
that the folk were a batch of intending emigrants
who had booked for the U.S. by the Montreal
S S Co’s Anglo-Saxon, but had arrived too late
to catch her. The vessel was never heard of
again. These people were thus unwittingly be-
wailing the fact that, though they had chased
Death hard, he had given them the slip.
“8.N.F.,” from N'Yark :
In the wav of railways the business-like Yank knows
lust how to do things. He gets Private Enterprise to
run his lines, and then creates Public Service Commis-
sions to eternally chase the Private Enterprise and im-
plore it to provide shelter sheds at the depots, lower
fares safer crossings, better lighting, more rapid tran-
sit punctual and more reasonable freight carriage, etc.
One example occurs at my door. At a popular New
York suburb the passengers are frequently herded, like
sheep, to a wicket gate, the only entrance to the whole
train, and compelled to await turns to scramble up steps
at least twice the height of those on a Sydney tram.
Then follows a ride of 13 miles in dimly-lit cars, 35 cents
(Is. s*d.) single, 70 cents (2s. lid.) return. The writer
has moved about a bit in the States. There are some
fast trains in parts, but few and far between ; and when
I he gets home he will have learned to chasten a growl
1 even at the Pinkenba-Brisbane express.

The Missing “Parisiana
»
HER FATE REVEALED
“The missing steamer “Parisiana” has been abandoned
in the Indian Ocean in the grip of a great fire, and by
now is a charred and buckled ruin reposing on the ocean
bottom.” —Daily Papers, 21st Jan., 1911.
On the “Parisiana” there were 183 of our famous “Bettendorf” Farm
Trucks—a large portion of them sold for early delivery.
The shipment to replace the trucks burned with her will—per cable advices
—arrive here about April 25th. .
By that date many of our original customers will have supplied their
requirements elsewhere.
It will take a fair sized warehouse to store these 183 i rucks, and we are
already short of store room.
Our inventory bassoon to be taken, and we must reduce stock before that date.
To do it—these reductions to those who will take delivery from April 25th
to May 31st 1911: —

TEN SPECIAL AMBEROL RECORDS ALMOST FREE
MANY people have been under the impression that if their Phonographs were equipped
with Amberol Attachments so as to play Amberol Records, they wotild be unable to
play the Standard (two-minute) Records which they already have. That impression
is an erroneous one. If you equip your Phonograph with an Amberol Attachment you can
play both Amberol (four-minute) and Standard (two-minute) Records, and you can instantly
change from one to the other by a simple process.
Now here is our special offer : With every purchase of an Amberol Attachment, ranging
in price from 26/6 to 43/-, according to the style of Phonograph to be fitted, the purchaser
receives 10 Amberol Records. These are Special Records, confined, exclusively to this offer,
and cannot be secured in any other way.
We are making this offer simply to acquaint Phonograph owners with the advantage of
having the Amberol Attachment on their instruments, thereby doubling the entertainment
that the Phonograph will give at a slight additional cost, and at practically no additional cost
for the first Amberol Records they own.
Call on the Edison dealer nearest you to-day and ask for the special folder giving details
of this offer, also list of the Amberol Records which cannot be obtained in any other way,
or write us direct for a free copy.

IN BANANA LAND
The idiocy of proclaiming Saints’ Days as
public holidays has now gone so far in
Queensland that on St. Patrick’s, St.
George’s and St. Andrew’s Days all the
Government offices are closed, and public
business is suspended. Naturally Civil ser-
vants are rejoicing, and some of them want
to know why the patron saints of Jersey,
Guernsey, Alderney and Sark are being so
shamefully neglected.
Queensland Government journals are dis-
covering that Deputy-Speaker Armstrong
is not a fit and proper person to fill the
shoes of the late J. T. Bell, and that Blair
or Douglas (Independent Oppositionists)
would occupy the Speaker’s chair with
credit. The meaning of which is that the
Denhamites have lost one seat (Rockhamp-
ton), and anticipate losing another (Dalby),
and are preparing to restore the balance of
power by caging any bird that is likely to
be a nuisance if it isn’t captured. Arm-
strong’s opinion of the manoeuvre would
make interesting reading.
“Politicus” on the old dispute:—
Thirty-six years ago I was at a meeting in Mary-
borough (Q.), held to protest against immigration,
and the speakers trotted out precisely the same
stories about the numerousness of the unemployed, as
“Whipcord” lately told. If the leading spirits at
that meeting had had their way, immigration into
Queensland (population at that time, about 100,000)
would have ceased right there. “Whipcord” asserts:
“The Bananaland Labor party is not against ‘white
defenders’ coming out. It welcomes them because it
knows they are badly wanted.” Yet a leading plank
of the Queensland Labor party’s platform has been
for some years—“ Stoppage of State-aided Immigra-
tion,” a plank confirmed by the last Townsville con-
vention. Does this look like welcoming “white de-
fenders” ? Out of 100 immigrants landed from the
Essex the other day, five were nominated (i.e.,
largely assisted), 89 were “assisted,” and full-paids
numbered exactly six. The Labor party is at liberty
under the present platform to welcome the six. In
the two years 1909-Y0 12,046 “souls” landed in
Bananaland, of whom no fewer than 10,421 were
liberally “assisted” by the State, or something like
86 per cent, of the total. Under the Queensland
State Labor platform, these 10,000 “white de-
fenders” would have had no financial aid at all, and
would have been lost to the State. If Queensland
can only absorb 6000 immigrants per annum as at
present (about one to every 110 square miles),
there must be a screw loose somewhere; but if it
is going to block 86 per cent, of that 6000 from
coming—-well, it’s Heaven help us in the future.
There is only one good title to any country, and
that is “effective occupancy,” and any party that
hinders “effective occupancy,” whether it be the fat
land monopolists or a misguided Labor party, is in-
flicting a vital injury on the Commonwealth. Labor
is coming into power in these States, and it can-
not be allowed to burk our most important problem.
I have no sympathy with the present ruling party
in Queensland, and I see quite clearly that that
party’s onlv hope of retaining power in the State
lies in the chance that Labor may make some car-
dinal blunder in broad national policy. And Denham
and Co. believe the Labor party’s rock will be
the Immigration Question. Wherefore the sooner it
reconsiders its official view on that question the
better will its chances be of sailing into the port
around which it has been hovering for years.
“Ecla” blames Eve again:—
This “living white” is just plain rot! I’ve lived
in most towns in North Queensland, and the bigger
the Labor vote the better chance an alien has to
grow fat. Croydon, Cloncurry and Ravenswood send
Labor men to Parliament, and have Chow bakers,
every one of them. Further, there’s not one pub in
50 in North Queensland with a white cook. And it
isn’t the Tory that keeps them going, either. In a
fight for early-closing on Croydon, writer helped to
picket an Assyrian crowd who wouldn’t close at 6
o’clock; and of the would-be customers whom we
interviewed there wasn’t one person suspected of
being a Tory voter. One woman, whose husband is
still a prominent light in the Labor movement, I
turned back three times. I’ve got her name ready
for anyone who calls me a liar —and he can have my
name, top, if he wants it. Likewise, the Hindoo
hawker is a regular caller at these Labor men’s
houses; and they don’t call if they are not wanted.
It isn’t the men’s fault; it’s the women’s. They
handle the money in the working-man’s home —and
they like the yellow beast. I know ; I’ve had a row
or two about it. I’m sitting on a chair now that a
Chow re-caned last week.
A SEASONABLE REPROOF.
MR. PUBLICHOUSE (with bitter sarcasm): "P’raps yer’d like an' ’am san'wich?"
MR. LOCKSLEY BUMMER (who has accompanied three pennyworth of beer with a bob's
worth of tinned fish from the counter-lunch): “ Not in Lent, my friend —not
in Lent. ”
Some of the Manchurian Russians recently
imported into Queensland take the palm for
toughness and stolidity. Recently a South
Queensland special train ran into one of
them who had gone to sleep on the line.
A discriminating cow-catcher picked him up
and heaved him aside with haruiy a bruise.
The condition of the special, however, be-
came the subject of departmental inquiry.
Bananaland’s Government has decided to
popularise the North by catering for
“sportsmen”—that is to say, people who
want to kill something. Trout ova has been
placed in some streams about Herberton,
and English pheasants are to be let loose
in the timber country. Gatton College—
the State agricultural institution —is at
present busy trying to hatch pheasants for
the other part of the murderous job. And
when it’s all done, the trout and pheasant
will attract the fellah who will be likely to
leave the beastly country when his holiday
is up, and write to the papers about the
beastly heat and the need for cullahd la-
bah.
Room! Room!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
From the acres broad of the virgin West —
“Room for the White Man, Room!”
From the tender green of the mountain’s
breast—
“ Room for the White Man, Room!”
The long Downs cry and the prairies call,
The big gums wail as they lurch and fall,
And a song goes up from the hilltops tall —
“Room for the White Man, Room!”
On ev’ry mile by the sea-beach lone,
“Room for the White Man, Room!”
On ev’ry peak that the brown hawks own,
“Room for the White Man, Room!”
From the meadows fair, and the Westland
sere,
From the ridges clothed by a rain-rich year,
The Voices rise: “Will the White Man
hear —
‘Room for the White Man, Room’?”
When the big guns boom by the Northern
Gate —
Doom for the weak race, Doom!
Ye will call for a fighting band too late
(Yet is there Time and Room).
In a few short years by the Austral main
In a death-grip locked with a heathen train,
Ye may call for help, aye, and call in vain—
“ Room for the White Man, Room!”
QUEENSLAND.
F. B.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, April 15, 1911.
My Dear Tiiargomindah,—
The Macgregor chieftain, whose jaunt
of 5000 miles to Cloncurry, Thursday
Island, the islands of the Gulf and other
parts of the balcony of Australia was de-
layed by dengue, had thrown off the illness
sufficiently to depart by the St. Albans.
The rest cure is expected to tune him up
for the local outbreak of ornamental triviali-
ties, without which no Coronation is com-
plete.
Talking of rest cures, Mrs. Mayoress
Diddams has gone to Collinton station to
fortify herself against the expected strain
of the Coronation ball, at which much gold
is to be sought on behalf of the hospital.
In a frantic effort to escape from the
combined attack of a motor-cycle and a
tram-car, I capnoned into the arms of Mr.
and Mrs. (Akenehi) Macleod in this city
on Wednesday morn. “What are you doing
here?” I demanded sternly. “Why aren’t
you trailing yards of orange blossom and
sprays of satin up a Sydney aisle, or having
a reception, according to the published
plans?” But the published plans were
wrong; the wedding happened on Monday,
and the Canadian mail brought the pair as
far as Brisbane, where the St. Albans called
in for them this morning.
Departers by the new Aberdeener, Them-
istocles, on Thursday, included the W. H.
Campbell party. Mr. Campbell signs the
cheques in the Western Champion office,
and Mrs. C. narrowly escaped the president-
ship of the Pioneer Club, which died sud-
denly.
Touching a mention, in Vandorian’s letter,
of the valuable industry of silk growing,
promoted with such vigor in this city: Mrs.
South, the prime mover, is a capable old lady
who, with no axe to grind, has made consid-
erable sacrifice to get things started on
proper lines. She has expended much cash and
time in taking attractive exhibits to coun-
try shows, so that the children might be
induced to give their spare time to the worm
that turns out soft silk and brings in hard
cash. Captain South, the lady’s husband,
trod the quarter-deck of many an A.U.S.N.
ship in the old days, but left the coasters
and wore the gold-laced garment that goes
with the command of the Government yacht,
till, a decade back, he began to take his
ease ashore. So far the silk-culture com-
mittee has received no help from the Govern-
ment, and although I’ve had my ear turned
in the direction of the Treasury, I haven’t
heard anybody even threatening to count out
tuppence.
Mrs. John Lemmone is in town resting
while John is nailing together the arrange-
ments for the coming Melba opera.
To-day the local wing of the army has
gone under canvas, and on Sunday Lytton
will be crowded with folk to see the guns
fired and the horses fed. There are also
Toy Scout camps to the right and left of us,
and the neighbors are in a state of frenzy
for fear of somebody being scalded at the
cook’s galley, or a “didn’t-know-it-was
loaded” episode.
Miss Edith Dickson, after two years’ trip-
ping in Eurone, has come back in the pink
of condition, even though she was in Italy
at the height of the cholera scare.
Mailbags and other unmistakable evidence
of the fate of the Yongala continue to come
ashore, but there is still no trace of the miss-
ing hull, and a vessel lying round in the
shallow waters of the North, inside the Bar-
rier reef, is an added danger in a locality
already overstocked with the commodity.
How about the naval authorities sending
along a survey ship to look up the address
of the wreck? It would be a rest for the
boys after their Easter exertions.
The death of Rev. T. Warham (“Lionel
Lit” of the Courier) is a distinct loss to
the literary community of Queensland. He
was an epicurean book-taster, who spoke
his mind, but always with the restraint of
kindliness. He was another sufferer from
dengue, and in a state of high fever escaped
from his bed and wandered into the ad-
jacent sea, where the shock of the cold
water was too much for an already weak-
ened heart.
Two other sudden passings have occurred.
A. F. Ulcoq, manager of the South Bris-
bane branch of the Q.N. Bank, who dropped
out on Sunday last, was a delicate man who,
in his many years in the Nor’-West, con-
tracted fever and other ills. He married a
relative of H. Rider Haggard, and was a
general favorite. Philip McMahon, still
another to succumb to dengue, was for
many years Curator of the Brisbane Botani-
cal Gardens, and held the position of Direc-
tor of Forests till he died. lie was a
man of weirdly theatrical appearance, but
great learning in his specialty. Mrs.
McMahon was a Miss Hendy Pooley, of
Sydney.
How would you like, my dear, to spend
28 years in a lighthouse—not six light-
houses, or even two, but just one single,
solitary, same-old pile? For myself I’d get
“the streaks,” like Kipling’s friend; but
Mr. William Picken, who returned to Bris-
bane this week, after spending that length
of time on the light at the lonely North
Reef, seems none the worse for it. Mrs.
Picken, who accompanied him, has been on
the reef (which is only 800 yards in length,
and seldom visited by a ship) for the past
four years. The lightkeeper had also two
assistants, and the only break in the mon-
otony of their lives was a month’s holiday
per year. Mr. Picken, who is 70 years of
age, has now retired from the Marine De-
partment’s service, and will spend the rest
of his days nearer to the clang of life.
Yours affectionately,
MARIO.
For every morsel of land offered in Central
Queensland there are a hundred sets of
teeth waiting to close on it. Barcaldine
had a Land Court the other day, when no
less than 106 tendered for a/ 12,000-acre
block, which was won by an applicant who
offered over 4d. per acre. At the Long-
reach Court there were 184 tenderers for
three blocks—two of 10,000 acres, and one
of 20,000 acres. It’s mainly the lure of
artesian or sub-artesian water that has
brought land-hungry southern people to these
parts, -v,'

TO every man with good red blood in his veins the free, healthy, open
life of the country must stand out in marked and favorable contrast
to that spent amidst the drab and cramped surroundings and
monotonous routine of the office. Many men who are slaves to the desk
would gladly take up land if they had the means to do so, and if they
could avoid the physical hardships of the pioneer. Many would gladly
embark their sons on this career if they could do so at a reasonable outlay
and with a certain prospect of their ultimate success. It is for such as
these that we afford the preset opportunity of acquiring WHEAT
FARMS ON EASY TERMS. A very moderate outlay of capital is all
that is necessary. The blocks we offer now in many instances have
been under cultivation, and the remainder can be made ready for the
plough at an expenditure of about ss. per acre.
Homesteads have been erected in many instances, and tanks and dams
are ready for immediate use.
The reputation of the soil for wheat-growing is famous throughout
the State, and mixed-farming and stock-raising flourish in the district.
THERE IS BIG MONEY IN WHEAT-GROWING, and many
have found it a short cut to wealth and independence. The demand for
wheat never let's up —the market is always asking for more j and if you
work with a will you cannot help but win success.
RINGWOOD.
RINQWOOD ESTATE is amongst the best
of the land in the Parkes-Condobolin District,
which produced last year over two million
bushels of wheat, and this season will show a
further increase on that great total. The wheat
produced in this district is of the finest quality,
and is much sought after by flour-millers. The
Estate consists of 17,120 acres of beautiful
chocolate loam, capable of growing an immense
crop. It has been subdivided into 30 blocks of
about 500 acres each. The property is fenced
in rabbit-proof netting, and many of the blocks
are practically complete in this respect.
RINQWOOD is some six miles from Ootha
Railway Siding, by good road, and half-way
between Bogan Gate and Condobolin, the ter-
minus of the line. Some 3500 acres have been
under cultivation. The balance is either open,
park-like land, or is lightly timbered. The trees
have been ringbarked for many years, and the
land can be cleared for the plough for about ss.
per acre. SOME OF THE BLOCKS HAVE
HOMESTEADS, AND ARE READY FOR
IMMEDIATE OCCUPATION.
THE RAINFALL is ample for successful
wheat-growing. Records taken at the home-
stead during the past five or six years show that
the rainfall has averaged about 20 inches. The
Estate is well served with tanks and dams, no
fewer than eleven of the blocks being provided
for in this way.
THE TITLE.— The land is to be sold as
certificated conditional purchase, all conditions
fulfilled, and buyers may convert into Freehold
at any time. You get immediate possession,
and have all the advantages of a conditional
purchase WITHOUT A BALLOT.
The prices of this land as conditional
purchases range from 275. to 89s. per acre ;
or Freehold prices, including all moneys
due to the Crown, 465/ to**96s. per acre.

AT SUCH A LOW PRICE?

Mr. W. S. KEAST, M.L.A., of Dandenong,
Victoria, was interviewed recently by Sydney
Evening News reporter, on the question of
Land Settlement. Among other things, Mr.
Keast* said :—“ In the Parkes district for
hundreds of miles, around such centres as
Forbes, Bogan Gate, Condobolin, etc., there are
millions of acres suitable for wheat farms. The
country is practically all cereal land, and adapted
for mixed-farming, with sheep-breeding. In
parts of Victoria, where the rainfall is 15 inches
(Ringwood averages 20), under dry-farming
methods we average 16 bushels of wheat to the
acre, and this district should produce more
than that.”
This is Convincing
Enough!

THE FOLLY OF DRUG
TAKING.
Do not take tonics for nervous breakdown.
There is only one way in which nervous breakdown
can be repaired, and that is by feeding the brain and

The Inky Way.
Despite differences of opinion as to the handiest
and most cogent methods, the pressmen of the Com-
monwealth are steadily combining to ask the news-
paper proprietors what they intend to do about long
hours and short pay. The Westralian men are the
latest to arise and demand “Whaffor” through the
medium of a newly-formed industrial union, which in-
cludes all the pressmen on the Perth dailyand weekly
papers. Having registered under the State Arbitra-
tion and Conciliation Act, they presented the pro-
prietors with a schedule setting out the desired im-
provements in working conditions and rates of pay.
The reply they got was that the time was not ripe,
or that Capital would be driven out of the country,
or that the constant harrying of commercial enter-
prise was becoming intolerable, or something. But
the pressmen declined to let the argument go at that.
The only fear they have is that the proprietors will
import men from the Eastern States to confuse the
argument, and all decent journalists are hereby urged
to stand clear. There are no other journalists.
* * * *
“Irish”: Has ever a one of yez ever reported a
trial from the dock —'I don’t mean as a prisoner,
but as a pressman? I did once, in Ireland. I had
been sent out to do an important case at Philips-
town (King’s Co.) Quarter Sessions, and found that
the only accommodation was in one compartment
of the large dock in the well of the Court, where a
small table had been placed. I was just getting as
comfortable as possible, when old Judge Curran
spotted me and stopped the proceedings. “What!”
said he, to the County Surveyor, who was custodian
of the building. “Is ut in this manner,” said he,
“that the Press is to be trated in my Court? What!
Wh-a-a-t!” Then, turning his rolling eye on me, he
said: “Come out,” said he, “and for the name,” said
he, “and the fame,” said he, “of the Press of Ire-
land,” said he, “sit by me side!” And I did, with
becoming dignity and calm.
“The Philistine”: I hailed T. D. Mutch’s an-
nouncement of the establishment of the Australian
Artists and Writers’ Union with joy, and fancied
that, on the union’s proposed affiliation with the
Trades Hall, the end of the trials of inky-wayfarers
would be reached. “Hassan” then gave the en-
couraging intimation that reporters on the Mel-
bourne dailies get six golden quidlets weekly.
Mutch extolled the virtues of his union, and quoted,
with much gusto, the Journalists’ Union of Broken
Hill as the first in Australia, and also alluded to the
Barrier Daily Truth. That particular brand of
Truth is owned by the Trades Hall; consequently I
expected that “Hassan’s” six quidlets would sink
into insignificance compared with the salaries drawn
by journalists in the employ of the Trades Hall.
But what do I find? For a start, this advt. in Mel-
bourne Age: “Journalist Wanted, Used to Cables,
Salary, £4. Apply by letter, with copy of refer-
ences, to Editor Barrier Daily Truth, Broken
Hill.” Truth itself adds: “Experienced man only;
no amateurs or learners.” I called in at the Age
office to see if the figure “1” had not been dropped;
but I was disappointed. I have since learned that
the Broken Hill Council (also bossed by the Trades
Hall) pays its dirtmen 13s. a day. For an “exper-
ienced journalist” it proposes to pay 11s. 6d. per
day. After that I became afflicted with doubts
about the virtues of affiliation with the Trades Hall
—at Broken Hill, anyway.
Catling, the time-honored editor of Lloyd’s
Weekly (London), has just given forth a book of
Anecdotage. He was born with a mechanical turn. As
a very small kiddy he went to see an execution;
then, coming back, he made an exact model of the
gallows, and hanged his sister’s doll. So a potential
Nosey Bob was lost in a mere editor. Sixty years
ago, ‘Llovd sometimes didn’t know where the money
was coming from to fetch out the next week’s paper.
He was seized with the idea of putting on the first
page “Edited by Douglas Jerrold,” who then had a
huge reputation. Jerrold took a thousand a year,
and took it easv. After his demise Lloyd found it
payable to print “Edited by Blanchard Jerrold,” who
took the money, and took it a good deal easier.
Catling, a regular employee, only saw Blanchard at
the office twice in 18 years! But his name was good.
PANTOUM OF A SUB-EDITOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Where the devil is Jones?
See if he’s back? What’s the use!
If that ass Bletherby ’phones,
Tell him to go to the deuce!
See if he’s back. What’s the use
Having such fools in the place?
Tell him to go to the deuce. . . .
Idiot missed the last race.
Having such fools in the place,
Damned if it don’t make me sick ;
Idiot! missed the last race.
Get those blanked cables through—slide!
Damned if it don’t make me sick. . . .
Murders ? The world’s oozing blood !
Get those blanked cables through slick,
Brown, or we’ll be in the mud.
Murders ? The world’s oozing blood !
They’ll have to watcli the blanked Turk,
Brown, or we’ll be in the mud.
(Jimmy, this blanked pen won’t work.)
They’ll have to watch the blanked Turk ;
He’s out for trouble, I think. . . .
Jimmy, this blanked pen won’t work.
How have you goosed the blank ink ?
He’s out for trouble, I think:
Outragin’ Christians —-well, well!
(How have you goosed the blanked ink?) . . .
Exeter Hall will play hell.
Outragin’ Christians —well, well,
There’s no accountin’ for taste.
Exeter Hall will play hell —
Civilisation’s disgraced.
There’s no accountin’ for taste.
Damn it, that Jones is a sot!
Civilisation’s disgraced. . . .
Best blanky race o’ the lot!
Damn it! that Jones is a sot —
Must ha’ been drunk as a sow,
Best blanky race o’ the lot;
Just have to fake it somehow.
Must ha’ been drunk as a sow. . . .
Tip that a submarine’s sunk ;
Just have to fake it somehow. . . .
All the blanked world’s gettin’ drunk!
Tip that a submarine’s sunk
Out at the Heads by a barque.
All the blanked world’s gettin’ drunk. . . .
P’liceman’s been stabbed in Hyde Park.
“Out at the Heads, by a barque’’—
Story does seem a bit slim. . . .
P’liceman been stabbed in Hyde Park. . . .
Blanky unpleasant for him!
Story does seem a bit slim. . . .
Ring up the hospital, Gibbs.
Blanky unpleasant for him,
Gettin’ a knife in his ribs!
Ring up the hospital, Gibbs ;
P’r’aps the poor blanker ain’t dead.
Gettin’ a knife in his ribs
Makes a chap long for his bed.
P’r’aps the poor blanker ain’t dead.
Got this dam’ damp in my bones. . . .
Makes a chap long for his bed.
Where in the devil is Jones?
MAORILAND.
FRANK MORTON.

WHAT IS CATARRH?
CATARRH is inflammation of the lining
membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not ar-
rested it invades the passages which lead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing,
destroys the sense of taste and smell, ren-
ders the breath offensive, breaks down
the affected- tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages, The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stomach, causes
dyspepsia, also consumption. Do you
want relief and cure? If so, try our
great remedy.

ALBATROSS.
It Being the Code of the Senhor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BAUKE, whom they nicknamed Tongue
o’, the Islands, wab the most skilful
torturer in all that Polynesian group.
And the affair of the Sefihor was his finest
effort, as well as his last, for the European
strain that had descended to him fropi his
unwilling grandmother gave him a nice idea
of the result of his action upon the Sefihor, The
Sefihor’s action upon him was a later story.
For the Portuguese blood - that had earned
the Sefihor his name had once been that of
great hidalgoes,. whose women - had been as
remote from breath of reproach as their
bowers were remote from every-day life. The
Portuguese had brought a little of this
aloofness into his life when he married,
though the girl of his choice understood little
of it.
She was the full-lipped daughter of an
Irish beach-comber, who had once been
something better. The Sefihor had noticed her
first because of the misery of her life, and
had liked her next 'because her dark beauty
reminded him of the women of his own land.
Afterwards he came to love her, and the rea-
son of love no man knows.
At-first she had thought it delightful to
have so romantic a husband, for he had seen
much of the world and of men in bis thirty-
odd years, while she, in her twenty, had
seen almost nothing beyond the isle where
her father dragged a scattered existence from
the sea.
But afterwards the romance of the new
loneliness, which she had exchanged, for the
old one, palled on her, for the Sefihor was a
jealous husband, and he built his bungalow
far out of the track, on the wildest coast-
line in all those miles of sharp-toothed reefs.
So Rauke had a chance to pay the grudge
he owed the Portuguese.
Time had been when the Sefihor, for the
sake of the white blood in Ranke’s veins,
had made something of a companion of him.
And Rauke, if truth were known, probably
carried more weight with his tribe than the
chief himself.
Now, in his new-found happiness, the
white man forgot Rauke, who nursed a bit-
ter resentment, as he went sulking inland to
his despised people. But the copra had to be
sold, and the Sefihor certainly gave the best
price, and the tribe would have murmured
even against Tongue o’ the Islands if he had
interfered with the legitimate course of
trade. As it was, he had a hard task in
winning the elders over to his master scheme
when at last he; evolved it.
The young bucks' thought it excellent fun
to steal to the qpast, and carry off the Sefi-
hor’s bride while her husband was away on
some thirty-hours’ trading.expedition. (On
anything over two days his wife accompanied
him.) They raked up a couple of canoes
and pulled to sea with their prisoner, leav-
ing very obvious tracks of their departure
behind them, and so swept half-way round
the island: There they quietly landed, and
cut inland. It was an excellent false trail
that they left behind them, and the search
would be among the adjacent isles. Rauke
pointed this out to the hysterical lady,
whose brain was still reeling from the sud-
denness of a night attack, killing of her
three or four native- servants, and her own
abduction.
Rauke assured her of his devotion, and
mentioned that it would grieve him inex-
pressibly to have to kill her, should her hus-
band by chance stumble on the place of her
concealment with a punitive expedition. She
had mentioned the probability of such a
thing as a last piece of hopeless bluff.
The quarter-caste seemed content for a
while after her capture. True, he made a
great point of giving her up to the chief,
who was a toothless dotard after all. But
he reserved the next right of succession, and
he somehow got two wives and three pigs and
a new grass hut out of the deal, so on the
whole he did well, and could afford an extra
pair of bone earrings. They decorated the
white woman, too, with bone earrings, and
necklaces, and feathers, and shell bangles
and anklets. Rauke’s white blood saved
her nose from ornamentation.
Then, one night, after some three months,
the chief’s favorite was missing, and the
toothless one bewailed the loss of his shells
and his white wife, and his youth, and many
other things. But he hadn’t the faintest
guess as to what had really happened. If
a knowledge of the desperately rash and fan-
tastic thing which Rauke had done had come
upon him, he would have wailed with great
bitterness, and then he would probably have
clamored to his young men to burn Rauke
alive, and after that he would have got drunk,
and, perhaps, died of fright. As it was, he
was only a good deal worried, but hoped
for the best. And he was still doing it a
few days later, when something happened to
liim. ,
Rauke’s revenge was on the Senlior, not
his wife. So he had sent Maluka, the dumb
boy, on an errand to the coast. Now, the
great good of Maluka was that he was
dumb, but not deaf. His tongue had been
extracted, or rather clipped off, by the tooth-
less chief in a fit of petulance over some
ill-cooked plantains. Therefore Maluka was
a useful second in the scheme which was
afoot.
For word had come inland that the Sefihor
was back from his tenth or one-hundredth
fruitless search for his lost wife, and was
living at his bungalow.
It was a moonless night when they
reached the coast, and the light in the bunga-
low burnt through the blackness and the
mist creeping up from the hollows. Away
inland there Rauke had set his distant stage
carefully, and the dumb man had orders to
wait till late at night before taking the
woman back.
A mile or more from the seaward side came
the lazy drone of the waters grinding on the
coral, and the swisli-swash of the just-
stirred pandanus thatch was regular as the
ticking of a clock.
Maluka signed to the Sefihor’s wife to wait
for him. He did it with a significant ges-
ture of upraised knife. Spirit-crushed and
obedient, she cowered almost in the shadow
of her own home till his return.
It seemed an interminable time, and she
fell to counting the flop-flop of the dried
leaves falling in various places. The reitera-
tion of every sound got on her nerves, till
at last she opened her mouth to scream.
Then, remembering Maluka, she refrained,
for Maluka was silent-footed. A half-arm’s
length away, in utter stillness, you could
not hear him breathe.
Once she reached up to pluck the foolish
ornaments from her neck. Then, afraid
that her gaoler might miss them, she
crouched miserably, and waited for the dumb
savage to give her leave to go to her hus-
band's arms. Ah! surely he would repay
her for all this misery.
The mist sequined her half-bare body over
with wonderful fairy effects, like the decora-
tions of a pantomime queen, and the cold
of near midnight gripped at her throat with
icy fingers. She fell to undoing one of the
two long plaits of hair that hung to her
waist. She had half re-plaited it again
when the dumb man’s touch on her shoulder
startled the ever-ready shriek from her. He
nipped -a tiny piece of flesh out with his fin-
ger-nails, very scientifically on the whole,
considering his lack of knowledge of anatomy,
and dragged her with him. He left her
before the house, and melted into the black
shadows.
There was a light on the verandah of the
far side of the bungalow. The Sefihor was
standing there, gazing seaward, and dream-
ing God knows what dream of his lost wife.
“Miguel!” The cracked little noise in
her throat sounded ridiculous to her. The
Sefihor turned sharply at the unbelievable
sound of his name, and stood sharply out-
lined in the patch of light outside the open
door. She came up with the long, easy
stride her .forced marches and loose, barbar-
ous clothing had taught her, and with some-
thing of a confident boldness, it seemed to
the half-dazed man.
“Miguel !” she said again. He staggered
back towards the door-jamb and stared at
her, unrealising and incredulous as he noted
the savage earrings and feathers and sharks’
teeth and the foolish shells about her. Truly
Tongue o’ the Islands had forgotten nothing
when he charged Maluka to give the woman
up in all her barbarous finery.
“I have come back —oh! aren’t you
glad?” she begged, lamely. Her Portu-

then you are for always my beautiful saint
in Heaven. That better than my dis-
honor’ wife on earth.”

and the Sefihor had had little use for prayers
in that full-lived life of his. He had al-
ways left such things to women.
“Miguel! Jesus!” she coupled their
names together in meaningless blasphemy, as
she turned to escape from that pointing
muzzle. But the Portuguese was the finest
shot in the islands, and to-night, of all
times, when he fired for the sake of his
foolish “honor,” he did. not miss.
After all, Eauke was robbed of much of his
revenge, in that he did not see its consum-
mation. But to the Sefihor his wife had
never before seemed so beautiful and worthy
of worship as when she lay as a martyred
saint at his feet.
LILLIAS GORDON.
The Preparations of Slavin.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ 1 EKE you, Ted, run the rake over
I I the ground there. I don’t like no
hay wasted. See, Bobbie, there’s
some the fowls has scratched out be the side
of the barn, an’ some in the bottom of the
waggon. An’ there’s some shed oats there, too
—good bit o’ horse-feed, that. See it ain’t
wasted, Hi, Molly, fling over that hay-
band- Confound yer, carn’t yer . reach the
tarpoliu! It ain’t, down enough on the
west side —the weather-quarter. There,
you, Maggie, shin off and tell mother to ’ave
some water hotted; I’m nearly ready to
’ave me clean-up. And I want tea be
8, so’s to git to Dwyer’s before dark. If you
kids had flung yourselves round, I’d ha’ been
off be now, hang yer! Tommy, you take
a handful o’ hay and a halter and ketch
Diver for me. Kick! He won’t kick yer.
Or if he do kick, kick ’im back.”
“Evenin’!”
Slavin paused long enough from twisting
a corner of the tarpaulin to nod to the
Stranger, who loafed, with a half-quizzical
smile, on his horse by the fence. And yet it
was hardly a pause, for Slavin improved the
otherwise wasted moment by dashing sundry
straws from his shaggy beard. Immediately
he was tearing away at some loose stuff at
the far end of the stack that to his eye
spoiled its symmetry. He had not listened
for the responsive “Good evening.” How-
ever, as it happened, the Stranger contented
himself with a nod anywhere in the direction
of the stack, being in a mood evidently to
observe rather than to talk. Slavin worked
rapidly round and round the stack, clawing
down the loose straw to arm height, or be-
low the tarpaulin that was thrown irregularly
over the stack. As he went he shouted injunc-
tions as to “clearin’ up,” and “takin' care
of the straw,” and other things, “I carn’t
go away with an easy mind, as long as
things ain’t straight and safe. I’m not that
kind. Casey, now, he takes his spell leavin’
things nohow.” Then there was more claw-
ing.
“Hi! is the fountain hot, and the tea
ready ?”
The Stranger looked sideways along the
fence to the figure that answered Slavin.
Slavin was already again half-way round
the back of the stack, and the Stranger got
the answer —“Yes, Dan; and your clo’es laid
ready.”
The watcher from the saddle beheld the
thinnest, brownest woman that ever God’s
Australian climate, and man’s hard work,
turned out. She had, like the woman of
Wordsworth’s ditty, “a baby at her back
and another at her breast.” Also one at
her side. Even a lover of the drab in life,
like the old poet, could have done little with
Mrs. Slavin in poetry. She was plain,
weary, and dirty, as well as thin and brown.
The lounger in the saddle wondered idly
what sustenance the shrimp of an infant
that had the front place on the mother’s
person might be getting.
“I beg your pardon.” He realised that
the brown slip of a woman had addressed
him. “Slavin’s a tidy man, a great worker.
He don’t leave no litter when he goes
Stop that,” she broke off, speaking to the
tiny one by her side.
“No, I suppose nor. Yes, a tidy man,”
His answer was vague—unsatisfactory, he
realised, as a reply to the woman’s terse
statement. But Slavin, reappearing round
the stack at the moment, looking like a
frowsy madman, all straw about the upper
part, and his lower limbs jerking about in
a pair of perilously-fastened pants, had
rather unnerved the rider.
The lady was for monologue apparently,
and did not heed his vagueness.
“Without the kids’ help he couldn’t of got
away this season. He r s that perticler, is
Slavin, to have everything redded up first.
Without the harvest safe he wouldn’t of
went at all.” (“Hold up!” to the urchin
at foot that dragged her skirt.) “It’s his
one treat. It would of been a shame if”
A roar from Slavin turned their eyes his
way. “Is Tommy got the horse yet?” he
demanded, still clawing the. stack, and spit-
ting the taste of mustard seed out of his
mouth.
“Yeh.”
Slavin, emerging presently, like the moon
from eclipse, from the opposite side of the
stack, poured out instructions meant for
wife and children indiscriminately. “Up at
4, no later. Dy’e hear? Milkin’ over be
7. See to that, mother. You, Bobbie, I’ll
skin yer when I come back if them weeds
ain’t cut down in the crick paddock. And
mind the calves in the bush selection is
watered and counted every day. Take the
taters to the barn that’s for seed, and see the
sprouts is off ’em. Mary is to go to school;
I want no more finin’. But she can help
with the milkin’ mornin' and evenin’. Hear
that, mother. Smith will help Tommy kill
the old ewe with the hurt back. Her mut-
ton will last you while I’m away. Salt
half. Smith’s cornin’ to-morrer; I promised
him the skin for his trouble. And he can
have the head and half the liver if he likes.
Hear that, mother? Keep the kids outer
the barn. They might git scratchin’ the
paint on the noo chaffcutter. And, Ted,
don’t you ferget”
All this time Slavin had been emerging,
disappearing, and re-emerging round the
stack. Mrs. Slavin’s head nodded all
through, and her tongue iterated: “All
right, Dan; I’ll see things is done.”
A great puff of satisfaction and relief.
“There, that’s snug. Not a prettier stack
in all the neighborhood.” Slavin eyed it
with pride.
His eye happened again on the Stranger,
who, feeling himself addressed, ventured:
“You’ve made great haste, but” —tentatively
—“I dpn’t see any change in the weather ap-
proaching !”
Slavin’s eyes were screwed up as one
who sees other things. He chewed between
speech a straw —the last straw.
“Weather ain’t the consideration,” he
vouchsafed. And, without intention of fur-
ther rudeness: “Wot d’yeh want?”
“Merely to enquire the way to Daev’s.”
Slavin spat the straw out —the final one
of a disposed-of harvest. He waved his
hand towards his wife, as if to say, “Ask
hex 1 .” Over his shoulder he added. “Sarah,
hurry up tea.”
The Stranger politely listened to the
brown woman’s directions to Pacy’s.
Then —“Better come an’ ’ave a cupper tea;
not much to offer,” he heard over her shoul-
der, His abstraction fled. Curiosity he-,
came triumphant,
“No, thank you, but—where’s your hus-
band going?”
“Where’s he goin’?” She pitied his ignor-
ance, “For his spree, o’ course. Always
after harvest he has it.”
She bustled off, and left him with a vision
of thin lips in a brown smile, and a feeling
that he had shown lamentable ignorance.
ALPENSTOCIK.
Blither.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HE sits in his seat on high, an old un-
killable god,
Though rheumy and dim his eye, and.
shaky his hand and rod;
For he is the god who drives the fools of you
hence and hither
Throughout your yammering lives —the bab-
blesome god of Blither.
Out of his eye drips rheum, and the edge of
his. mopth .is moist;
And high on a wobbling boom a dish-clout
wet they hoist;
Things slummicky, slimy, damp, are his em-
blem and incense too,
A fumy oil is his lamp, and his priests ai'e
a knock-kneed crew.
There crawls the Poet who wails of the
burbling Might-Have-Been;
And the Bard of the Perfumed Gales, or the
Softly Sylvan Scene ;
There sneaks the Critic who Couldn’t, but
blabs to the men who write, '
And likewise-the Woman who Wouldn’t, but
mumbles that yet she Might.
There yabbers the helpless Tory the fear of
his soul to shrive, ■ ■ 11
And shrieks at some new-born glory. “The
horrible thing’s alive!” .us: ,
’Tis grasping .he knows not whither, and
vexes his anguished head, . b
For they who are . vowed to Blither prefer
things barren and dead. '
He hath his hymn and his song, this god of
slaver and sludge, ' ,1
And if they be right or wrong—go read of
his book, and judge!
The Tory’s clamor of pain When Thought

is generally sane and trustworthy. It is only on really insignifi-

B»e Green Coupon System.
IT IS merely the application of the principle of co-operation to the former individual methods
of giving' discounts for cash. Traders for many years past have given discount to
encourag'e cash trading'—some in Cash, where the amount spent was larg'e enoug'h ; some
by means of tickets printed by the cash register means, and in other ways. Butchers, bn
presentation of a number of these tickets, would give a cash discount in meat, g'rocers in
groceries, and so on. Others, finding' that their customers did not want the discount in the
same class of goods as those sold by them, purchased small stocks of glassware, crockery, etc.,
to give as premiums.
All These Efforts Were Not Very Successful, owing' to the fact that it took a
consumer too long' to collect sufficient coupons, or tickets, at any one place wherewith to
obtain something' of value ; and, besides, the trader did not like handling' premium g'oods which
were not in his line. Therefore a number of traders of different kinds, such as a draper,
a g'rbcer, a butcher, and a bootseller, combined together and issued an interchangeable coupon,
and instead of redeeming' the coupons themselves, appointed an ag'ent to do that for them.
They each paid money to the agent in proportion to the number of coupons they issued
to their customers, and the ag'ent on his part undertook to buy g'oods with the money entrusted
to him by the traders, and to give full retail value to their customers, and, further, undertook
out of the difference between the price he paid the manufacturer and the retail price at which
the g'oods were redeemed by the public, he would establish show rooms, pay all expenses,
advertise the traders, etc., the sole expense to the trader being' the amount he was prepared
to give his customers by way of discount.
Now it will be admitted that the individual traders buying small stocks must
necessarily pay almost retail prices, but the agent acting for a large number of
traders and furnished with sufficient capital can buy largely enough to enable
him to purchase at bedrock prices from the manufacturer. Therefore, the ag'ent
creates the fund out of which working' expenses and profit are paid. The individual traders
are saved all the trouble and expense of keeping' stocks and redeeming' coupons, in addition
to which the advertising' done for them results in their gaining a large increase in cash
trade, and being' thus in possession of more cash they are themselves able to buy better
from the wholesale houses, and obtain larg'er discounts from them. The result is, the public
are encouraged to pay cash, and trade is done on a more solid foundation all round.
The Green Coupon Company fills the
place of Agent for all these various traders.
The public naturally appreciate a system which practically becomes for them a
compulsory saving's bank, for the coupons have to be accumulated until a certain number has
been obtained, when they represent something' substantial, whereas the equivalent discount in
cash on purchases of sixpence would be so trifling' individually that it would be frittered away.
Of course, the system must be honestly administered, and on this point the Company
courts any amount of enquiry and investigation, and if anyone is sceptical as to the value
and quality of the g'oods given in redemption of coupons, he or she is invited to visit the
Company’s Showrooms at y
